The Red Page
The R.A.A.
Isn’t it about time that Australia had he?
Academy of Letters?
The Australian language is in the making;
Australian literature is big enough to suffer
from growing pains; and there are many
matters of importance to Australian authors
that require some sort -of authoritative con-
trol and guidance. But Australian authors
are a body of individualists, subdivided in
suburbs, each hardly cogniscent of the exist-
ence of the other bloke, making his own
experiments and hoeing his own row with-
out help from his fellows. There exists a
little society in Melbourne which promised
once to be of practical value to authors in
building up Australian literature on the
firm footing of an adequately protected
national industry; but even in a time of
crisis like the present Federal election no
sign of leadership has come from that
earnest group of literary trades-unionists.
What is obviously heeded for Australian
Literature is some body elected on a wide
franchise that will act as the Trades Hall,
plus the University, for Australasia. Hence
this page suggests the immediate formation
of the It.A.A.—the Republican Australasian
Academy.
This body, it is suggested, should consist
of the leading literary figures in the Com-
monwealth and M.L., of practitioners rather
than of professors of Australasian Literature.
It should be the final authority on all ques-
tions of the Australasian language, such as
a standardised method of Australasian spell-
ing, the use of Australian, and imported,
slang, the compilation of an Australasian
dictionary, the decision of such questions
as the split infinitive, the eternal “dorn-
mawn” rime, the proper spelling of Abo-
riginal names, the question of the duplication
of post-office addresses in Australasia, and
a thousand apparently small points that will
continue to arise in Australasian literature.
Also it should have sufficient authority as
a last court of appeal in any literary ques-
tion ; and some of its most important func-
tions would be the control of all pensions
of the dependents of literary men, of pecu-
niary or other help to literary beginners, of
advice to embryo novelists and playwrights,
of defence for Australian writers in all
copyright disputes, and of the awarding of
a money or honorary prize to the best work
in prose and verse, produced each year in
Australia.
The beginnings of the R.A.A. would neces-
sarily be modest. The first members of such
a body must be chosen on a wide, but purely
literary, franchise; and for some years, no
doubt, the functions of the R.A.A. woulc
be merely consultive and advisory. Bui:
if the R.A.A. foundation members were
wisely chosen, representing the best that is
in Australasian literature, the R.A.A. woulc
soon become a necessary part of our informal
literary constitution and would attract to
itself a support that would enable it to
ask for a yearly grant of money from the
State. In one State the State Government;
subsidises music—with results that are
already satisfactorily apparent. It must
be the aim of the R.A.A. to obtain, either
from individuals or from the State, a sub-
sidy for its useful and necessary activities.
J*
The French Academy was founded by
letters patent, for the purification of the
French language.. Its original members were
only eight; its permanent total is 40. Among
other useful works it compiled a dictionary;
and though opinions differ strikingly about
its present and its historical value, the balance
seems to lie in its favor, for the marvellous
lucidity of the modern French language
may surely be put to the Academy’s credit.
The French Academy is an academical
body; the Republican Australasian Academy
would be more than that: it would have to
take a practical interest in all matters that
affect Australasian authors and their work;
it would have to be sufficiently important
to impress even politicians.
How is such a body to be elected? This
page suggests that the writers of Australasia
should select their own Academy.
At first the R.A.A. would be composed of
academicians living far from each other,
an Academy without an abiding place. How-
ever. let us take courage from the first
Academy in the world. The word is derived
from the olive grove of Academe, a pleasure-
garden or gymnasium in an Athenian suburb.
Surely there are plenty of olive groves in
Australia, and a surplusage of gymnasia
and pleasure grounds? Any of these would
do for the annual meeting of the R.A.A.
It must be remembered, too, that the
Academician would oocupy a position of
pride and honor in Australasia. The signa-
ture, R.A.A. —Republican Academician of
Australasia —would be worth more than any
title conferred by a university, or even than
the Freedom of Whitechapel or Woolloo-
mooloo, conferred by mere municipal bodies.
Doubtless the Academician might be privi-
leged to wear a button, though there would
always be the painful possibility of him
being mistaken for a Win-the-War-Leaguer.
Of course, this being a country of adult
suffrage, there would be no sex disability for
membership.
This page suggests that the authors of
Australasia should select by ballot six foun-
dation members. The elected academicians
would then select by ballot ten names of
the Australian dead—who would not be
entitled to vote, to confer upon the R.A.A.
a historical and representative quality. And
when an academician died, or rimed dawn
with morn , or became Prime Minister, or
did—or didn’t —split a scissile infinitive, or
made a pun, or was three times convicted of
bigamy, or accepted a K.C.M.G.-ship, the
remainder of august colleagues would select
an academician to fill his place. And the
academician elect would have to pronounce
the eulogy of his predecessor. (And this
page and, it is hoped, many other pages,
would publish it.)
The suggestion made by this page, which
hereby elects itself perpetual secretary to
the R.A.A., is as follows:
The qualification for voting will be the
publication in The Bulletin of a piece of
verse or prose, other than a mere par., by
the voter. Such voter must mention his
credentials with his vote. Each voter may
name six, or fewer, persons for election to
the R.A.A., and may also briefly put the
case for any candidate to the readers of this
page. (Any person on the staff of this
paper is, of course, barred from being a can-
didate.) From the votes so received, this
page will select the six candidates who are
highest in the poll, and who therefore become
the foundation members of the R.A.A.
And the voter who places most nearly in
order most of the six elected academicians
will receive a guinea for his prescience. The
poll closes on June 23. Ballot-papers, which
must be addressed to this page, must have
their selections arranged in order of the
voter’s preference.
Free Verse
Some 70 entries were received for the
vers lilre competition, on some aspect of
the war. The majority chose War itself—
not some aspect of it. The new form is
evidently not so easy as it looks. The
majority of the competitors have not
grasped the fact that Free Verse, despite its
liberation from the strict metre of verse and
the useful bridle of rime, has a rhythm of its
own and demands almost the close-knitted
texture of the short story or the sonnet.
Thought and emotion and imagination are as
necessary in Free Verse as they are in any
metrical composition. Many of the vers
librists merely cut their prose into arbitrary
lengths and omitted all punctuation marks.
P. Stewart, C. W. H. Bingham, Dora
Scott, P.W., H. V. Edwards, Roy R. Rede,
“Sedan,” Edward Perugini, P.N.R., “Cap-
stan,” N.L.WI, L. J. Villiers, Edward Searle,
M.M., Boyce Boyden (2), Edgar L. Stevens
and H. W. Stewart make commendable
attempts at what is too obviously an un-
accustomed task. R. C. Henderson masters
the form, but dissolves into vagueness.
“Breton’s” “Machine Guns” is more ambi-
tious, but he alternates patches of barren
ground with outcrops of adjectives. Gerald
Shaw merely writes prose as will be seen
when it is printed as the thing it is:—
THE WAR STEED.
Brown Is a buyer from Billigo. As Brown can;" 1
cantering carelessly through the slip-panels that
seldom or never were up, my mates who were grazing
with me, in the paspalum paddock, whinnying
rushed to the furthermost fence ; for he’d been there
before, and they knew that when Brown saw a
likeable hack he’d buy it, or, failing, would camp in
the kitchen till he’d eaten its hide. As for me, I
stayed where I was, and gave him a grin of derision;
for I knew my defects ; I had heard them narrated—
hip-down and- spavin’d and gone in the knees —I was
safe —so I thought; but one never knows; and the
war has its lessons for all.
An hour or so passed... .Coming out of a dream
I heard my master saying to Mr. Brown: “If you
can pass him for twenty-five, you’re on a tenner, for
he’s worth no more than four”. ...And I went
through the slip-rails with Brown.
As 1 stood in the sale-ring, dull-eyed and droop-
ing, I thought I’d be soon passed in; but a big-
bellied, boosv-faced, bumptious old fogey, who was
standing with Brown, seemed to think me a prize;
and I was conscripted for cavalry work ; and Brown
got his tenner; and some poor unfortunate devil
will trust to my strength and my speed and endur-
ance, and he’ll go to Hell, and I'll feed old Abdul;
but my master and Brown and the boosey-faced
buyer, and the people who sent him, will talk of the
Anzaes, and sav: “Ain’t we doing it fine? Them
Turks’U be sorry they stirred up Australia.” And
they’ll buy some more Treasury-bonds.
Mary E. Marvin writes ’-■rose. embodying
a delicate pathos. O. N. Gillespie’s original
fable @f the frogs in a Somme lagoon unfor-
tunately ends in a swamp of vagueness.
C. H. Souter utilises the form neatly on a
subject too slight for publication. Bill
Bndd’s excellent effort needs the blue pencil.
Ella _S. Morrison’s fine impression is too
reminiscent of a vers libre already printed
on this page. And “Jane” supplies us with
comedy, spoiled by a line we have omitted :
A tariff upon imported books, as an aid
to Australian literature, was a year ago a
mere pious hope ; but suddenly it seems to be
coming true. This tariff will not be im-
posed by Australia: it is being sternly im-
posed by the war. Already the scarcity and
the high price of freight are operating as a
tariff wall against overseas literature. The
Australian importer cannot pass on to his
customers the whole of the increased price
he must pay for freight; for there is a limit
of price to certain classes of books, and
once that price is exceeded those books cease
to be necessities for the customers. There is
therefore noticeable a tendency on the part
of Australian booksellers to publish Aus-
tralian books, thus saving excessive freight
and certain delay, and the difficulty of regu-
lar shipments. This page has heard already
of one book written by an Australian which
was actually accepted by a London pub-
lisher, but which the Australian agent has
decided to print in Australia owing to the
delays and drawbacks incidental to London
publishing.
J. H. Maiden continues his vast Critical
Revision of the Genus Eucalyptus, the
latest parts being the 9th and 10th of the
3rd volume.
Science Progress, a quarterly review
(Murray, per G. Robertson, 55.), continues
to provide highly-nutritive but rather tough
provender for the scientific reader. The
January issue contains portraits and biogra-
phies of the two Australian winners of the
Nobel Physics Prize for 1915, Prof. W. H.
Bragg, of Adelaide, and his son, W. L.
Bragg, now on duty in France.
HOW A FLAPPER SEES THINGS.
I have not done .so badly out of this War.
Jack left me a separation allowance, and perhaps
we will be married when he comes home;
And Bill lent me his car before he left.
Poor Bill has been missing a year—
I do not expect he will ever want it back—
So I try to do my bit, taking wounded officers out
for drives,
Which makes up a little for all the dances that never
happen now.
My V.A.D. uniform was not very becoming,
So I took up spinning—we should all try to help
Win the War.
I have some awfully sweet souvenirs: a powder-bowl,
made out of a shell, ,
And Ted sent me a fez he took fiom a dead Turk,
But it had horrid blood-stains on it ....
He£_ lips are stained red as the blood of men.
From eight offerings the winter has to be
chosen. Boyce Bowden’s “Bandolier” is
clever; and he has made his brief lines sound
like rapid firing; but there is more in this
form than metrical virtuosity. Helen Jerome
proffers us excellent melodrama on a theme
unhappily too familiar, an echo of the Bel-
gian atrocities. O. N. Gillespie and “Up
Stage” present clean-cut impressions, lack-
ing, however, the poetry of the winning
entry. And here selection becomes so diffi-
cult that this page would almost like to
bracket the four equal. There is more
poetry and imagination in Bartlett Adam-
son’s splendid poem than in any of the
others; but iu these there is a humau quality
and a pathos that he deliberately disdains.
As it is one of the conditions of our competi-
tions that the name and address of the
winner must be published, as a guarantee to
other competitors, and “Spinster,” for obvi-
ous reasons declines to afford us that per-
mission, this page seizes this technical point
to limit the choice to one of the other three.
“Black Bonnet’s” poem hardly measures up
with that of Jean Curlewis; and, solely on
the ground that the latter’s work has a more
intimate appeal than Bartlett- Adamson’s
lofty poem, the prize is awarded to Jean
Curlewis, “Avenel,” Mosman, N.S.W., the
young daughter of the well-known Australian
writer of kiddie stories, Ethel Turner. But
the other poems mentioned in this para-
graph will appear and be paid for. First,
here is Bartlett Adamson's:—
ARMAORDDON.
Striding from star to star
Two gods went splendidly past
Across the river-shallows of the wide-flowing
slcy;
And glancing to choose the starry stepping-
stones,
They saw the world.
They saw:
The German storm advancing like ponderous
periods of jargon:
A crash of hieroglyph: an inky mass:
Over the daintily-painted page of Belgium.
They saw:
,France, like a light-foot rhythmic matador,
Leap to the ready, poise alert, and flash
A sword of light that drove the headlong
storm
Staggering backward.
They saw:
The Britannic millions: a confused crowd:
Trying to march in a line.
An awkward-squad, more or less of heroes,
Eager and disorderly as vers libre is,
Trying desperately to m,arch in a line;
Trying, and trying, and tardily
Learning the measured might of discipline,
Until their brown battalions thudded by,
Sivinginy in line.
They saw:
Russia: a slow voluminous music visible,
Rising and falling, billowing and receding,
Ebbing and flowing, flowing,
To what predestined end!
To sink maybe to caverns of woe;
Or swell and thunder to some clear ocean-
tone of exultation.
They saw:
These and others: a circle of nations
Rise and kindle a ring of sacred fire:
A prisoning wall of flame around the roaring
Black mass of storm.
They sate:
A leaden mist of mingled battle-smolce and
praying-breath and hate and woe
Creeping,
Creeping over the world and covering it;
And seething, curling into fantastic roots,
rising in rugged trunks, branching, and
forming
A forest: a grey moss on a boulder:
A monstrous forest of fog, among whose
roots
Men lived and wrought and fought and died,
Or crawled with tattered flesh and tattered
wits;
And women loved, and wept behind their
eyes;
And romping children shouted and ran, play-
ing at war.
They saw :
A luminous vapor rising slowly, sloivly,
From out the monstrous forest of fog, and
hovering
Lightly above it:
A breath: a shimmer: a haze of summer-
heat :
The visible and volatile
Souls of the multitudinous dead.
They saw:
In one keen casual ylance,
These trivial incidents of everyday:
This turmoil-tinted boulder in a stream:
This ripple of some far cyclone-passion of
creation.
And without pause,
Across the river-shallows of the wide-flowing
sky,
They passed:
Striding from star to star.
BARTLESTT ADAMSON.
And here is the winning poem :
SUBURBAN
It is eight o'clock at night,
The quiet stars are looking down into a
street of quiet suburban houses,
Behind most of the drawn blinds there is a
light shining,
It is all very quiet —
But behind every one of those windows
They know about the War.
This is a very patient people God has made:
All the day long they go about their busi-
ness,
And all the time they are being tortured
worse than ever God’s Son was tortured
on the Gross.
The mothers go about their daily busi-
ness. ~.
You ivould not think when you see them at
breakfast that they have been lying
awake since the small hours, watching
the light creep in among the shadowy
furniture,
Wondering
If he was killed while they were snatching
a feio hours sleep —
If he suffered — s
The fathers go about their daily business —
You would not think, perhaps, that they cared
very much until you meet them on the
tram and straightaway they show you
their sons' letters.
The daughters go about their daily busi-
ness —
Only the sons do not go about their busi-
ness:
They are not there.
The churches say that the God behind those
stars is all-powerful,
All-powerful-—and yet He does not stretch
out His Hand to end War!
But if God were like that I tell you that the
people would stream out of their houses
into the open and shriek and shriek
until He would have to flee away into
the uttermost recess of His heavens
So that He could not hear them.
Or, goaded into an agony of strength, they
ivould tear down the very battlements of
Heave” and seise Him where He sits in
His security thinking to judge the
people on Judgment Day,
And Judgment Day would be the day when
the people would judge God.
But instead there is the quiet radiance behind
blinds.
So I know that that God is not God at all.
The real God walks with them daily, help-
ing them —a spirit
All made up of the glory and goodness in the
hearts of the boys who were slain,
And of the tenderness in the hearts of those
that loved them.
It is after eight o'clock at night.
Clouds have blown up and blotted out the
stars,
In all the houses the War crouches menacing
and grim,
It is all very terrible....
But from every one of those windows
There is a light shining.
JEAN CURLEWIS.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Dreamer.
Of my farm on the Connaught hills I do be
dreaming.
Neat and decent, as such things should be;
Cows and pigs, and a wheat-field gleaming—
Two acres of that, and of bog-land three.
The strong man I was when I drained the
bog-land,
Wide of the shoulder, and thick of the
thigh ;
Great weights could I lift with the swing
of my one hand,
Small leaves on the hillside grew clear to
my eye.
And Mary, herself (och, cream was her fine
throat,
Her cheek smooth as cherry skin) kept
the house neat
As the ankles that peeped from the swing
of her petticoat—
Och, but the evening’s meat tasted too
sweet!
’Tis long dreams I dream of the fairs and
the games of strength,
Of the fairings we bought, and the dance on
the green;
My foot was the lightest, ’tis strange a
man grows at length
Glad, when he moves, on his blackthorn
to lean.
Wide does the land stretch, a field with no
hedge at all,
Never the one man reap it his lone;
’Tis a land for the young, for in old age
the heart grows small
And longs for the close hedge to circle his
own.
’Tis lonely I sit with my old §tick between
my knees
(From the dear friends and Connaught I
came far away,
Braving the storms of the wide seas, the
grey seas),
With my pipe and my dreams at the close
of the day.
N.S.W.
JOHN EGAN.
Vignette.
Tree upon the scarred spur. .
Black mountain myrtle,
Standing grim and silent like a monk cloaked
and cowled ;
Young pine for pilgrim staff,
Ilimony for girdle,
Rosary and crucifix
All of white foam-berries
Strung by Montezuma while the pole-storms
howled.
Myrtle on the cleft spur!
When the deep thunder
Rolled among the mountain’s ribs as through
a skeleton,
Deep and deep your feet they set
In the glacier furrow;
Rain and wind and cloud-kiss
Spoke with you and loved you ;
Strange strong things caressed you while
the wild stars shone. -
Waratah with flame feet
Through the sunny weather,
Twinkling like a leprachaun of world-old
tale,
To the chime of Christmas bells
March the Gordon lilies,
Blowing golden trumpets.
O dauntless little people
That dared the level lances of the' West Coast
hail!
Years o’ days, years o’ days!
Sun-calm and storm-time!
Gold and red of Autumn leaves —so I turn
and dream ;
Dream again of Hamilton,
Myrtle-crowned and wistful....
Wizard priests intoning
In a temple olden
And across the silences the wild eyne’s
scream.
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
Moon-Mirth.
Dip me a gallon of golden mirth
Out of your well, y maide. moon,
Bubbling, doubling over the earth—
Dimpled joy of a starry noon.
Ladle me laughing bowls of light
Giddily glad from pools of bliss,
Winking, blinking beads of delight—*
Weal and wine of a woman’s kiss.
Dear little moon, when the world goes wrong
I and my love look up to you,
Flowering, showering laughter and song
Over the land the long night through.
Oh, for a glass of your gaiety.
Fair little moon, to spill o’er men,
Pearling, whirling all of them free.
Making them lovers like us again.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
Dawdling Song.
In a wee
Golden boat
(Do they see
You and me?).
We’re afloat
On a stream
Smooth as glass,
Green as grass,
Sweet as dream.
Naiads fair
Gaze at us
Loitering there;
Tawny hair
Odorous,
Mouths aswoon,
Eyes ag10w....
Bless the slow
Afternoon!
Lilies yearn
As we look,
Lotus burn
Just astern.
Every nook
On the strand
Holds a gleam...
Happy dream!
Happy Land!
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.

Why Regular (or Under-sized)
Metric Tyres are Generally
Over-loaded!
AIR-PRESSURE, when based upon
size-of-Tyre, can be a very mis-
leading measure.
This when the Load is not carefully
considered, or varies widely on different
occasions.
The Tyres of a Seven-Passenger Car,
if carrying usually only two people at
correct Pressure for Seven , might prove
much over-inflated for the Comfort of
only two people—the then surplus pres-
sure resulting in excessive Vibration and
Jar.
That would be unsatisfactory, of
course!
But, a Two-Passenger Car, if forced to
carry Seven Passengers, would be much
worse.
Because, the excessive Load would then
flatten the Tyres away beyond the proper
14%-of-contact-with-the-earth —wear their
Fabric Walls out prematurely—and burn
them out through the excessive Heat of
Friction generated between the layers of
flattened portion of Fabric.
Excessive Load therefore interprets into
practically the same results as would the
use of UNDER-SIZED Tyres.
SUCH excessive Load, on Under-Sized
Tyres, is usually, and necessarily,
compensated for by the use of an
exceedingly high Air-Pressure in the
Tubes.
By such means only can the flattened
portion of Tyre be lifted off the ground,
up to the proper 14%-of-Tyre-Diameter
contact.
But;, when so lifted, the necessarily
high Air-Pressure not only tends to strain
the ,Fabric of the Tyre, through danger-
ously increased tension, but also causes
excessive Vibration, through over-infla-
tion.
This would, in turn, promptly show it-
self in discomfort to Car-Occupants—in
deterioration of Car-Mechanism, and in
shortened Mileage of Tyres.
That explanation, on the use of Under-
Sized Tyres, should now interest every
Car-Owner
Because, Car-Owners can prove, for
themselves, that ad millimeter “fabric’
Tyres made in Europe are UNDER-
SIZED (not Goodrich t wpO.
THE Calipers—or any other depend-
able Measure —will prove that the
so-called “815 x 105 m/m” Tyres —
for instance —actually measure only about
815 x 96—815 x 97—815 x 98—815 x 99
—SIS x 101, according to the make, when
purchased new. Other sizes in propor-
tion.
This condition freely interprets into the
necessity for either buying high-priced
“ Over-Sizes ” of metric Tyres, to carry
the Load properly, at the proper Air-
Pressure —
Or
Buying FULL-SIZED GOODRICH
Tyres, which measure up (even when
new) to the exact dimensions their “Size”
specifications call for.
These, at no higher cost to Consumers
than same quality of “regular” (or
UNDER-SIZED) Tyres in all other
Metric Makes.
Now, which will you have, Reader? —
The FULL-SIZED Goodrich Tyres, or
Under-Sized Tyres of other Metric Makes
—at same price to you for same quality !
GOODRICH
FULL-
SIZED

303 Adelaide Street, Brisbane

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal. Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 8000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,

which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions -under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

AUSTRALIA LOSES AN ADVANCED
TRENCH.
The defeat of the Australian party at the
polls and the triumph of the Imperialistic
outcry are convincing facts. With an excellent
platform of reforms, the party behind Tudor
has received a practical knock-out, and the
Phewsion, frankly opposed to doing anything
for this Commonwealth, has been returned
to power. On the theory that the job of
legislators was to win-the-war and that a
man selected to make laws could best earn his
screw by making none, the Cook-Hughes t
combine boldly appealed to the country and
got a vote of confidence. What they can do
towards winning the war more than their
opponents would have done is not evident;
nor is it clear how a programme of progres-
sive legislation would have retarded final
victory. These things are great mysteries,
the key of which is held only by the secret
party conclaves who sit to discuss the fate
of empire; but the electors of the Common-
wealth have swallowed the amazing guff
handed out to them; and the Tory and
foreign-trader smile broadly at their reprieve.
Of course, the dominant interest of the war
itself had much to do with the result. The
unfortunate and illogical opposition of the
Labor party to the principle of conscription
had suggested to many people that Labor
was not whole-hearted in its desire to smash
the Germans; and as its present leaders
failed to put their case with force and
clarity, the person with the ballot-pencil
took their partial silence for a confession
that they were all the Imperialists declared
them to be. As at the New South Wales
State elections, much use was also made of
an alleged connection of the P.L.L. with
the 1.W.W.; and, although nobody with even
a slender knowledge of the actual political
position believed this, the statement was
accepted at face value by enough people to
influence the total vote. That Hughes had
himself, up to the moment of his involuntary
expulsion from Labor, heen in exactly
the same position in regard to the I.W.W.
as his recent colleagues who now opposed
him was forgotten or ignored. Toryism,
supported by a section of outcast Laborites,
hacked the win-the-war clamor by anything
that came handy; and as it had no policy
to expound it had plenty of time for insinua-
tion.
But the decision of the electors of the
Commonwealth, whatever the causes that led
up to it, hits sent, the Australian party back
to relace its boots and rearrange its punch,
while the Imperialists continue in office and
devote their whole strength to a considera-
tion of somebody else’s efforts to win the
war. Labor has plenty of. faults. Its trust
in party machinery and reliance on organisa-
tion rather than on leadership and propa-
ganda were bound sooner or later to produce
a reaction ; and its insistence on blind fidelity
to a platform rather than broad trust in the
people as a whole was a violation of the
foundation principles of democracy. Admit-
ting all these things and seeking in no
way to palliate or excuse them, the Labor
movement does stand and must still stand
for Australia. Its platform has always
been better than its achievement; and the
great problem now' before the party is the
best mode of strengthening its fighting
force. To get and to hold office is not
the purpose of the : big and honest move-
ment which so lately seemed to have the ball
at its feet. To have the mere name of Labor
triumphant and a few of its representatives
drawing the cash of office is nothing. In the
State of New South Wales we have seen
Labor in power for a period of years and
the work of putting the main planks of the
party’s platform into effective legislation
still practically untouched. In the Common-
wealth sphere we have seen allegedly Labor
men acting in their administrative capacity
with an utter disregard for the spirit of the
democracy which they professed to serve.
In both State and Federal activities the
tendency to put party above principle and to
sacrifice great deeds for the sake of small
victories has been very marked. Australia
is not tired of Labor thought or Labor
dos’Te, but Australia is pretty sick of the
failure of men to do the job they were sent
to do. There are any number of men
attached to the skirts of Labor whose only
idea is to get an extra shilling or two a day:
but the great’ bulk of those who have given
the movement their honest and unselfish sup-
port have bigger aims and seek only the
general good. It is these men who consti-
tute the verv heart and soul of Aus-
tralianism, and whose splendid efforts have
hitherto been dwarfed by the unworthiness
of a party machine which could coerce but
could not inspire or control.
* * * *
To defeat tlie llughes-Cook combine in
Parliament need be no part of the work of
the minority in the House of Representatives
and the Senate. That unholy combine in
restraint of Australia will defeat itself
thoroughly and convincingly if left to its
own devices. The Imperialistic yell which
is its only stock-in-trade wjll soon wear thin ;
and the alleged win-the-war policy, which is
really a policy of hide-behind-the-censor-apd-
do-nothing, will be known soon enough by
the people for the sorry jest it is. Hughes
can be counted upon for a continuance of
his noisy masquerade as the saviour of
empire; Cook can be absolutely relied on
for unimaginative futility; and Pearce will
never slack from his tame-cat job in an
atmosphere of mystery ard amusing so'crecy.
With forces like these fighting for them in
the political arena the Australians can afford
to leave the Imperialists to their victory and
devote themselves to the job of reconstruc-
tion. Primary government by means of the
Referendum and Initiative, as outlined in
Tudor’s recent speeches, must be brought to
the front and got into working order. It
was a plank in the Labor platform which
held an honored position when Labor looked
as if it could never climb to power; and
now that Labor is im against’its. job, its
real importance returns. The lesson is an
obvious one and one which cannot be evaded.
[To express the views of The Bulletin, by
S. H. Price, F. J. Kirby and D. M. Weight. 214
George Street, Sydney.]
LITANY OF THE MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We have sweated and, sought and failed and
fought for the better of three red years.
Some of us damned —we shall damn again—
the deeds of our braver peers.
There is never a skirmish we have not seen
but we judged it from toe to head,
Never a slur that we might have burked, but
we cast it at quick and dead.
The ravelled skein of our past lay plain, to
spoil or to clear and use;
And we knotted and mauled till the loose
was tight, and the erstwhile tight was
loose.
We have sparred with shadows, and sat and
screamed in the dust like an angry
child,
We have paused to breathe while the foe-
man sat on the farther shore and
smiled!
Lobd, we have called and crawled for men,
and the men who were ours to take
Wondered and hungered, were maimed, and
begged—for the land’s benignant sake!
It has come to pass that the great things
wax, and the little things recede;
And the ear that was glad with a deedless
phrase is sick for a phraseless deed.
Fill for us, Father, the need of men since
warring and men began—
Not votes or vaporings, gold or Spoil,
Not men—our need is a Man!
Thou knowest we’ve done what men may do
with the little we’ve had to hand;
And the strawless bricks that we made were
fair —though scarcely the kind we’d
planned!
Seen by the light of the wit we owned was
our statesmanship complete;
For he who knows not the living God may
bow at ju-ju’s feet!
A reed that should bend, but never break,
our £mall souls satisfied;
But a reed that is lissome to every wind is
Hell!- —we have leaned and tried!
Were a racehorse bred from a butcher’s
hack, we would win at an easy walk;
If a gun were foal to a Senate speech, we
had conquered the w mid with talk.
Yet there’s never a doubt but our way is
right—as the proofs have testified —
For the race we’ve won (in our minds) is
run, and the runners are home and
dried! „ T
Yet such is our make —-we are finite, Lord,
not infinite as Thou—-
That a lingering,, doubt of a limit reached
remains to us even now.
So, only to buttress our lesser strength, to
widen our mental span.
Send us, to prove and to perfect us.
Not men, O Lord —but a Man!
We have fought and scrambled for place and
pence, while the ships gaped void at the
docks:
We have tinkered with measure and party-
clause, edict and ballot-box.
We have painted and prinked our foible out
with the colors our care had stored ;
And backed for a little to view our work—
and it wasn’t a picture, Lord !
Money we held, and we paid it out in ways
that were slack and strange;
For he is a fool who abides by rule and a
slave who cavils at change!
The things we sought that we did not find,
the places we did not look,
They were never seen on a picture-screen,
they were never writ in a book !
We chased our tails as a kitten would —and
the chasing made us mad —-
But the men who led us called it. right—
Lord, they were all we had!
We have taken a brand-new canvas out, we
have flourished the outlines in ;
We are making a start for the hundredth
time. Lord, show us how to begin!
We cherish a dream of a color-scheme, the
lilting of lines that scan,
We are weary of lung and of unslacked
tongue—
O Master, give us a Man!
“A child may lead” —but the written screed
bv these shall stand defiled,
For they be men with bodies of men, and
the wit of a little child.
They turn the key of the treasure-chest, they
tilt the bag unbound;
They pour the gold through a seam-split
purse in waste on the sterile ground.
Oh, never yet did a spender grudge, or leave
his lust to lack
While a stranger lent what he pouched or
spent, and a stranger paid it back.
The forest and farm are ripe for toil, the
land is our Treasury ;
Yet the tongue is lighter than axe or spade,
and the coffer is Made—let be!
What do they reck of a bed of thorns whose
shoulders on down recline?
Should he lift his nose from the tilting pot,
whose brother pays for the wine—
Who, having drunken, wipes - his mouth and
settles with good advice
His score from the chimney, stirring not—
and calls it meet at the price?
Handle us, sift us, con us through, coterie,
creed and clan—
Lord, there is nothing we want so much —•
Not quite so much —as a Man!
We have taught ourselves to examine our-
selves ; and our thoughts are thorns that
prick.
We have added them on to Your thoughts of
us and the total has made us sick !
For never a boy with a paper cap, who
played with a wooden sword,
Was half so sorry a brigadier as some of
Your servants, Lord!
We have drawn a line that we thought was
straight, and finished where we’d begun;
We have settled our bill with a thousand
cheques where it might have been paid
with one.
We have walked in circles more than once
(we have talked in circles, too!) ;
And the net result is a concrete pain in a
spot that is known to You.
We have gulped our quaff with alacrity,
we’ve worshipped the World and Flesh;
And the retrospect of a thing is awe! Lord,
we would start, afresh!
The things wc lack that we ought to have,
the things that we’d like to drop—
Show us the road to the goal of both —-where
to begin and stop!
Give us the brain that shall weave our pain
to the web that our dreamings plan-
Not fame or treasure or ships or spoil—
A man, Lord, send us a Man!
VICTORIA.
J. Alex. Allan.
THE AUSTRALIAN SENATE AND A
THROTTLED DEMOCRACY.
An address to the congregation:—
And now,' brethren, having talked for
many years about our great democratic
institutions and government of the people by
the people, and having fluted at great length
about juntas and the responsibility of Par-
liament to the nation, and the representa-
tion of parties and opinions in the sovereign
tribunal of the Commonwealth, let us con-
sider the Australian Senate. Particularly let
the elector who favored the party known as
Labor, always blind to the precarious nature
of its place in Australian politics, consider
this Senate. And let not the elector who is
by way of being a Liberal or a- Phewsionist,
or he who is neither of these, things, but
hearkens unto the loudest yell, close his ears
nor cover up his eyes. Labor has just been
flung out of . the Senate with a loud bang,
even as Liberalism was thrown out. in years
gone by. But it would ill become Liberal-
ism to be puffed up with pride at this cir-
cumstance, for the day may come when it
too will be flung off the premises—when it
will go before the electors confident as
Labor was a week or two ago of saving its
bacon, and awake to find itself in the mud.
Brethren, the Australian Senate is an acci-
dent. Its every aspect is a parody on repre-
sentative government!. To the Australian
people it is as dangerous as dynamite-—and
in all the experimentation made with parlia-
mentary government it is the people who
must count, not parties and not politicians. A
House of Parliament in which large masses
of the people are denied representation is a
mockery of popular government. Even
though it may in theory be based upon the
popular vote, it nlay in practice be found to
so distort this vote as to become even more
objectionable than houses of parliament rest-
ing upon a narrow franchise or even upon
nomination or the accidents of birth.
How does this Senate of ours respond to
the test of being democratic —or even of
being representative of Australian political
opinion? Go back a year or two. At the
election of 1910, for 18 Senators 2,106,521
votes were recorded in favor of Labor candi-
dates, 1,973,789 for Liberals. This Repre-
sents 51 per cent, of the electors supporting
one set of principles or policies, or. some-
thing, and 49 per cent, in opposition. The
result of the election was that 51 per cent,
of the people returned 100 per cent, of
Senators. Labor got IS seats, the other push
none —and Labor sang hymns of praise over
the joke and the Liberals wept and gnashed
their teeth. There was another election of
18 Senators in 1913. Labor polled 50.05 per
cent, of the votes, and these put 11 (or 61
per c-eut.) Senators where they wanted to
get. The remaining 49.95 per cent, of vote-
could only shove seven (or 39 per cent.)
Senators into Parliament. Then followed
the double dissolution of 1914, when Aus-
tralia had to elect a full Senate of 36 mem-
bers. For the Labor candidates 6,234,878
votes (or 53.13 per cent, of the whole) were
recorded. These were enough to annex 31
for 86 per cent.) of the seats. For liberal
candidates 5,499,237 votes could only secure
five (or 14 per cent.) of the seats. And
again Labor, with a majority of 26 Senators,
joyfully disported itself around the landscape
and called upon the public to rejoice at what
had happened. This was that the Labor pro-
gramme being supported by one-sixteenth
more of the population than was opposed to
it: was in the Senate guarded by Senators six
times more numerous than their opponents.
Labor described this as a “sweeping victory.”
It orated triumphantly about “the vote of
confidence given by the people.” It made
jocular comparisons between the miserable
little contingent of five Liberals and the 31
victorious Laborites thrown up from the
ballot-box. It was a great party win, true
enough. Also it was a smack in the face for
every man with the stuff of a democrat in
him. On no ground could this preposterous
over-representation of the majority be de-
fended. But it carried its own nemesis
The majority Senators saw nothing but their
own 31 and the ridiculous five. They forgot
the people outside. They overlooked that
the margin between their own 61 milfion votes
and the 5* million votes belonging to electors
who had been virtually muzzled was mighty
narrow. They Save leisure now to inspect
the same thing turned upside. At. the
Senate elections just completed not a single
Labor candidate, was returned. Phewsion
grabbed 18 seats, and it is morally certain
that had the whole Senatorial bunch
been up for judgment there would not to-day
be one Labor man in the Senate. Yet there
were 2,750,000 votes cast at the ballot against
3,250,000 votes for Phews. One side gets
all the available representation ; the 2,750,000
no more than if it lived in Timbuctoo. You
will have observed that Liberalism rejoices
at this, even as Labor did in its hours of
ignorant inflation years ago. For the poli-
tician living from hand to mouth, who inter-
prets everything in terms of his own or his
party’s immediate success, . there may seem
some measure of poetic justice in the happen-
ings of the other day—provided, of course,
he is not on the side presented with the
boot. But does any Australian citizen with
a free mind imagine that a democracy can
govern itself by accidents? Can it be sup-
posed that the banishment of opinion from
Parliament is desirable, or that an institu-
tion in which rival political sentiments are
alternately throttled is distinguishable from
tyranny? The argument that nearly three
million votes which missed the ’bus can cap-
ture all the accommodation in the vehicle
next time if they are reinforced by a frac-
tional percentage of the votes which went
the other way, is not intended for serious
attention. It Implies leaving public affairs
to the determination of a handful of electors,
and these the most unstable of their kind. It
means saying that government ought to be
the monopoly of the stragglers who might be
cut out of either one of two mobs. These
jests lead nowhere but to muddle, unless it
may be to ruin. Most men would like to hear
some reason why they should not regard this
last election as additional evidence that the
Australian Senate is an insult to Australian
democracy—and always has been. It repre-
sents a survival of medievalism.
* * * #
In constitutional theory the Senate is not
merely a second chamber of revision ; it is
more. It is the chamber in which the States,
considered as separate entities and corpo-
rate parts of the Commonwealth, are repre-
sented. The States are thus represented in
order that they may maintain and protect
their constitutional rights against invasion:
to give them opportunity for advocacy ot
their special interests as States and ventila-
tion of their grievances. Around this ques-
tion fierce controversy raged in the pre-
Federation era, the net result of which was
a series of compromises with fact, experience,
hope and resulting in the manu-
facture of a Senate in which representation
is purely territorial, the constitutional idea
being that the States, not the people thereof,
are represented. Thus was supposed to be
crystallised all human wisdom in relation to
second chambers. In practice the Senate
has not been a State House or anything lik.
one—nor even a chamber of revision. State
considerations have not weighed in its selec-
tion. It is just a collection of partisans,
elected on party tickets, playing a strenuous
hand in the party game—a duplication, so
to speak, of the House of Representatives.
In 16 years it has been proved not once or
twice but many times that the functions the
Senate was designed to perform have been
obliterated, and that Australia has a Parlia-
ment vitally differing from the one it
reckoned on having. In some respects the
Australian Senate is the most powerful
second chamber in the world. But it rests
on a basis as false as a judge’s wig. James
Bryce, in “Studies in History and Juris-
prudence,” writing of it, says:—
It embodies an experiment full of instruction for
the future. The statesmen of the Convention whiefi
framed this latest addition to the world’s stock o.
Instruments of Government passed in review all pre-
vious experiments, found in them examples to follow
and other examples to shun, and drew from theta thf
best essence of the teachings they were fltten to
impart. When the Convention delivered its highly-
finished scheme of policy it delivered judgment upon,
the work of all who had gone before.


And what that Convention thought it had
made it had not made. The judgment of
experience will be upon a scheme almost
opposite to what the framers of the Consti-
tution had in view. At the election just over
what State interests were at stake? What
candidate or elector thought of State as
opposed to national interests or party pro-
grammes? Not one. The community was
divided into warring groups, of which one
was struggling to get a majority in the
Senate in order to hamstring the House of
Reps, on national issues; another was fight-
ing to get a majority in order that Parlia-
ment might not be paralysed. The
bi-cameral system of Parliamentary govern-
ment is one of the unsolved problems of
democracy all the world over, and there is
nothing Australia can point to to-day to show
that the Commonwealth has got any nearer
to perfection than any other country. The
Senate is Federal in conception—therefore
it is anti-national. Its attempts to function
as a national body are futile, for it is
parochial in composition.
But the status of the Senate in the
Commonwealth outfit is of less direct import-
ance just now than the system on which the
Senators are elected. Whatever its place
may be in theory or its functions in actual
practice the system of election (the French
scrutin de list ) is not defensible. It begins
by giving one political party or another a
ridiculous majority, and ends by denying
every other party a chance to participate
in the responsibilities of government. . It
sells the electorates body and bones to the
partisan with the loudest howl or the most
complete organisation. There is the bizarre
possibility that a single voter in each of the
six States might by his scrap of paper make
millions of votes ineffective and hand control
of the Senate over to a single party. Thei’e
is the certainty every time of the Senate
being as misrepresentative of State and
national opinion as if the election took place
in Mesopotamia. Take N. S. Wales. There,
approximately, 1,100,000 votes have elected
the three Senators: 1,000,000 votes none.
In Bananaland 400,000 votes return three
members; 390,000 votes were as inoperative
as if they numbered two. All that great
body of opinion represented by these
1,390,000 votes in two States is denied a
share of expression ; 1,400,000 votes monop-
olise the situation. To call this a joke
hardly describes it. And the worst of it is
that every time this kind of thing happens
the people who immediately benefit naturally
become convinced that change must not be
thought of. Labor saw the injustice and
danger of it all and rubbed its hands and
said “Thank God.” Liberalism makes the
same remark —and all the time the Senate
is drifting into the position of being the
stronghold of reaction, whether Labor or the
other crowd has ama jority. Both sides want
to have it so—according to whether one or
the other is on top. Hughes in days gone
by defended misrepresentation in the Senate.
His only regret now is that the misrepresen-
tation is not even greater. But the Senate,
whatever it may be, cannot be taken by the
Australian democracy as a refuge for poli-
ticians. If half of the people are refused a
hearing the community might as well not
have a vote —for it is useless. Proportional
representation and the preferential vote
might afford some relief from all this mass
of absurdity, but not so long as State repre-
sentation is equal. And if State representa-
tion were not equal and there was represen-
tation of people and political opinion instead
of mere earth, then the Senate would not
be what the Constitution contemplated, and
talk about its existence and powers be
away ' back at the starting-point. That
is where the Senate and the Constitution is
now. Failure is written all over it.
Representative government in Australia is
on a basis of strangulation, and a Constitu-
tion which from foundation upwards is one
the people reckoned they were not having
anything to do with.
[To express the views of The Bulletin, by S. H.
Prior, F. J. Kirby and D. M. Wright, 214 George
Street, Sydney.)
PLAIN ENGLISH.
When Religion Refuses to Be Made a
Political Catspaw.
The deadlock which is supposed to have
beep reached in England with regard to Irish
Home Rule is apparently purely an affair
of British politicians. The latest indications
from Ireland itself seem to point to a grow-
ing feeling of unity. Of course, the rem-
nants of the Sinn Fein don’t join in, and the
Orangeman still beats his drum with violence
to the memory of a dead Teutonic prince;
but when the names of three Protestant
bishops are found side by side with those of
15 Catholic ones demanding that there shall
be no partition of the island, it looks as if
commonsense were winning out. The sup-
posed religious differences of Ireland, magni-
fied for political reasons by people in Eng-
land, have never had t he influence on politics
that Orangemen and Engish Tories assert.
Ireland’s very best rebels have been Pro-
testant, and many of her most beloved
national leaders have adhered to the same
faith. It was Protestant enthusiasm that
stormed the town of Antrim in the face of
eavalry and artillery, and it was Protestant
statesmanship that won a brief respite from
the madness of ages when Grattan secured
a free Irish Parliament in Dublin. Anybody
who knows Ulster knows that the Orange-
men who profess to stand most for sec-
tarianism work least for their religion; and
that if it wasn’t for the seriousness of a sec-
tion of Englishmen who mistake yell for
religious conviction the controversy between
green and orange would never have been
presented as a struggle between church sys-
tems. Thierry, the French historian of the
Norman Conquest and the things that sprang
from it, records that in the United Ireland
insurrection both priest and parson made
this clear. One rebel went to the scaffold at
Carrickfergus attended by a priest and two
Presbyterian ministers. If the commonsense
of Irish Protestant clerics would now come
to the rescue, and following the lead of the
three _ bishop*, state plainly in English ears
the simple truth that no churckgoing Irish-
man is in the least danger from his Catholic
neighbors, it might make even Edward
Carson realise that the so-called Ulster
movement is a loud-mouthed futility. But
Carson has enough on his hands at the
Admiralty just at present; and his prosecu-
tion of his share of the war doesn’t seem to
be of a kind to increase his hold on English
opinion. In fact, there are unkind critics
who say that they begin to realise what
Ulster’s conduct of a civil war under his
leadership would have been like and who
even hint that the no-surrender business,
backed by three-and-sixpenny German rifles,
wouldn’t have hurt much.
Perverted Democraticism.
“0.K.”: Richmond (Melb.) Town Council,
being mostly animated by the unpleasantly
democratic spirit of Webber, M.L.A., has
granted licenses for the establishment of
fellmongeries on the Yarra at Richmond,
whereby the whole scheme for making miles
i of river bank beautiful may be reduced to an
absurdity, or something very like it. _ A first
protest, made by the more aesthetic' rate-
payers of Richinond to the Board of Health,
seems doomed to failure, inasmuch as fell-
mongery stinks are not physically unhealthy;
so the outcry has been taken up by Melb.
City Council and the Public Works Depart-
ment, on the “public nuisance” ticket. Obvi-
ously the smellful industries aforesaid would
have to be de-licensed some time or other;
the nuisance couldn’t continue forever in a
crowded residential district, near to the
beauty spots of Melbourne city, and if it is
admitted that the fellmongeries would be
bound to disappear, sooner or later, even
Webber, M.L.A., cannot give any convincing
excuse for encouraging an objectionable stink
in the meantime. The kindest supposition re
these licenses granted by the Richmond Coun-
cil is that they were intended to assert
Labor’s claim to select convenient fields of
industry, without the least regard to the
bloated indignation of adjacent propputy-
owners. Rather than suspect the councillors
of any personal business interests in the
matter, one would ascribe their action to a
kink in their good democratic understanding.
The writer looks back to the time when the
beautification of St. Ivilda-road was in its
infancy, and there was talk of the arrange-
ments in view whereby the unlovely fac-
tories of South Melbourne would be put out
of sight of St. Kilda-road travellers. Then,
also, did democracy (with a kinked under-
standing) protest against the mstheticism of
those people who would put ugly things out
of public evidence if there be no just cause
on reason why it should not be done. Half-
baked Socialists wrote to The Bulletin
about it, evidently expecting the journal to
sympathise with the idea that no selfish
capitalist should be allowed to avoid eye-
contact with the sordid aspects of life. The
idea that workers would be all the better if
every prospect from the St. Kilda-road were
made pleasing was an idea that had not come
within the philosophy of these bawlers,
and Richmond Council is, perhaps, suffering
from the delusion that, because fellmongering
is a malodorous trade which honest workers
are engaged in, the whole of the neighboring
Yarra district should not be too proud and
haughty to put up with the stink. The same

A Few Things Australia Doesn’t Need.
“Good Australian” from the U.S.A.:—
There are numerous things Australia does not want
which are easily catalogued.
Big populations do not lead to more happiness and
comfort for the majority of the people. The dense
populations of the U.S.A., China, India and Japan
do not make those countries happy. We do not
want a turbulent flood of immigrants after the war,
mostly ignorant, all of them inferior to ourselves, no
matter whence they emerge. Our country is doing
very nicely, and there is no need for it to grow “big”
hastily. Let us go along slowly, increasing our num-
bers and our wealth by natural evolution. The
corollary of the White Australia policy is to improve
the lot and take more care of the white Australians
already here and those who come here every night.
Enormous trade figures are no use to most of
us; enormous wealth is no good to any of us.
Why should we strive for them? Let the United
States strain themselves to achieve columns of bil-
lions in people and in dollars. Why should we try
to imitate them ? Are we not doing very well, all
of us all round, in money, in health and in ethics?
Parasites should not be allowed to increase; rather
ought they be encouraged to decrease. Therefore
we do not want any more politicians or Parliaments,
and we do not want any more representatives. Six of
our Parliaments and 600 of our politicians are a
wasteful and ridiculous excess, a useless set. of
encumbrances entailing too many laws, too many
loans, too much waste of money and men. We
would enjoy just as much liberty under one Parlia-
ment as we do under seven, and an Imperial Parlia-
ment, from Australia’s viewpoint, would be a delu-
sion and a snare.
Big statistics not denoting more comfort or
freedom, we ought to set our faces strongly against
dumping. Our tariff question should not be treated
as a partisan or as a political one. High Protection
we require urgently, allied with the strictest penal
laws against dumping. Tariff rates on luxuries
should be modelled on the ancient sumptuary laws,
and made so stiff that those who bought diamonds
and furs would pay enough in duties for the upkeep
of an efficient fleet of submarines; while those on
silks, laces and millinery would maintain a complete
aerial service. Our first and last care should be to
protect our own wage-earners and producers against
those of every other country whether it paid high or
low wages, whether it was an ally or a neutral.
Platitudes, are they ? Can the truth be told too
often? This country does not need a world status
or a plaoe in the sun. It does want to sit tight on
the rights and privileges of its own people; and if
somebody is not vigilant in the near future, Aus-
tralia is going to be made a dumping-ground, a
scrapheap, a spoil-tip for the products and the people
of foreign countries not so good as this to whom we
are under no obligation.
Of the smaller things, we do not want slums, or
millionaires or imported professors and experts to
establish the egg-sucking industry. There is no call
here for town-planners or wowsers, or more noise or
additional hustle. None of these things add to indi-
vidual comfort. Let us contrive to remain behind
tfee times in many things, for they don’t all spell
happiness to you and to me. Skyscrapers, luxurious
betels, express lifts, faster trains, high-speed electric
trams, quicker telephones—none of these “modern
necessities” are really essential to peace and com-
fort. All these and many other like contrivances we
can have here if we choose to pay for them, but why
should we? We ought to try to make Australia a
place where honest business gains fair profits, and
decent people live in peace and comfort.
All this looks heterodox and narrow. Well, let it
so seem. It would not if we knew how well off we
are. We enjoy quite a lot of things other nations
do not possess. The lowest death-rate, the lowest
crime statistics, the lowest disease figures, together
with the best food, the best of every kind of climate,
and the most freedom. What more do we want?
Isolation by distance is our strongest coast defence,
with universal training as our safest domestic
defence. Why.should we jeopardise our safety, im-
peril our liberty and our prosperity by crying out to
people who are not our equals, “Come here and we
will share with you all we hold dear” ?
A Swindle that Needs Suppressing.
Any inquiring person who, marvelling at the
new outgrowths of superstition to be seen
on every hand, tries to discover the secret
of the success of Spiritualism and other
profitable mysteries, can get a strong hint
from the appearance of those who attend the
stances and manifestations. The bulk of
the people present are garbed in black. It
is when bereavement is fresh in the mind
that the great uncertainty with regard to
the things beyond the control of the under-,
taker is really felt; and those of a certain
mental twist will swallow any extravagances
of the professor of the occult, and pay for
them if only they can be persuaded that they
are getting messages from the dead. The
present war has given splendid facilities for
all sorts of charlatans, and the cheapest kind
of fortune-tellers get a hearing in the hope
that they might know something about the
men at the Front. The whole thing is par-
ticularly cruel; and the special measures
being taken by the N. S. Wales police _ to
get at the offenders ought to get a helping
hand from decent people. The harm done is
in some cases very serious; and as w word
from the charlatan may rob a credulous girl
of most of the joy of life and fill her with
despair, it is time to transfer some of the
trouble to the swindler who plays with souls
at ss. a time.
Let Us Pray.
“Fax’*: As a constitutional unbeliever, with
a sympathetic feeling towards the theory of
Christian Faith and a sincere regard for
simple piety in other people, I hope I may
be allowed to argue that the Churches are
bound to do more harm than good by the
ancient practice of inviting public prayers
for the recovery of sick individuals. To me
it seems even more impolitic than_ the osten-
tatious offering of prayers for rain or sun-
shine, seeing that the rain or sunshine _ is
certain to arrive‘presently, whereas the sick
person’s decease is bound to occur sooner or
later, pray we never so earnestly. When I was
young I used to attend Anglican Church ser-
vices where the parson sometimes desired the
prayers of the congregation for a parishioner
who was said to be “seriously ill.” I can’t
remember the time when I didn’t think the
parson made a grave mistake in asking the
true believers among the congregation (if
there were any such) to take the risk of
praying for the recovery of anybody from a
serious illness. The first thing an intelligent
kiddie learns from frequent attendance at
church is the inefficacy of prayer where
sick persons of importance are concerned.
Or, at least, it was so in my time. Some
member of the large and overfed Royal
Family was always passing from the sick
list to the grave despite the parson’s appeals
for preventive spiritual measures. The
prayers of the righteous did not avail.
Sometimes it would be Mrs. Smith, the
well-beloved mother of a large family, who
was prayed for, and then my infant curiosity
was aroused as to whether the poor lady
would get better, and I found that her re-
covery was the rare exception rather than
the Christian rule. Moreover, I was not too
young to reason that if heartfelt prayer
could rescind the law of Nature in particular
cases (and if in some cases, why not in all?)
an admirable mother would never die. Thus
I soon became confirmed in my natural Un-
belief. Therefore was I sorry to read in a
Melbourne journal .that Dr. Mannix was
requesting private prayers, especially the
prayers of children, for Archbishop Carr in
the illness which proved fatal. The ineffi-
cacy of prayer as regards the saving of
human life is the lesson of every war. At
the present time, when Christian Faith
is suffering from a storm of cruel
blows, the world’s stock of piety seems
pretty sure to be impoverished, in a
Johnsonian way of speaking, by the risky
appeal for prayers of children for the con-,
tinued preservation of a very old and very
sick prelate. Archbishop Carr died, as he
was most likely to do. Owing to his death
having been slow, instead of sudden (in
which case there would have been no special
prayers offered), it pointed the very moral
that all good churchmen are combating all
their lives.

From Thomas McMahon, a journalist who
has been studying the land which is ruled
by a horse doctor:—
There is a complaint, not only in the Territory,
but out of it, that some few big financial cos. have
had distributed among them a vast area of its best
country. This is hardly true, inasmuch that,
though some of these concerns have very large
quantities of land, it will be found in every instance
that it is a mixture of good, bad, fair and indif-
ferent. It is further complained that absurdly
easy terms, with long leases, are ensuring for specu-
lators prospects of enormous profits. As a matter
of fact, 42 years’ lease is the longest in the Terri-
tory, and, judging by the rate of progress, condi-
tions both natural and political will have to
change very much if at the end of their leases these
cos. get out in a solvent state. Some might be like
the Englishman who bought his parcel from the
South Australian Government some few years back
thinking he had an easy bargain. He is now rush-
ing this property into the market at 2s. 6d. per
acre, after giving 10s. an acre and patting in some
development work. Judging by the way unionism
blocks the advance of the small-capital man, it
seems only possible for big financial people to
undertake the risk. Vestts. for instance, have up
to date risked over a million of money in the
Northern Territory. Their meat works, not yet
completed, was tendered for at about £lOO,OOO, and
so far they have had to put up nearly £600,000
and not one beast has been slaughtered. The follow-
ing are some of the items of the parcelling out of
the Territory: The Bovril Estates comprise 11,380
square miles; lease expires 1943 ; rental per annum,
£ll3B. Vestys, 7843 square miles; leases expiring
1942 and 1944 ; rental, £7S4. J. Bradshaw com-
mands 6800 square miles; lease expires, 1944 ; rental,
£6BO. The total ground rental of these holdings
is £2602 per annum, the area of country, being
equal to the size of England. The lease rents are
based as follows: The first seven years, 6d. per square
mile per annum; second seven years, Is. per square
mile per annum; third seven years, 2s. per square
mile per annum ; and the balance of the term, 2s. 6d.
per square mile per annum. Altogether there are
about 17 lessees who hold 81,729 square miles. The
Territory, by the way, comprises 336,116,800 acres.

From a man who knows:—
Some five years ago the N. S. Wales Department of
Agriculture started a number of demonstration farms
to show the cocky the “commercial results of scien-
tific principles.” But so far the Department’s appli-
cation of scientific principles has brought about
nothing to the State but the loss of a lot of hard
cash. A belated report for the year ending June,
1916, which has just drifted along, shows that it is
up to someone to stop the waste. Last year the
22 farms of various sorts cost £121,000 to maintain,
whilst another £87,415 of good capital was expended
on them. From the whole, revenue totalling £ 52,712
was received; so apart from capital expenditure there
was a loss of over £68,000. Except the little experi-
mental farm at Pera Bore, all the others lost money
—most of them large sums. The Hawkesbury Agri-
cultural College and a few othei's are doing good
experimental and educational work and shouldn’t be
expected to show a profit. But most of the older
experimental farms have got right away from the
experimental side-—as a result of a foolish official
policy—and have become mere illustrations of how-
to lose money at farming. Worst of all are sundry
farms that were launched for the one purpose of
proving that scientific farming pays. Temora farm
is a striking example of failure. In five years its
revenue has totalled £lB4B ; has cost the country
over £13,000, and has proved of practically no use.
It was started in a district where private farmers
had proved that wheat-growing paid well, and the
sooner the Department gets out of the business there
the better for its reputation. Other demonstration
farms have been almost as futile, and should also be
closed down. Newer ventures, such as the North Ban-
garoo horse farm, Trangie experiment farm, aud Emu
Plains irrigation farm, which in prison reform is a
useful social experiment, deserve a chance to show
what they can do, providing expenditure is care-
fully watched. At present the expenditure on all the
farms is very high, and suggests extravagance. A visit
to any of these Government concerns and a comparison
with the running of a successful private farm show
in five minutes where the money is wasted. Success-
ful investigation into farming, diseases of stock,
fruit, plants and so on is of immense value, and the
Department should test out every new principle and
pass the result on to the man on the land. But that
work can be done for a proportion of the money
now wasted.
“Sky Pilot” writes:—
Re the article, “Running with the Microbe and
Hunting with the Doctor” (B. 12/4/’l7). From
the bishop’s point of view—and from that of any
decent man—sexual vice is vile and must not he
made “safe,” but be abolished. It isn’t venereal
disease that they fight, it’s sin—the root, not the
leaves.
To which The Bulletin adds that
the fight of the bishops against sexual sin
is certainly a stirring spectacle; but
it isn’t particularly dangerous—to the sin.
If the bishops and “Sky Pilot” had some
decent and practicable moral remedy to
suggest which would be more' effective than
plain medical treatment, coupled with the
compulsory notification of the disease, their
talk about getting to the root and leaving
the leaves alone might be all right; but
even moral treatment would hardly suggest,
as the bishops do, the leaving of the sinful
to spread their contagion as they will. Con-
sidering that this disease may be conveyed
to perfectly innocent people by a towel, a
tea-cup, or even the communion vessels used
in the services of the churches, the attempt
to separate the sheep from the goats by
shepherds who feebly wave a little crook in
a, pastoral letter doesn’t seem to indicate
much vision or earnestness of purpose. It
may be, in the kindly Christian phrase,
a “muddle-headed” view that while people
in increasing numbers are suffering and
dying from the scourge, and little children
being born into Hell because of it,
that drastic practical measures should
be taken without discrimination to save both
the sinner and the victim ; but the bishops
have been a long time fighting sexual sin and,
on all appearances, the thing has got them
pretty well submarined, blockaded and
bombed out of their trenches. Their
alleged remedy has been tried ever since
venereal disease was introduced, and it is
about time we had a change, even if it hurts
their feelings.

A War of Booze and Bash.
There was a cheerful item of news from the
Russian Front the other clay: “When the
artillery prepared to fire, the Germans
advanced, bearing white flags and vodka.”
And there the message stopped after the
manner of a serial story leaving the critical
situation suspended in an atmosphere of
doubt. What happened to the shells and the
Germans and the booze is all wrapped in a
great mystery. Did the frozen Ivan carry
a resolution of protest against the Czar’s
anti-grog ukase, and dropping his munitions
rush out to meet the national liquid? Or
did he drop a shell into the advancing Hun
and vodka and blow the outfit off the scenery ?
This paper regretfully suspects that Ivan
broke the pledge he never took and rushed
to where the German wanted him. All of
which raises the question of whether the
dryness of the Russian army has turned
out entirely satisfactory or whether it has
not given the enemy another chance to
introduce fresh tricks into the game. Any-
how, so long as there is clamorous disorder
behind the Front, the danger of the German
arriving to clean up the mess with a bayonet
ir. one hand and a dram in the other seems
well within the region of probability. This
would introduce a new note into the history
of European warfare—the defeat of a first-
class power being caused by its hankering
after democratic institutions and refresh-
ments. In the interval of waiting to hear about
what happens to the Bear it is noticeable
that on the other side of the scrum there is
no mention of the Allies advancing towards
Von Hindenberg with sausages and but-
tered toast. The yarns from that quarter are
about bombs and gas and earth-shaking
artillery fire, and long, desperate struggles
with bare hands and naked blades: of posi-
tions won and lost; of armies locked in a
murderous struggle. Every day this pande-
monium lasts makes it clearer that when the
breaking point comes the smash will sound
as if the world had torn itself up by the
roots. Meanwhile, our own Billjim is hold-
ing his end up with a strong, brawny hand,
and has done again at BuUeeourt what he
did at Pozieres. Exactly what is meant by
him and the kilted Sandv being “astride of
the Hindenberg line” will be understandable
later on. Meanwhile he is in a hot corner,
and will have to argue quite a lot about
staying there.
Archbishop Thomas Carr, who died in
Melbourne last week, was born in Gal-
way County, Ireland, in 1839. He studied
at Rome, at St. Jarlatt’s, Tuam, Co.
Galway, and at Maynooth; was ordained in
1865 and had six years of parish work. He
was curate in Westport (Archbishop John
McHale’s parish), subsequently professor
and vice-president at Maynooth. Conse-
crated Bishop of Galway in 1883, and
appointed Archbishop of Melbourne in 1886,
succeeding Dr. Gooid.
Dr. Dani.el Mannix, who succeeds Dr.
Carr as Archbishop of Melbourne, was born
at Charieville, Co. Cork, on March 4. 1864.
He was educated at St. Colman’s, Fermoy,
and at St. Patrick’s, Maynooth, where he
became in turn professor, vice-president and
president. He was a senator of the Royal
University of Ireland, and served on the
governing body of University College, Dublin.
He was a leader of education in Ireland, and
modernised St. Patrick’s College, Maynooth,
alike on its spiritual, educational and domes-
tic sides. His Reverence is also considered
one of the most brilliant theological scholars
in the Church of Rome.
“Fax”: Safe to predict that the heart of
Melbourne city will never again be enclosed
within a funeral march for two hours and
more on a Saturday afternoon, as it was
when all the cable-tramway services were
suspended on account of Archbishop Carr’s
last outing. Between 3 p.m., or earlier, and
5.15 p.m. a space half a mile long, by 220
yards wide, was a trap for the unwary. Take
the case of, say, a man who had dropped into
Cole’s Book Arcade to buy a book for his
sick mother-in-law, before going to see the
boat-race on the Yarra an hour later. He
was trapped as soon as the police closed
Coilins-street to cross-traffic. An intermin-
able procession of slow-going funereal citi-
zens was following the tramlines by which
the Book Arcade is enclosed on the north,
south and west. The impenetrable proces-
sion was in charge of Bourke-street, Eliza-
beth-street, and Coilins-street at one and the
same time. The bit of Swanston-street to
the east of the Arcade, and of every
thoroughfare up to Spring-street, was blocked
at both ends by the marchers. The interned
citizen could walk up to the east end of
Bourke or Collins streets, bu.t the half-mile
journey was useless. The liberty of the sub-
ject was at a dead-end for more than two
hours.
The second Victoria Cross won by an Aus-
tralian in Africa during the present war is
notified. The first went to Lieut. W. Dart-
nell, who died to save his men. The bit of
bronze goes this time to John Mayman, whose
name is on the honor roll of the little school
at Timor, near Maryborough (Vic.), where
l)e was born, as was Major Percy Black, who
the other day carried his many honors to a
soldier’s grave. Mayman was a Westralian
miner before he enlisted. He has been serv-
ing with Botha in East Africa, and though
he does not say how he won the Cross, his
previous record shows that he was a suitable
candidate. While working on the Hidden
Secret mine, Vic., he rescued a Dago, who,
after trying to hang himself with the
knocker-line, cut it and fell into the water
at the bottom of the shaft. In 1902 Mayman
saved a mate who was buried in running
sand at the Berry No. 1 mine, Vic. The
soldier is 47 years of age and is a brother
of George Mayman, of Kalgoorlie.
“Verdad” writes: —
Further reports from Argentine about Chile, this
time that Chile has a secret treaty with Germany,
support my contention (B. 3/5/’l7) that they are
due to Hun intrigue. The rumors are riot mentioned
by the most important dailies of Buenos Aires, and
are flatly contradicted by Chile. Germany has apolo-
gised to Argentine for sinking one of the latter
country’s ships and has promised reparation. All
this shows that Germany is really concerned about
keeping Argentine, if not friendly to Germany, at
least neutral; and one of the best ways is. by insinu-
ating that Argentine runs the risk of an attack from
Chile if she becomes pro-Ally. What caused a lot
of the friction in past times between Chile and
Argentine was the boundary question. Before the
frontier was delimited by the Commission some
years ago, this line was defined ris being the Andean
watershed—to the west was Chile, to the east was
Argentine. But the watershed was difficult to define
towards the south, where the Andes spread out and
are much lower than in the north. There are lakes
and swamps practically on the top of the ranges,
and the digging of a ditch a few feet deep would
often change the watershed a mile or two, by causing
the water to flow in the opposite direction. During
the disputes, patriotic Chileans and Argentines used
to alter the watershed with a spade at nights to
include more territory in their respective countries.
When this question was finally settled, and peace
reigned, the two countries erected the big statue of
Christ on the boundary-line on top of the Andes,
on the road between Buenos Aires and Santiago, as
an emblem of the peace that was henceforth to reign
between them.
“Norman Sinclair”: I belong to the Win-
the-War party now. In September, 1916, I
was rejected on an attempt to enlist at
Victoria Barracks, Sydney. Tossed out same
month at Brisbane; ditto a few weeks later
at Mount Morgan. So I humped Matilda
from Rockhampton to Hughenden; railed
from there to Cloncurry; took the road again
to Mt. Oxide, and then tramped back to the
Dobbin Show. Now I’m on a job with an
83 wet bulb and working six-hour shifts.
Hot as Sheol and as dusty as the Liverpool-
road. Also, there is carbonic-acid gas, and
that pulls the wind ont of a man quick. But
I can still eat a pound of tough steak for
breakfast and swallow a dollar’s worth of
arsenical beer a day. I’m thinking of going
to a physical-culture school where I might
learn to get fit and well.
“X.M.”: The fact that our present cometic
visitor was seen by casual naked-eye obser-
vers before the Australian astronomers,
with a £70,000 outfit, discovered its presence,
reminds me of the Gin. telescope in the
Federal territory. It is perched on a hill,
with a waggon-load of box-kites and other
meteorological apparatus, of no use since
installed, except to provide a job for a care-
taker. If it was not partly dismantled he
might be allowed to have a squint through
it occasionally, to see if he can spot the good
times alleged to be coming.
“Cleveland”: In peace, time I have a
special marine job, so when I decided to go
and give England a hand I guessed I could
be most useful as a submarine-hunter. I
put it to the nearest naval chiefs, and they
assured me that I was just the sort of man
that was needed urgently on the other side.
But there it ended. I was told that the only
way I could get to England was to enlist as
an infantryman. I am now learning foot-
drill as patiently as I can, and reading the
torpedo statistics.
