Last Saturday’s revival of “So Long
Letty” at Melb. Majesty’s was for six
nights only, and although a boyish, boat-racy
audience cheered the performance on its way
some of the old-time gaietjf seemed lacking.
Perhaps it was that too many soldiers have
marched out of camp to the tune of Letty,
and will never hear it more. Or Dorothy’s
vivacity was a trifle less hearty, and Work-
man’s fooling more distinctly labored than
before. Anyway, things were not quite so
brisk on the matrimonial exchange. The
interview between Marie Eaton and Frith, in
his bathing-suit, was brighter; also William
Greene, as the casual young man, was an
improvement on his predecessor, notwith-
standing the fact that Greene’s nose has no
business in a frivolous character. It is one
of the accepted features of stage villainy;
nature intended it to be associated with dark
deeds in bellowdrama. A new lady shaped
promisingly as Tommy’s aunt (who must be
a half-witted relative), and the 80 prancing-
girls were a joy. Their pink-and-gold cos-
tumes have lost their early freshness, but the
interlacing of their arms is beautiful, and the
unanimity with which they, shake their legs
at the prevailing depression nearly persuades
the writer that all the world’s a picnic, and
all the men and women merely out for a
spree. “High Jinks” goes up next Saturday
for six nights, and then “The Three Twins”
will get a look in.

The humor and satire of “Penelope” have
put in their allotted nine nights at Mel-
bourne Royal, and the place where Marie
Tempest learned to remember that even the
barndoor hen gives her lord and master a run
is now closed for two nights. On Satur-
day a new melodrama comes along at
the Royal. “London Pride” brings from
its darkened birthplace a good reputation.
Barry Lupino, lately resting in hospital, is
back again as Very Good Eddie at Melbourne
King’s, and every night he rushes madly
downstairs to dazzle the hotel clerk with his
skill in projecting cigarette-smoke from his
little nose. In other respects the show is as
it was before Lupino happened to nearly kill
himself.

At the Melbourne Princess’s the nights of
“The Bnnyip” panto, are numbered —end of
next week and no further. There are only
three more matindes whereat children can
criticise the performance for a shilling from
any part of the house save the ampitheatre,
where the fee for inspection is sixpence.
This fourth week of Ada Reeve’s season at
Melb. Tivoli has the same bill as last week,
except that the tabloid tragedy is missing,
and a new turn —Raymond Wilmot Wilbert
on the Golf Links —is helping to fill the gap.
The versatile histrionic Ada has not much
more time to stay.
Next Tuesday (22nd) afternoon there will
be a large and various show at the Royal in
aid of the widow of Tom Dawson, a com-
edian who made his mark on the Australian
vaudeville stage in dismal, pessimistic char-
acter songs. One of his specialties was “a
hard-boiled egg.” Tom was a good Aus-
tralian as well as an amusing comic song-
ster, and he died fighting for his country at
the Battle of Pozieres. The benefit for his
widow will be Melbourne’s special oppor-
tunity to acknowledge the services of its
departed friend.
Those wonderful Picture-girls’ Eyes,
whereof Ada Reeve sings, are in full glare at
the Melba and the Britannia (Melb.) this
week. The Britannia is featuring Dorothy
Dalton in the same lady-vampire character
that she adorned on the Majestic screen last
week. The title of “The Female of the
Species” does only half justice to her awful-
ness. At the Melba all who have tears to
shed must prepare to shed them on behalf of
Theda Bara in “Her Double Life.” The play
is just another perversion of “The New Mag-
dalen” story of old days. Theda penitently
tries to lose herself under a new name, but
the villain still pursues her. A look of terror
in the eyes is Miss Bara’s specialty, and a
blood-curdling sneer is the trump-card of
Stuart Holmes, who plays the persistent
villain.
★ * *
At Hoyts’ Melbourne theatres this week
an Amurkan mining picture-drama is leading
item. Ethel Barrymore plays heroine in
“The White Raven,” and her influence over
the wild, unwashed “boys” is worthy of
Tittell Brune at her best. The play is in
five acts and 75 emotions. The dissolute
characters who gaze longingly at Ethel in
the course of her adventures are a numerous
and motley crowd, and her behavior under
the circumstances is a lesson to mere bits o’
fluff. The question as to whether souls are
by nature transmigratory is treated in “The
Soul’s Cycle,” which is a fanciful film for
far-thinking philosophers.
Adelaide is getting the afternoon-show habit
strongly. The Majestic seems to have good
houses every day for its variety show, in
which, just now are the Belldays, and Rose
and Bell, fine athletes. The male partner is a
moving humorist also. Wondergraph Pictures,
after experimenting with continuous —to
which the Grand and. Pav. still stick—is now
trying its evening bill twice in the after-
noon. The Royal, when not occupied by
drama, shows pictures twice a day, but
West’s sticks to evenings only—bar Satur-
days. The only drama in town just now is
at the Tivoli, where the “King of Crime”
has been chiefly an opportunity for George
Cross, and Blanche McPherson (as the blind
wife).
There remain only two more nights of
Kathlene MacDonell’s remarkable presenta-
tion of the modern practitioner of the oldest
profession; and those who haven’t yet seen
this brilliant actress in the delightful Eng-
lish comedy, “Outcast,” run a big chance of
missing a worth-while show. Marie Tempest
is due at Sydney Criterion on Saturday;
meantime Kathlene MacDonell (and Charles
Waldron) are still with us.

“J.R.”: The Swedish Government has
embarrassed itself by food riots, by con-
tracting to sell the German Government
many millions of pounds of reindeer venison,
for which the Lapps, who are to supply the
carcases of their tame deer, are to get some-
thing like 7d. per pound live weight. These
deer have been allowed to feed in the forests
of Lapland, and it is renorted that when
the present stock is disposed of under con-
tract with Germany, the Lapps will not be
allowed to restock, and in that way hungry
Sweden will, when next winter comes round,
find itself with not only its regular European
population short of food, but a semi-civilised
aboriginal Arctic population howling at its
cold back door and trying to buy food that
doesn’t exist with frozen sevenpences.
“Hamer”: Fate pointed out a derisive
finger at the Germans one recent day when
the latest thing in submarine frightfulness
had an interview with a steamer
which was ferrying some British troops
across the Channel. This vessel is so
ancient that it shoves itself through the
water by means of paddle-wheels. But when
the torpedo discharged by the submarine
missed its mark, the ancient shot out all
the speed the engines could raise and
charged; and it was the projecting paddle
that caught the latest thing in Hun crafti-
ness and sent it to the bottom.
“Caroline”: Here’s another little item for
Webster’s list of postal complaints. When
the Anzacs were transferred from Gallipoli
to France my brother was in hospital in
Egypt. While he was there he didn’t re-
ceive any letters, although we at home were
writing regularly. Later, when he returned
to duty, and joined his battalion in France,
he received no less than 87 letters on one
day and 9% on the next! Still-, he did get
them, which is more than some of the others
did!
“Poll Clerk”: The result of the Senate
voting showed the considerable amount of
difficulty the electors had in grappling with
the task of voting. D. Watson easily ied the
Labor bunch in N. S. Wales because a
goodly number of electors thought they were
voting for J. C. Watson. In my booth the
number who plumped for the top three on
the card was surprising. One old chap came
in to mai’k his card, but clean forgot who
he was to leave in. So he asked per-
mission to retire, and soon returned chant-
ing, “Bowlingraeandwatson, Bowlingraeand-
watson. Bowlingraeandwatson,” the whole
time he was waiting to be attended to.
Nothing is too good for our returned
soldiers, of course. This is probably why, in
some; cases, the officials at booths on the
sth inst. were torn away from their State-
paid jobs (their screws running quietly along
in the meantime) and thrust into polling-
billets at one or two guineas for the day,
while the returned soldier went on drawing
his small pension and hunting for a job. Of
course, handing out ballot-papers needs skill
of a superior kind, and old friends must not
be forgotten; but the hard-up so’dier
deserves more consideration when there’s a
spare pound or two going—especially as he
is repeatedly told by political claquers that
he is Australia’s saviour.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The story of how the long late Martin
Simonsen came to leave Germany would be
worth retelling these times, even though it
had been told of yore. Simonsen, a Dane
by birth, was violinist to the Court of Ham-
let in his young days, hence his ambition to
settle down as a musical professor in Ger-
many. He went to Hamburg to make his
home, and it was from there that he went to
America on a starring tour. For advt. pur-
poses, or because he loved dawgs, Simonsen
kept a beautiful St. Bernard whilst he was
living in Hamburg. In a quite unaccount-
able manner the noble brute got off the chain
and was found roaming at large by the dis-
trict police officer. Simonsen was summoned
before the local magistrate and lined. The
same thing happened again and again, till
at last; the much-lined musician began to
keep his big dawg under constant observa-
tion. Finally, he saw the animal being let
loose by the very man who used to serve him
with the summons. When the irate fiddler
was called to court this time he explained
the mystery of his dog’s previous wanderings
in the light of the recent revelation, but his
evidence didn’t go for a row of pins. The
informant swore that he discovered the St.
Bernard on the loose, and was innocent ot
having ever in his life unfastened the
animal’s collar. The magistrate held that
no good German could doubt the word of a
man in authority, and the raging Martin
Simonsen was fined again. Thereupon his
nibs sold up his home and shook the dust of
Germany from his boots. His trouble in
later life was that his name always caused
him to be classed among the purely German
Jews.
“L.H.P.”: When Allan Wilkie played
“The Bells” recently in Melbourne, he staged
on the same evening a curtain-raiser called
“Delicate Ground,” which was regarded as
a curiosity. Originally done at Dondon
Lyceum in November, ’49, by Charles Mat-
thews, R. Roxley; and the great Vestns, the
piece was very popular. It was from the
pen of Charles Dance, the scene laid in Pans
during the revolution of ’93, and Pauline, the
heroine, was one of the finest emotional pai ts
to be found in the range of one-acters.
“Hal August”: Some Sydney suburban
picture-gazers sat up in their seats and
stared the other evening when a three-rceler
was fairly on its way. The leading juvenile
in the film was a German character inter-
preted by a German actor. The audience
hooted warningly, but the operator kept on
turning until the Hun stood before a framed
portrait of the Kaiser and saluted. At this
stage the audience started to howl and
stamp; the picture was stopped, and an
apologetic manager explained that the reel
had only been received that morning and
hadn’t been inspected by the management.
The explanation satisfied the audience; but
the film censorship and the exchange that
supplied the sausage-roll might be asked a
few questions.
“Private Boxer”: It is difficult to realise
that Lizette Parkes, the juvenile leading
lady of “Turn to the Right,” was not so
very long ago “Baby Parkes,” a song-and-
dance prodigy of the Rickards halls. The
versatile young Australian has certainly
made good in her country and can give a
lead to many highly-priced star importations.
Lizette’s younger sister, Elsie, by the way,
is also commencing her career in vaudeville.
She is one of the singing and dancing extra
ladies who provide the necessary ornamental
background to the Tivoli Follies.
“Masetto”: Melba is always welcomed'

who were to make the trip.
“Harrison O.”: “Half an Hour,” the
Barrie playlet that Ada Reeve is doing at
Melbourne Tivoli, is in no way characteristic
of the author. Its melodramatic story might
have been made more effective by a less-
gifted writer. Yet Barrie, in his own vein,
is a master of the one-act play. “Rosalind”
and “The Twelve-Pound Look” are two per-
fect little things of their kind, and Ada
Reeve or anyone’else who put them on would,
deserve public gratitude. Miss Reeve’s
talents would have fine scope in “Rosalind,”
a charming story of an actress, beautiful in
her warpaint, who escapes to a distant
village from a boy-lover, loafs about in large
slippers and loose clothes, and revels in the
comfortable feeling of being middle-aged.
Failing professional presentation, the Reper-
tory , Theatre might do worse than try a
Barrie one-acter. The fact that in England
Barrie is a wildly-successful commercial
di*amatist need not deter director McMahon.
There are a number of commercially-success-
ful English dramatists whose work is never
likely .to be seen here except in a Repertory
Theatre, a? ..hose whom it hath pleased
Frovidencp to place xn charge of our theatri-
cal affahs have decided that certain of the
plays would not. be understood in these
benighted parts. ,
The encore nuisance, which makes the
ordinary musical show so tiresomely long,
has been quietly suppressed at the
Melbourne municipal concerts under Dr.
Price’s direction. A line on the bill politely
requests the audience nof to demand a
second helping of individual coux-ses, and the
crowd is content to accept one soixg at a time
as a fair thing. After all the “inevitable
encore” system is due to the song-recital
artists who are down for only five numbers
on the programme, but are tacitly under-
stood to be prepared to give double as many.
The bill, in their case, is admittedly open to
extension, and the audience bangs its hands
until the artist sings again. The same rule
applies at musical-comedy first-nights, when
persistent encores are supposed to be signs
of success. Everything worth mentioning is
done twice over, and the performance lasts
half an hour longer than at a matinee, where
it goes on its bare merits and is less tedious.
“Up Stage”: We were playing in Cane-
ville at the time, and having failed to excite
the frivolous, decided to appeal to the pious
by staging “The Christian.” Benson, our
“heavy” man, was gloomily cursing the pre-
carious financial position, and the mana-
gerial edict that “members of the co. will
refrain from sending any laundry out, as the
prospect of making an undignified exit from
the town seems probable,” when he became
interested in two sunburnt drunks babbling
outside the pub. One blinked sleepily at
Glory Quayle on the playbill and said : “Er
bloke mi’ ’swell go t’ther gaff t’night.” His
friend gravely concuri-ed. Benson ap-
proached them bowing. “Thinking of seeing
the show to-night, gentlemen? Allow me to
book your two seats now.” They solemnly
paid out —and we fled on the wifigs of their
money. They were too drunk to appreciate
good acting, anyhow.
Died recently in Melbourne, Fred Hailes,
son of Geoi'ge Button Hailes, old-time Fitz-
royite, who had five sons all over 6ft. in
height. He was close up to the ’7o’s when
he went over. A life-long acquaintance with
music and musicians constituted him an
authority. In the long-gone days of “Penny
Readings” the father and his boys
took part in the old Fitzroy Court-
house, glees, rounds and such being a family
specialty. One of the boys has been for 30
years in the Williamson orchestra. Old G-
B. Hailes came to Melbourne in 1840, and
was one of the executors of the will of John
Pascoe Fawkner.
“Javon”: Several months ago “Jwosp”
pointed out that a sketch in a Sydney show
had been looted from a Maoriland pressman.
The sketch climaxed neatly with the dis-
covery by a jealous man that he had done
the shooting in the wrong flat, aud had a
strange dead couple on his hands. The idea
is older than “Jwosp” thinks. It was given
to John L. Toole in ’79 to make a play out
of, and was even then a century old in Eng-
lish. In the original yarn an Assyi’ian
gentleman had a hand.
“S.S.”: It was contended by Wilson
Forbes, veteran Australian actor, just dead,
that an actor was a very bad actor indeed
who could not do something rather better
than any other actor. He was not averse
to reciting a case in point which gave .him
precedence in one department of art over
actors much more eminent. It all happened
long ago, in days when W.F. had not
had the opportunities that came later of
manifesting his versatility. An entertain-
ment had been organised for some charity
purpose, the chief interest of which lay in
the fact that the most gifted actors were to
play the most trifling parts. Titlieradge
was asked to come in with a tray, deliver a
letter, duck, and back out again. “I can’t
do it.” he said, “I have neither the ability
nor the personal appearance. One man in
Australia has made that sort of thing dis-
tinctly his own. I doubt if the public will
stand for any other man in the part. Give
it to Mr. Wilson Forbes.”
Instead of the outbursts of mummer-
worship that mark ordinary first nights at
Melbourne Majesty’s, all the cheers and hand-
clapoings last Saturday were for college boat-
crews. Parties of boys in colored flannel
coats trooped into the stalls to the plaudits
of their partisans among the gods, and when
the winding team arrived in their light blue
they got a reception that would have satisfied
Melba. These demonstrations in honor of
the boys were a welcome change to first-
night habitues. For once they were spared
the absurdity of the mummer-worship which
usually keeps the gallery girls busy before
the curtain goes up. If any actresses of con-
siderable self-importance came to get a recep-
tion last Saturday they must have been
shocked to find themselves overlooked. For
this occasion only, nobody up aloft was tx-y-
-ing to discover them before they sat down.

“Agricola” : English medicoes are drawing
attention to the low resistance of Australian
soldiers to septic invasion. I was in the
same dangerous state, but overcame it by
continued use of glycero-phospliate of lime,
which is the form in which we can best
assimilate phosphorus for the nerve cells.
It is of immense value to Australians.
N. S. Wales’s Win-the-War Govern-
ment has just taken the first step towards
bringing the conflict to an end. It has boi’-
rowed £3,000,000 in London—cost, about 6
per cent. Allegedly this is for “works.”
Actually the money is wanted to repair the
gaping holes in numerous loan and trust
accounts from which the previous Ministry
had been diverting heavy wads. It now re-
mains to be seen whether a Holman Minis-
try in a new suit is any more decent than
before; whether it will merely fill up the old
holes and proceed to make some new ones.
Meanwhile the spectacle of Holman and co.
winning the war by relieving J. Bull of cash
is a solemn circumstance calculated to im-
press the world with a sense of Australia’s
determination to cadge a way to victory.
A gallant, dashing bushman was Kelvin
Roach, 9th Light Horse Regt., who fell at
Gaza after two years’ campaigning. Trooper
Roach didn’t even wait for tlie clarion call
to arms. At the time war was declared he
was on Sid Kidman’s station, 900 miles up
from Adelaide, and learned the news from a
stray newspaper. He hastened south carry-
ing all his 22 years. Comrades declare that
for deeds of valor at Lone Pine alone he
more than once won the V.C.
“Three Stripes”: Looking like an escaped
Hun, Gunner R. W. Betts, of the 102nd
Howitzer Battery, arrived in London covered
with trench mud, unshaven and unwashed
for four days, but he showed he was a
white man by drawing his back pay—£loo—•
and pouring it into the Australian War
Loan. A mild-looking young man with a
Charlie Chaplin moustache, he has been 21
years helping to upset the Kaiser’s plans.
He was offered promotion for having brought
down the first Turkish aeroplane at Gal-
lipoli, but preferred to remain with his
mates. He is a nephew of vice-chairman
iSoyes, of Vic. Recruiting Committee.

IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Tooth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten

FROM STORKLESS REALMS.
MISTRESS (to applicant for position of
maid): “Do you know anything about
children?"
M AIN: “No, mum. Up to now I have only
been employed by society people.”
John Bull has been solemnly warned by
his Food Controller that the use of starch
must be restricted, and that one result of
this will be to “affect the wearing of starched
garments.” The Juggernaut of Fashion,
thank the Lord, rolls at last over those great-
est of all abominations in the realm of men’s
wear —the stiff-fronted shirt, the cuff and the
steel-ribbed collar. Once the British race
breaks off acquaintance with these outward
and visible signs of disaomfort and respecta-
bility it will no more return to them than to
chain armor. No man: ever lived who felt
comfortable in a concrete shirt, nor who
failed to thank Heaven when he dragged the
badge of servitude from under his whiskers.
All that is now necessary to cause every
mercer in Britain to write his stock down to
the price of old linen is for George V. to
attend church either without a shirt at all
or in one built on the commonsense plan
favored by navvies and golfers. Why the
starched and ironed rags have ever been
worn in a climate such as Australia lias is
one of the great, unfathomable mysteries of
social life.
There are signs of a change of heart in the
Vic. Education Department. When build-
ing the latest State school (at Noble Park,
near Dandenong) the administration
avoided the necessity for paying high prices
for Oregon and Baltic by the audacious device
of using Australian timbers throughout. The
Department may yet be found working at
Australian-made desks, stocked with Aus-
tralian paper, pens, ink and other once-
imported necessaries. Still, of course, it is
easier to have one’s leadpeneils made in
New York or Germany than to worry the
Federal Government about keeping its pre-
election promises to look after Australian in-
dustries.
* * * *
A staff-sergeant in Pharaoh’s late estate
swells the chorus of praise for the
Y.M.C.A. Billzac, he says, realises
that all, or nearly all he receives in
the way of recreation (not to mention pen-
nibs, writing-pads, envelopes, books, wrap-
ping-paper, string and good advice) comes
wholly from this beneficent institution, free
and for the asking. At first Bill was vaguely
suspicious of this “something-for-nothing”
policy, and he’d look furtively about for a
collection-plate; but that day is long past,
and he knows now that the only hint he’s
liable to get of “church” is when one of the
neatly-printed wall-notices—such as “Be the
kind of fellow your mother thinks you are”
-confronts him iu the middle of a “blanky”
and makes him'"feel that a “dash” would do
as well. Although, as a general thing, Bill
doesn’t go much on religion’s forms, yet the
practical Christianity of the “Y-emma” has
appealed so strongly to his sense of fair
dinkum, that when Armageddon is over Bill
will never forget the association which took
off its coat and got down to business what
time a score of other “movements” were
futilely arguing and wondering when and
where they’d better begin.

Sara Bernhardt’s motto is from the
Italian “Che Sara Sara.” ' What will be
will be, which she Frenchified as “Quand
Meme.” All the same, after more than half
a century of hard work, she must be taken as
a business woman all through. Had her
recent fever been real she would have per-
ished early, like her friend Aimee Desclde.
All Bernhardt’s eccentricities were for the
reclame, the advt. She did little more than
walk through her parts in Australia. So
the astonishing statements about her activity
may be discounted. It is told privately that
one Saturday in Melbourne she x’ehearsed all
the morning, played in the afternoon and
again at night. At midnight she went oft’
to De Castella’s St. Hubert's, in Melba’s
Lilydale district. There she put in the
Sunday, and is alleged to have shot a native
bear in a tree. (For goodness sake, don’t let
this reach Norman Lindsay’s ear.) Sara’s
scenery came from Paris, except that of
Theodora, painted by Hennings, who had
shown Ills virility with the scenery for
Miln’s “Antony and Cleopatra.” Nobody
could understand here how in Paris the
lobby would be thronged .by agitated men
in the entr’actes of her performance. How-
ever, that’s a secret.
• * * *
“Reinforcements”: Liverpool isn’t very
far from Sydney, and the opportunities for
losing letters addressed to soldiers who are
in camp there are not great; but. the
authorities make the most of them. Within
a fortnight of going into camp I had three
letters and one telegram lost for me. Then
after being hurriedly transferred to rein-
forcements and removed from “Siberia,”
where the recruits are kept, I got the glad
news that three letters had arrived for me —
at the place I had come from. I hopefully
awaited delivery. After a couple of days the
Recruits Depot orderly-room made up a
budget of letters addressed to transferred
men, sent them away, and that is the last
that is known about them. They may have
worn out and drooped to pieces or been
devoured by mice, but they have not turned
up at Battalion Headquarters, and the camp
post-office, when annealed to, looks blank
and scratches its ear.

“The Immature Male”: Over here iu
Pharaoh-land railway employees can’t very
well complain about the monotony of their
job. At a “station” on the Sinai Peninsula
the Gippo porters are trained to tackle any-
thing, from a “tank” to a tin of milk. This
is what I saw them take out of a local
“goods” : 22 camels, one mobile hospital and
accessories, two siege-guns, one dental chair,
50 mules, a truck-load of Christmas parcels
for Billjim, five field-kitchens, a brace of
staff-officefs (marked “fragile”), 2cwt of
canned peaches and the innards of an aero-
plane—The only heavenly thing in the con-
signment.

“All right, my dear. Tell me when you want

“Plumber”: Met my little Billy Anzae
again yesterday. He looked a trifle paler
than usual, and his arm was in a sling.
“Hullo, Billy; what’s up now ? Climbing
trees?” “No; nothin’! Just come back
from the doctor’s. Collarbone’s broken.”
“Is that it? How’d you do it, Billy?” “Th’
dog knocked me over last Saturday.” “What!
Four days ago? Why didn’t they get it set
before? Didn’t it hurt?” “A bit; but
Dad’s away and Mum had all the cows to
look after, so I didn’t sing out. Just went
and hammered th’ dog.”
* * * *
“S.T.S.” : The military and pensions people
both decline to wowse over the soldier’s lady
whose matrimonial status is uncertain.
About one who had been married in church
all right, but was alleged to have another
and previous husband somewhere in the
background, the Pensions Office assured me:
“At worst, sbe is an unmarried wife.” The
Military Allotment Office said: “At worst,
she could collect as an additional wife.” I
don’t know which term is the more delicate.
“Globe’s” complaint:—
The man on transport duty is treated as a rank
outsider. In England, the Commonwealth Bank,
London, is the only place where he can
exchange money free of commission, .whereas soldiers
have also two or three different offices to choose
from, and the English public look on Aus-
tralian notes and silver, without a 20 or 30
per cent, exchange, as expensive curios. At the
Agent-Generals’ offices transport is not recognised as
being on active service, so he receives none
of the invitations extended to soldier’s to
inspect the Royal Mews, Houses of Parlia-
ment and other sideshows. If in uniform,
which he has to supply himself, he is subject to
the same restrictions as soldiers in regard to place?
declared out of bounds to active-service men. Con-
cessions are given on the English railways to the
military; but not to the transport man unless he
happens to be a pommy. Then he gets a pass home
every six months. The transport man pays income
tax, yet to-day, when every soldier on this ship
recorded his vote for the Federal elections, no
arrangements were made for us. I myself,
enlisted with the Navy till the end of the war, but
had to sign an agreement which stated that the
service carried no pension rights. Transport workers
have little to say, and think less of what they are
doing for the country. Many have had their share
of war, a lot have been wounded and some killed,
but you never see their names in the lists of honor.
The motto is: No active-service privileges, and all
the muck.
THE PASSING OF THE CAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The mice are sporting gaily in the wheat-stacks and
the houses,
They frolic in their thousands, and the damage that
they do
A storm of indignation ’gainst the Government
arouses;
The pest grows greater daily, and the outlook’s truly
blue;
For the mice are bound to play
When the eats are all away—
Yes, the cats are all away.
The furriers have turned them to a purpose, which
was new,
Tabby, tortoise-shell and black,
They are sleeping on the back
Of rhe soldier’s smiling donah, or his pretty Sister
Sue.
1 never see a fur-coat but I tremble for the farmer,
And suffer sad misgivings re the fate of household
stores;
I never see a soldier out a-walking with his charmer
(Looking lovely in the garment which her very soul
adores),
But I heave a sigh and say—
"Oh, the cats are all away—
Black, tortoiseshell and grey.
Of the dead I’emains of mousers I ani holding a
review.”
The girls are nice and sweet,
But the mice who eat the wheat
Pray for gallant Billjim’s donah and his pretty
Sister Sue.
Myself am somewhat smitten by a sportive human
kitten,
Whose coat onte lived on mousers that have met
their Waterloo;
And when she talks of cheeses which the blithesome
mice have bitten,
I answer: “Little lady, it is all along o’ you;
For the mice come out to play
If the eats wear garments gay—
Becoming, warm and gay—
Being dyed to nicely match the gentle wearer's
native hue.
Ah, the badge of all your tribe
(Says this caustic singing scribe)
Is the catty coat you coveted, my little Sister Sue.
VICTORIA.

TAKING NO CHANCES.
H IM: “Go on; you don’t need a holiday.
You’re as fit as a fiddle.”
H ER: “Maybe, just now —but I’ll be played
out before I've convinced you that I
need a holiday.”
The egregious Douglas Sladen has pub-
lished a snobbish booklet concerning the
gilded British prisoners at the Ruhleben
camp in Germany. Sladen evidently intends
the following to make Jeames’s blood boil or
his flesh creep:—
Picture an earl—who has remained incognito, a
baronet, a leading Manchester solicitor, and a
banker—to take no further instances, finding them-
selves with only ten shillings a week to spend; sent
to bed at early hours under the absolute control of
a common guard. Picture them having to go bowl
in hand for their meals, like paupers to a soup
kitchen.
The thought of those distinguished internees
having to appear anything like paupers
reminds one forcibly that none of them ever
said a word, or did a thing, to prevent the
perpetuation of pauperism in England. Had
there been no paupers the harrowing com-
parison could never have been drawn.

“S.T.S.”: Tasmania must be fond of liti-
gation if it has run, as stated B. 19/4/T7,
an Equity suit in the Full Court to settle
that a soldier’s will was good though un-
witnessed. The page in each paybook headed
“Will” contains no instructions of any kind,
so about half of those that get filled in are
left unwitnessed. South Aus. has never made
any difficulty about admitting them to pro-
bate, though an affidavit as to the hand-
writing is of course required. . I have even
known two cases of an ordinary gossipy
letter from the trenches being accepted as a
will because it included a remark, “In case
I get knocked out, I want my insurance
money to be divided between” so-and-so. It
is S aid that will-making is to be declared a
compulsory military function prior to
embarkation.
“G.”: The never-ceasing swell around
North Keeling Island has told its tale on the
broken Emden. Abaft where the bridge
should be has been completely washed away,
and the forepart is high and dry at low tide.
Being firmly embedded in the coral shores
of the island, what is left of the wreck
should be visible for years. The steel
remains. On a dot miles away are the cable
and wireless stations, where the operators
wait for another naval fight to cheer them
up. I passed there a week ago, and they
•wirelessed pathetically: “Wish you could
come in. We’ve not seen a stranger for
months. What does fresh meat taste like?”
Jack Sommers: Here in smashed and
muddy Flanders we met a working herd
of the weird outcast brotherhood known as
the N.C.C.’s (Non-Combatant Corps). It is
their job in life to mend roads and endure
insults from the fighting men. Their entry
into the Australian zone was not marked by
many expressions of goodwill, but the
“boys,” as a rule, treated them with a
quiet contempt. (Other troops try to
roast ’em in their nuts.) They walk
to their work with a kind of wonder
in their mild, mostly-bespectacled, non-
combatant eyes, and are a queer breed
of pariahs, whose Tommie N.C.O.’s
don’t spare their feelings. Personally
I think they’ve got a devil of a lot of
pluck to stick it out. I’di sooner face
the whole Hun outfit myself.
If the principle of shifting a man
from police-court, to camp can be jus-
tilled, it seems to have
been so in a batch of
rail w a y prosecutions
lately half-heard in
Adelaide. The offenders
were conductors or port-
ers who had collected
small sums for excess
fares, and kept back
sundry coppers (to make
up for “shortages,” one
of them said). It was
theft, all right, but they
were all young, had all
lost their billets in con-
sequence of their be-
havior, and had there-
after enlisted. The
Bench thought that they
had learned their lesson,
and declined to register
a conviction, and sent
them along to -wipe out
the stain with blood
< Fritz’s) in the good
old melodrama way.

* * * *
From the oozy bottom of a lake in the
Melb. Exhibition Gardens, which was being
cleaned, someone picked up a medal awarded
in ’B6 to bowler J. A. Manning, of the Hawks-
burn C.C., and stolen 27 years ago. Stranger
is the history of the pedestal for the model
of the original Eight Hours monument.
This has been found under the stage of the
old Trades Hall, now in course of removal,
and model and pedestal will stand on the
table of the council chamber. The model,
which depicts a workman and the statue of
Liberty holding a torch, was designed by
Percival Ball, the sculptor. When the
pedestal in Spring-street, which carries the
present Eight Hours monument —-a crude
tallow-candle conception of granite—was
unveiled, the model was taken along to the
celebration and left, between drinks, in
Johnny Sheehan’s pub over the road. Here
it stood on the dining-table for years, until
removed by Ben Douglas, an old-time union-
ist. Eventually he gave the model to the
Trades Hall. "The pedestal had been placed
under the stage when the statuette was first
mislaid. Now that they are united, the
original design may be carried out. But it
will cost £3OO6 —a mere matter of detail
these days.

At South Yarra station, Melb., one after-
noon recently a small boy fell off the plat-
form as a train ran in, and apparently went
down under the wheels; but while . the
mother was putting up heartrending cries a
porter came from the other side and deposited
a whole boy on the asphalt. The kid had
landed oto his flat between the wheels, and
was not even scratched. Mamma grabbed
little Willie, she glared at him wildly, she
gave him a shake-up that nearly dislocated
his neck. “You little imp!” she cried,
(Shake, shake.) “You little devil!”
(Dust knocked out of him.) “Careless
young wretch !” ( Biff.. Shake.) “I’ll teach
you—falling under trains and spoiling your
clothes!”

"Brossie”: When you buy a packet of
chocolate at the troopship canteen and find
inside a friendly message from some gener-
ous Australian girl, you wonder how you
came to acquire such a pleasure in
such a plain commercial way. And
when you open your purchased tin
of tobacco and discover that another
nice Australian sister hopes you will
see her face in tiie smoke-wreaths, you ask
yourself why in thunder the canteeners don’t
search their stock for these incriminating
documents before they sell them to the men
who should got them for nothing! Many a
time w T hen leaning oyer the rail and trying
to puzzle it out, I have seen a box go over-
board, hit the sea a resounding smack, and
read as it drifted astern the label, “Comforts
for Soldiers.”
Those returned soldiers whom a patriotic
Government is putting on five-acre blocks of
market-garden land round Melbourne will be
returning their little holdings in clods when
they find out a little more about the job.
Consider the Moorabbin and Burwood gar
deners on their 20-acre blocks this season.
The latter lot planted acres of tomatoes, and
reaped about a case to the acre. Some rue-
fully ploughing them in. Moorabbin got a
half-crop—at Bs. to 10s. a case. To-day
some Burwood men are taking in three 60-
dozen loads of “caulis” a week and getting
ss. to Ss. a dozen for them, while poor Moo-
rabbin, which can’t grow “caulis," drags up
cabbages and has to be thankful to get rid of
them at 2d. a dozen. Turk’s heads and
pumpkins are lying under the hedges wait-
ing for a rise and rotting. They bring 6d.
a dozen ; yet the lady who cooks them pays
6d. for one decent Turk in the cheapest
suburban shop. That’s where the main
trouble lies, the middleman — and the tongue-
in-cheek politician.
South Australia absolutely refuses to
listen to the bark of the sectarian. There
was a “rally” in the Exhibition Building at
Adelaide lately, to show that Australia is by
some unspecified means, getting into the
clutch of Borne. (S.A. has a smaller R.C.
proportion of population than any other
State except Tasmania.) The opposition
mustered in some force and asked the
speakers awkward questions, but there was
no more excitement than at an ordinary poli-
tical meeting. The minority finally called
for and gave three cheers for the Pope. And
even then no heads were broken!
“Bad Bill”: Here’s an order issued by
A.I.F. Headquarters in Horseferry-road :
The strictest discipline is to be maintained between
the A.I.F. and the Civilian Staff. This covers both
the period of office hours and afterwards. No com-
munication should take place between the two sets
of staff, other than that demanded by the exigencies
of the service. 1! this instruction is not strictly
carried out, arrangements will be made to place all
the A.I.F. Staff in barracks. Individual failure to
observe the above rule will be followed by the
soldier being returned to Tidworth.
This is how T the joke works out. When I
came here (London) from Gallip. I met my
Doreen and we got hitched, but as I can
get no separation allowance for her because
we did not get spliced in Australia, she
works at ’Eadquarters with me. Now 7 they
tell me I’m not to speak to my own missus!

THE POETS TRUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The poet wrote in measure fine
Of sonorous and honeyed line
His love for Joan, the small and sweet,
VVho’d come on little golden feet.
And in her rose-leaf hands, he swore,
Imprisoned him for evermore.
He sang of Joan's brown eyes, whose fire
Gave warmth like wine. His vibrant lyre
Lent music to her hair and stress
Of measured grace and tunefulness
To ears and lips’ deep corners where
Were neetared sweets and essence rare.
Joan’s wee, brave, lilied breasts he sang,
And at his touch a psean rang
In honor of her waist so slim,
The fondling turn of every limb,
Her neck, like suave, old ivory,
Where ’neath his lip her life ran free.
He sang a land of bliss alone
Where he might bide with love and Joan.
All raptures dear she brought to him
Of devils and of seraphim.
This wondrous song he sold next day,
And bought a stole of furs for May!
VICTORIA.
E. DYSON.

REFLECTIONS.


MAKES COUGH SYRUP WITH TAR
AND SUGAR.
A CHEMIST’S RECIPE.
“As a chemist, I have, of course, long
been familiar with the virtue of bitrate of
tar in the treatment of coughs, colds,
catarrh and bronchial troubles; but did not
give it any thought until one day my wife
contracted a severe cough which refused to
yield to all the usual remedies from which
she sought relief. It then occurred to me
that bitrate of tar might be just the thing,
and I decided to make an experiment with
its use. I made a plain syrup by dissolving
half a pound of sugar in half a pint of hot
water, and then stirred in 2 ounces of bitrate
of tar. This made nearly a pint of a pleasant
cough syrup, of which I told my wife to
take two teaspoonfuls every hour. By the
end of the first day, much to her delight as
well as my own, the cough had disappeared
and has not returned. I was so pleased that
now whenever anyone asks for a cough syrup
I advise the use of this simple home-made
syrup, for honestly believe it is one of the
best for coughs, colds, catarrh and bronchial
troubles.” Bitrate of tar, as is W-ell known,
possesses peculiar healing and soothing
properties: and owing to the great demand
for it all leading chemists have made special
arrangements to keep a liberal supply in
stock. — Apvt.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Secretary Henry Wellsteed Apperly suc-
ceeds veteran Richard Teece as manager of
the A.M.P. after 42 years’ service with the
big society. Born in Melbourne in 1861 he
came to Sydney when a boy with his parents
and was educated at the Fort-street and
Grammar Schools. He joined the Society
as a junior articled clerk in 1875. In 18S5
he became accountant of the Adelaide branch
and six months later was promoted to Mel-
bourne. Four years later he was in charge
of the actuarial computing staff at the head
office (Sydney), and soon afterwards became
accountant. He was the first manager of
the Society’s new industrial branch (1904) ;
first Resident Secretary of the London
branch (1908) ; and succeeded the late R.
B. Cameron as secretary in 1910. When he
had nothing to do he was Queensland’s chess
champion.

The name of Richard Davies Hanson, a
long-ago Chief Justice of South Aus.., was
recalled last week when his only son died in
Adelaide, after a long and useful but quiet
life in the civil service. The third Richard
Davies Hanson is now in the trenches.
Among recent investitures of the Military
Cross at Buckingham Palace was that of
Major Percy W. Woods, a N. 8. Wales
fighter. He was company-sergeant-major of
the 3rd Battalion, and gained his first star
two years ago—just after Lone Pine. A
lieutenant three months later, he received a
captaincy in the 55th Battalion in March,
1916, and was later made adjutant. Now he
has the crown at 32.
“Seguidor”: Some months ago The
Bulletin gave a par. to the earlier adven-
tures of a Richmond (Vic.), lad, by name
Francis Luksehewitz, otherwise Luks, whose
comrade was the wind that tramps the world.
A wanderer since boyhood, he had the ambi-
tion to serve the Allies as an aviator.
Turned down in England, he went to Russia,
but was imprisoned as a spy, and came home
A WOMAN'S WAY.
“But. I thought your wife had forgiven you
and promised to forget it?"
“So she has, old man. But she didn't promise
to let me forget she’d forgiven me.”
in disgust after gaining his freedom. Soon
he left again for London, and his mother
heard that efforts were being made by Aus-
tralians in England to get him into the Fly-
ing Corps. Then “silence for a while.” Now
she has received a French field postcard in
which her son tells her that he is in the
Foreign Legion, and has won a decoration.
He is still going strong —-unlike his only
brother, Bob, who, leaving Australia with
the Bridging Train, had the bad luck to get
his foot crushed in Egypt, where he is still
in hospital.
William Hamilton writes: —
In B. 19/4/’l7 the honor was given to Private L.
Keyzor for being the first V.C. winner in N. S. Wales.
My son, John Hamilton, was a member of the same
battalion as Keyzor and gained the great distinction
at Lone Pine. Private John Hamilton is the first
native-born V.C. winner for N. S. Wales. He was
born in Orange. I was born in Canterbury
(N.S.W.), and my father first saw light on the
I-lawkesbury. You can see that the boy is the true
merino. Keyzor is an Englishman.
Some surviving ex-S.. Aus. Parliamen-
tarians over 80 and thereabouts: H. B. T.
Strangways (85, residing in Eng.), Charles
Goode (90 this month, Adelaide’s oldest
merchant), Robt. A. Tarlton (89, residing
in S. Africa), Ebenezer Ward (now in
West Aus.), philanthropist Edwin T. Smith
(in his 88th), John Cheriton, John Bosworth,
William Gilbert (in his 89th), Alfred Catt
and Simpson New!and (who wrote “Paving
the Way”).
“Dryberra” : Major Price. J. Morgan, dead
of. wounds in France, was more familiarly
and affectionately known at Duntroon as
“Mary” Morgan, where he was one of the
best all-round athletes of the College. His
particular Duntroon pal, Capt. W. R. Hodg-
son, also of the Artillery, was badly wounded
in the Landing on April 25. That day he
won the Croix de Guerre. Few of the first
batch from Duntroon got away from Gal-
lipoli safely, and the. death toll was heavy.
Two of them, Boyle (the youngest of the
first batch and a captain at 20) and Cap-
tain Capes, are back at the College on the
instructional staff.
“Musketoon”: Dr. Florence, a Coota-
mundra (N.S.W.) medico, addressed a re-
cruiting meeting at that town the other
night, and got no response to his appeal.
This is how he told the crayfish what he
thought of them: “Well, be d —d to you!
Stay where you are! If you get sick don’t
sent for me to help you; go elsewhere, and
be d —to you.” The doctor’s wife, who
was a Miss Pinkstone, has four brothers in
khaki. Another is in a soldier’s grave at Gal-
lipoli. Her father is Fred Pinkstone, who
ran the Coota Herald for many years.
Lt.-Col. W. K. Bolton, the new Victorian
soldier Senator, who, before he joined the
win-the-war crowd on the hustings, had ex-
perience of the real thing in Gallipoli, is
the father of a family which carries a punch.
Dad is 54, and was in charge of the Bth
Battalion. His eldest son, Capt. John G.,
was with his parent at Anzac and is now
in France. He has had a varied career as
an engineer in the U.S. and South America,
having boiled his billy on the Andes. Ser-
geant Hammersley G. is with the Canucks,
having donned the maple-leaf as a private.
He was at Pozieres and the Somme, and
carries three wounds. Lieut. .Moore Hunter
Bolton, electric-tram engineer, was twice
wounded in ' Gallipoli, where his stay ex-
tended from the landing to the Cape Helles
fighting. Sister Ethel May. now of Melb.
Base Hospital, has nursed Billjims in Egypt.
Everyone else in the family is too young
for active service.
D. B. "Fry, killed in action with the A.1.F.,
was for six years on the scientific staff of
Sydney Museum, and at the outbreak of war
he was taking a science course at the
Uni., and acting as demonstrator in zoology
under Prof. Haswell. Fry first wore khaki
in the A.M<C., and made two voyages
in hospital ships as orderly. Then he
got a transfer to the infantry, studied
at Duntroon, and saluted as a lieu-
tenant; but there being no room in
that rank he marched as a sergeant.
At Salisbury Plains (Eng.) he fo.und
that even the sergeant’s stripes must
go if he wanted to get to the Front in
a hurry; so off they came, and this
good Australian joined the other good
Australians in the ranks. A younger
brother, also in the A.1.F., was killed in
August last year.
A young man with a name well-known in
Victoria was killed at Bapaume in March.
This was Private Charles Neild, sometime an
architect employed by the Water Supply
Commission. He was a son of that Dr.
Neild who was for so many years City
Coroner and widely-known literary and
theatrical critic. Young Neild went through
Pozieres, only to meet his death later on by
the explosion of a German trap while he
was helping to pass comforts around among
his fellow-Billjims. *
William Addis, one-time assistant minister
at Charles Strong’s church in Melbourne,
died in London recently. All his life he was
a sufferer from religions itch, a painful spiri-
tual complaint. Addis was born into the
Free Church of Scotland, his dad being a
meenister thereof. As a youth he turned to
Church of England, but later he took up
Roman Catholicism and became a priest
under Cardinal Manning’s wing. He stayed
in the R.C. Church for 10 years, and finally
he was ordained in the Anglican Church.
EDMUND JOWETT, Nationalist candidate
for Maribyrnong (Vic.).
Hard upon the heels of the news that
Flight Lieutenant Reggie Baillieu, big
“Bill’s” nephew, had won the Military Cross,
came the announcement that the feat had
been imitated by William the Great’s second
son, Harry L. This iad, who is 25, enlisted
in Blighty, passed as an officer, and got his
star in the Eighth Artillery Brigade, B.E.F.
Then he got to business in France, where
lie recently acquired a slight wound. He is
an old Melbourne boy, and rowed for two
years in the Public Schools’ Race.

“N. or M.’s” farewell: —
Dead at Hobart, aged 09, W. H. Burgess,
who for many years past has had a finger in most
ot' the Speck’s pies. lie, started in his father’s
grocery store, and as head of the firm of Burgess
and Bovutt continued the business until he found a
wider outlet in wine and spirits and general mer-
chandise. In ’Bl he won the West Hobart seat in
the Assembly, was “Colonial Treasurer” in Adyt
Douglas’s Ministry from ’B4 .to ’B6, and again under
Philip Fysh, after which he retired (though still
remaining on the Executive) until last year, when,
urged by the Libs., he stood for Denison, was re-
turned by a big majority, and proved a frank and
level-headed critic of his party’s financial policy.
He was an alderman from ’76 to ’Bl, mayor from
’79 to ’BO, Warden of the Marine Board and Master
Warden. He was a moving spirit in the construc-
tion of Hobart’s Ocean Pier, which is the biggest
of its kind in the world. For a number of year?
before his death he was Consul for France. He
had the bitter experience of being one of the direc-
tors -of the ill-fated V.D.L. Bank; was one of the
first to identify himself with the volunteer movement
and served in the forces as captain and adjutant o)
Artillery; member of the Royal Society of Tas.; was
prominent in the Chamber of Commerce; keer
on sport, football and cricket, but more particularly
rowing; and gave active help to various association l
and clubs. Finally he was a fine, broad-minded man.
“Third Turn”: Moustached like one oi
Napoleon’s field-marshals, and with the court
liuess of an old-time Irish gentleman, Hugl:
Toohey, who died at Abbotsford (Vic.)
leaves a gap in the ranks of the Legislative
Assembly’s general division staff which none
of the younger brigade can fill. He hac
been 3S years in the service, and was nearly
TO years of age. He was a privileged re
tainer, and a member was not really i
member until Toohey had shaken him by
the hand and welcomed him to the Assembly
Fui’ther details of the surprising adven
tures of Private A. Sast, a Russian-bori
soldier of. the famous 10th (South Austra
lian) Battalion, show that his strenuous
entry into Gallipoli was the prelude to i
knockabout turn which took him all over thi
Turkish Empire. In the front rank at tin
Landing, he was taken with a mixed crowc
of prisoners by way of Maidos to Constan
tinople, and was eventually sent to Rustchuk
on the Danube. His Bulgar guards made th<
error of overlooking his body belt, whict
held 20 good Australian sovereigns. Witt
some of these he subsidised an escape, f
Bulgar agreeing to take him across intc
Roumania. Finally he reached Moscow
where the British authorities picked him ir
and sent him to England.

THE BULLDOGGERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
M. Andre Tudsq, Lb Journal’s correspondent at
the British headquarters, refers to the Anzacs’ mettle
and Canada’s magnificent tenacity. Both have estab-
lished the following law: “Wherever we are installed
in -the trenches we don’t give way an inch.”
Now this is the creed of the Anzac men,
The men with the hearts of gold :
“What we win from the foe, by the steel and gun,
By the steel and gun we hold.
“From the heights afar, and the sky above,
There may come the hail of death,
But we yield no yard of the ground we’ve won,
Till the last man yields his breath.
“We are few, who should have been many, here,
And our ranks are thinning fast,
But, by the Christ who died, for each boy who falls
We will take toll to the last.
“We are fighting now for the folks at home,
For the land from which we came;
And we’re hanging on, and we’re fighting hard,
And we’re dying hard and game. ,
“There are long, quiet nights for our gallant mates
Who have fought and who, fighting,' fell;
But, for every one who has dropped his gun,
There’s a fresh Hun face in Hell.”
Ayel This is-the creed of the Anzac men,
The men with the hearts of gold:
“What we’ve won from the Hun by the steel and gun
By the Lord our God we’ll hold.”
N.S.W.
JOHN TAYLOR.

Yes, and why not? Why shouldn’t the
profits for the past half-year be the largest
on record except for a couple of specially
fruitful terms before the war? Look at
the balance-sheet figures as at March 31: —
1914. 1916. 1917.
Notes in circulation .. L 1,103 £B9l £BBO
Bills in circulation, etc. 59,322 197,864 262,583
Deposits at interest ..1,491,800 1,559,>86 1,547,298
Free deposits . . . . 833,490 999,303 1,123,947
Liquid assets .. ..1,261,233 1,178,962 1,477,911
Advances .. ..1,590,682 2.065,633 1,963,483
Premises . . • • 62,965 85,050 83,470
Thus since the beginning of tlie war this
bank has got £548,993 more of the public’s
money; that is to say, in these war years
the people have added 23 p.c. to their
deposits, etc., in this bank.
And this bank has merely had the com-
mon experience. The N. S. Wales banking
averages, in fact, show that during the
three years the total increase in deposits has
been as much as 28 p.c. Now, most of it is
current account money: the depositor has
merely left it for safe custody. In the
Royal the deposits at interest account for
only £55,498 of the total increase of
£548,993. And that £55,498 is all Govern-
ment money. In N. S. Wales the averages
show an increase of £15,092,000 in tne
current account money and only £2,198,000
in the deposits at interest. So it is mostly
free money which is piling up in the banks;
and, though the banks can’t use all of it,
they can use some of it. Thus _the Royal,
though it is paying for only £55,498 more
fixed deposits, has increased its advances by
£372-,801 and its other revenue-earning assets
by about £lOO,OOO. So it ought to be mak-
ing more money than it did in 1913, even
though the Commonwealth Bank is cutting
into the exchange business, and notwith-
standing the larger taxes and other charges.
In fact, in view of. the much higher
charges for accommodation, this pape.r will
be much surprised if this and other banks
couldn’t show larger profits than they are
doing, but very wisely prefer to put a bit
up their sleeves for contingencies. And
there are contingencies all right—in a world-
upheaval of this sort nobody can foresee
what will happen. Further, as soon as the
war is over there will be a steady demand .
on these large current account balances, and
that will cut out a considerable source of
present revenue. Whether there will be,
on the other hand, any conditions to swell
profits, is not clear. Nobody suspected that
there would be anything like a trade boom
at the end of three years of war. But the
lavish employment of the national credit
lias, produced one. There is no telling what
the national credit may not do after the war.

S. Australia is moving very cautiously. It
isn’t living in such an atmosphere of boom
as Sydney imd Melbourne, Sydney in parti-
cular. For , one thing it doesn t produce
very much of the goods that are putting war
profits into some Australian pockets. Though
both N. S. Wales and Victoria produce more
wheat, as a rule, no State grows anything
like so much per head of population. Even
in the year of its most colossal wheat har-
vest —1915-6 —N. S. Wales produced only
36 bushels per head, and that was more than
double the average, whereas S. Aus. has
averaged 47 bushels per head for the past
seven years, including the year of the awful
failure (1914-5). Of wool, its clip averages
less than one-sixth of that of N. S. Wales
and approximately one-half of that of "V ic-
toria'; and it isn’t well situated for the meat-
export trade, mostly for the very reason
that it is well situated for the business of
growing and exporting wheat. Now, this
isn’t a good war for the wheat-grower. The
submarine and the raider have made large
holes in shipping. For that and other reasons
(one of them the greed of shipowners)
freights have been and still are abnormal;
and that hits the wheat-grower and the
wheat-growing State much harder than the
wool-grower or the meat-exporter, because it
costs almost as much to ship a ton of wheat,
worth to the grower fd. per lb., as a ton
of wool, worth 2s. per lb. Partly on that
account, and partly because of the enor-
mously greater expenditure of Defence
Department money in Sydney than in Ade-
laide, the deposits in banks in S. Australia
have increased only from £10.812,543 to
£11.168.209 in the past two years, while in
N. S. Wales the growth has been from
£62,537.000 to £77,531.000. No wonder,
then, that Adelaide and Westralia find it
hard to understand a good many of the
gibes at the Win the War patriots of the
Eastern States!
This Bank of Adelaide is for the afore-
named reasons showing none of that remark-
able expansion which some N. S. Wales and
Victorian institutions exhibit. Actually in
the past two years it has done no more than
might have been expected after any two
years of normal prosperity, > or perhaps
hardly as much. Compared with the posi-
tion just prior to the war most of the figures
are actually less.' Here are the figures:—
1915. 1916. 1917.
Notes in circulation £ll,OOB £9,700 £8,517
Bills, etc 270,436 184,316 '. 270,989
Deposits .. •• 3,486,027 3,459,742 8^759,455
Assets.
Liquid assets .. 2,126,062 1,566,932 2,302,632
Advances .. . . 2,589,386 3,043,900 2,710,842
Premises . . . . 115,209 . 115,923 114,658
The most striking feature of the past year’s
accounts is an increase of deposits coupled
with a reduction of advances, which of course
means between them an increase of liquid
assets. But the changes are not quite what
they seem, because 12 months ago this bank
was helping the Commonwealth Government
to finance the wheat pool. So far as ordinary
advances are concerned the bank has let its
customers have another £270,000. But, even
so, money has been piling up, and no less
than £530,000 was on short call with other
banks on March 31, as compared with
£65,000 12 months previously. Either S. Aus-
tralia can see few likely investments at the
present time, or the Bank is not disposed to
do much for borrowers until the clouds clear.
In those circumstances, considering its heavy
holding of liquid assets —equal to no less
than 56 p.c. of all its liabilities—it shows a
fair profit. Not a large one, by any means—
rather less than 5i p c. on the capital and
reserves combined. But that, again, is the
S. Australian way. It wants good interest if
good interest can be got; but more than all
it doesn’t want to lose its principal. Which
is one reason why S. Aus. has £2l 13d. 6d.
per head in the Savings Bank, while the
Commonwealth average is only £lB 10s.

This co. is an amalgamation of interests in
the wool and pastoral produce business —the
old still represented on the Board by manag-
ing-director G. W. Bagot; the new by the
wool managers, Geo. Dowling and Geo. Jef-
frey. With John Lewis and Harold Hughes
and other remarkably well-informed S. Aus-
tralian and West Darling sheep and cattle
men on the Board, and with accountant
W. L. Ware as chairman, the co. ought to
find it difficult to make mistakes, even in so
precarious a business as pastoral finance.
For many years it was content to creep.
Then it realised that it was not getting over
all the ground there was to cover, and the
next table shows what happened :
1908. 1915. 1916. 1917.
Capital .. £75,000 £156,250 £156,250 £156,250
Reserves .. 26,515 67,333 76,192 69,419

and cash . . 328,259 287,027 411,624 334,803

and stock .. 1 100,a33 , 1i350 u,350 Nil

In fact, even a bit more progress than that
was made, because a subsidiary trustee co.
was formed three or four years ago to look
after the dead man’s money—and there are
very nice pickings in that, especially when
the money is in pastoral property. During
the past year the advances have been reduced
and the co.’s own debts have been relieved
to correspond. This no doubt has been due
to the freer market for wool. Incidentally,
high prices have given the co. another splen-
did year for commission earnings; and the
reserves are now so substantial that the
directors have wiped out the last of the
goodwill, leaving all the capital and reserves
represented by substantial assets —as sub-
stantial, anyway, as advances on station
properties can be. How much is represented
by those advances, how much by advances
against produce in hand, and how much is
cash on hand cannot be told from -the pub-
lished accounts, which are in this respect
defective. Also, on the other side, the lia-
bilities are lumped—“ Sundry creditors in-
cluding fixed deposits and all other liabili-
ties,” the item runs. The directors have,
however, been long enough in the business
to know how dangerous it is to finance dry-
country pastoralists on borrowed money
which is liable to be called for in a hurry.
Still, that is something for the investor to
assure himself about. Three-fourths of all
the money that has been lost by pastoral
finance coS. in Australia has been lost simply
because the cos. haven’t been able to wait
for the country to recover after a drought.

And if anybody says that the co. has made
more than that during the past year or two,
and has put the surplus away into a contin-
gency fund, this paper will not be able to
bring any evidence to the contrary. It cer-
tainly will not argue that there is no need to
have a contingency fund. The ccp, like a
good many more, has made large profits by
selling at war prices stuff bought at pre-war
prices; but, then, it has been buying more
stuff at war prices, and some of it probably
will not be sold until the war is over. Then,
in some lines especially, there is going to be
a smash. For bulky goods there may be for
a couple of years a difficulty in getting ship-
ping space ; and for other goods—metals, for
instance—high values are likely to last while
the world is patching itself. But in chemi-
cals there will probably be a rapid fall in
values in Australia as well as in Europe;
and if Washington (Soul’s are providing for
the contingency, it is no more than one
would expect of a co. so prudently managed.
The co. also handles many sidelines that are
more or less luxuries; and, unless the
entirely unexpected happens, the community
will before long look twice at tuppence
before spending it on these things. Maybe
the co. has extended its operations a little;
yet it seems more likely that the profit shown
six or seven years ago was more nearly nor-
mal than that of the period since then.
How extremely profitable the business has
been the dividends show: and the next table
merely supports that conclusion :-

All the money that has been got from share-
holders during the 10 years is £30,000. To
that was added £BO.OOO transferred from
reserves, the two sums together carrying
capital up to the present £lBO,OOO. Thus
in 10 years over £45,000 has been, added
to assets apart from stock (most of this
is freehold premises), and nearly £50,000
has been added to stock (book value). Mean-
time a dividend averaging nearly 20 p.c. on
the actually-paid capital has been enjoyed.
There are not many cos. anywhere in this
line of business that can show such results:
generally the chemist’s trade gives little
more than bread and butter on account of
the small turnover.

Some of the figures in the last line are esti-
mates. In the first three quarters of the
year £14,7G7 was paid away as bonus on
trade; and a round £5OOO is added to that,
as a guess, for the last quarter. In the nine
months the dividends amounted to £13,880.
The capital was lately increased to £52,207
by the issue of 101,530 shares at 55., making
the total 210,126. The dividend for the
last quarter is 7d. per share, which, allowing
a pound or two for unpaid instalments,
would, if it were paid oq the lot, be £6125.
Apparently the dividend was at the rate of
about 70 p.c. p.a. for the first nine months
and 47 p.c. p.a. for the last quarter. But the
effective return is much less than that,
because, as the table shows, the premium
on the early issues of shares averaged about
13s. 6d., so that the dividend on the money
actually paid to the co. for shares was only
about 20 p.c. Approximately, in accordance
with the scheme on which the co. was formed
—mostly among a few big hotel-keepers in
Melbourne —an equal amount has been
divided as bonus to purchasers.
But though a big premium has been got
on most of the shares issued up to a few
months ago, the business demanded a lot
more capital than shareholders had provided;
and apparently the bank wasn't prepared to
go on indefinitely. After a deal of trouble the
Treasury was persuaded to sanction the issue
of more shares; and the £25,000-odd thus
raised has been put into premises and plant,
along with much borrowed money. The
following table shoots how much has been
spent: —

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per' cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.:
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers. — Advt. [2

*Subject to remarks hereunder.
This co. is doing the sensible thing nowadays,
and is writing down its profits to cover depre-
ciation. -Coal-mining cos. have themselves
to thank for some of their labor troubles, for
they have persistently over-stated their true
profits. This co., for instance, used to show
the excess of revenue over expenditure as
profit, quite overlooking the fact that all the
time it was using up its capital in the shape
of the coal in its mine. The time came when
its mine was practically worked out, and
all it had to represent its £105,000 was a
good plant and various large holes with a
little coal around some of them. Fortu-
nately; before it was quite too late it bought
another coal area and developed a second
mine, the Neath, at remarkably little ex-
pense; and latterly it lias been opening up
still another property adjoining the Bellbird
and Aberdare. This mine, in which a very
fine seam has been proved, should be putting
out coal by the end of the year.
In the past term the co. has been hit by
the common troubles, and the net profits
have not sufficed to pay the dividend. Still,
the cash position is no worse, notwithstand-
ing the money that has been spent on opening
up the new mine. This gives a bird’s-eye
view of (1) the past term and (2) the past
10 years:—
Mar., ’O7. Sept., ’l6. Mar., ’l7.
Liabilities .. £17,423 £4,398 £4,385
Plant, stock, etc. 22,424 33,086 32,786
Liquid assets .. 644 39,340 40,316
That illustrates what was said up above
about the new policy of the co. —the policy
of saving up revenue to repay capital when
the mines are exhausted. In the past 10
years something like £53,000 has been thus
put by; and meanwhile £lO,OOO net has been
put into plant, etc., oL which only about
£4OOO lias been provided by shareholders. It
is an entirely satisfactory record.
* * * ' *
“Quidam” : When is a start to be made to
consolidate the mining laws of N. S. Wales?
They are merely a collection of archaic
ideas which pertain to the early allu-
vial days, to which has been tacked
multitudinous alterations, additions and
improvements of various governments and
administrations ever since. That they are
quite unsuitable, for the present day
mining industry has been admitted by vari-
ous politicians times out of number. One
of the reproaches to the Labor regime _in
N. S. Wales has been in the fact that during
its existence nothing to improve the condi-
tion of the prospector and mine-developer
was attempted. The backbone of the
mining industry—the legitimate, independ-
ent worker, who does the discovering,
pioneering and developing of the business —
being naturally a roving and disorganised
sort of person, got practically no improved
conditions. This is the more reprehensible
in that the miner has always been a staunch
democrat, and has been a material factor in
the evolution of Labor in politics. Instead
of receiving in return for his sturdy adher-
ence a serviceable, simple and equitable code
of mining laws such as Queensland has had
for many years, he has only had several
trivial and valueless additions to the awful
hotch-potch handed down from the early
days when almost all conditions were differ-
ent. At present, delay, inefficiency and ex-
pense appear to be the watchwords of the
department that has to administer the
weird collection, and the needs and griev-
ances of the legitimate miner are nobody’s
business. Acts and amending acts and
regulations galore, and the general basic
principle that trivial formalities and übiqui-
tous fees should over-ride all broad lines of
equity and effectiveness make our mining
laws a burden and a snare to the hard-work-
ing and often slow-thinking prospector and
developer, and a gracious boon to the subtle
and quick-witted shark.
* * *
The absence of exciting news from the
vicinity of the sensational gold find at Dark
Corner (N.S.W.) for some time has led
many sceptics to conclude that the show has
petered out, blown up, evaporated, or some-
thing of the sort. Such conjectures, how-
ever, are wrong; the show being, for reasons
best known to the finder and the person who
obtained an option over it, very much m the
same condition as when innumerable people
dashed to the locality in motor-cars and other
conveyances and pegged out leases over a
large tract of country. Machinery to the
extent of a lih.p. benzine engine has recently
been installed, to enable the water which
prevents sinking to be wrestled with, and in
due time the holders of adjacent areas will
probably be able to ascertain whether the
rich specimen stuff goes down or whether it
doesn't. Still, the installation of such
machinery at this juncture would appear
a debatable matter, especially when one
has enough specimen stuff to pay tor
better things. It is, however, cheerful to
know that if the show has not so tar been
proved to be better than anticipated, it cer-
tainly has not been proved to be worse.

tors, £5098; cash, £3504; and stores, £2790.
The co.’s capital is £174,600. Plant consists
of four dredges, two the property of the Para-
cale co., in which Philippine Dredges holds

siderably reduce its debit balance, besides
writing off £7045, commissions and discounts
connected with last year’s debenture float.

“Oof Bird” : Years ago an expert wrote a
book on mines and devoted a chapter to the
choosing of managers. Among other things
he said that a man with a good record,
especially a trained mining-engineer, could
not be secured for the small salary which
ignorant bodies of directors insist on offer-
ing their managers ; and the' natural conse-
quence was that shareholders fell in. After
a good look around the smaller mines in
Westralia I agree with him. Quite a number
of mine managers I have met have no right
whatever to hold such a position. Some are
inexperienced and incapable, and in many
instances owe their appointment solely to
the influence of the controlling market
clique. I do not know one who is a
thoroughly-trained mining-engineer. Mostly
these managers do everything their direc-
tors want, despite the fact that the
man who is strong enough to make him-
self heard beyond the walls of the Board-
room when asked to do anything which does
not meet with his approval, or is not in the
best interests of the co., comes out on top
in the long run.
The Northampton (W.A.) lead and copper
field has been stimulated into life again by
high prices. Since 1845, when the first lot
of ore was sent away, upwards of 8500 tons
of copper and 34.000 tons of lead ore have
been exported from the locality. The
busiest time'was in the ’7o’s and early ’Bo’s,
after which production rapidly decreased on
account of the low price of metals. In 1893
production practically ceased. Latterly the
Fremantle Trading Co. has been spending
money on two of the principal mines on the
field—the Baddera and Narra Tarra —rand
seems to be doing fairly well. A number of
small leaseholders are also reported to be
working at a profit.
“Only fit for a narty of six men, and not
big enough for a c 0.,” is how a candid
director described Yilgarn Consuls at the
recent shareholders’ meeting. But there are
other Westralian shows in a far worse plight,
due to the fact that those in control are
guided not by their interest in the mine,
but by the interest which the dominating
clique has in the share market. Thus share-
holders are kept in tlie dark until collapse is
inevitable. In regard to the Yilgarn
Consuls, The Bulletin pointed out clearly
at the time of flotation that its prospects
were very poor.
* * * *
Fremantle Trading Co. (W.A.;, alias Fre-
mantle Smelters, shows a credit balance of
£3GOS for last financial year. Divs. of 10
p.c. on preference and 3 p.c. on ordinary
shares were paid. The co. derives most of
its revenue from treating Northampton lead
concentrates, and when a Bulletin man
passed through a few weeks ago it had about
£20,000 worth of lead awaiting shipment to
England.
* * * *
The Chamber of Mines delegate told the
recent Kalgoorlie mining conference that
gold-thieving is still rampant in Westralia.
Years ago the whole matter was probed by
a Royal Commission, but up to the present
practically none of its recommendations has
been given legislative effect. The Chamber
appealed to the Minister to take effective
measures to suppress the evil.
-X- * * *
Westralia’s mineral production other than
gold for the first quarter of this year was
worth approximately £61,879. Copper, tin,
coal and lead accounted for nearly the whole
of it. Total production to date is valued
at £4,171,576, of which more than one-half
is represented by copper and tin, with silver-
lead ore a good third and coal close up.
* * * *
Hampden-Cloncurry (Q.) is developing a
group of low-grade mines at Pindora as a
concentrating proposition, and the experi-
mental work at the mill has given encourag-
ing results. There are eight shafts at Pin-
dora along a lode of 455 ft. At the 300 ft.
level from No. 6 shaft the north drive is in
127 ft., with 42in. of ore worth 4 p.c. in the
face; at No. 8 shaft the drive south is in
126 ft., the face carrying 55in. of ore of 2 p.c.
value. There are 100,000 tons of low-grade
concentrating ore in the reserves, but this
isn’t really a large quantity for a co. with a
capital of £350,000, for at an average extrac-
tion of 3 p.c. per ton there are only 3000
tons of copper available. Still, most of this
co.’s mines are shallow, the deepest level, for
instance, at the MacGregor, is only 320 ft.,
and the Trekelano hasn’t opened its 400 ft.
level yet. The Duchess (1000 ft.) and Hamp-
den (500 ft.) are the two deepest properties.
# # # ♦
Maoriland’s Waihi mine hands out a shil-
ling dividend (£24,795) on .Tune 1, raising
the total payments to £4,929,341. The gross
profit for 1916 was £174,000, out of which
the co. paid £127,000 in quarterly dividends
and taxes; £20,000 was written off for depre-
ciation, and £4OOO for hydro-electric power
work. The April return was £26,533 from
15,220 tons, so the Waihi is becoming low-
grade. The co, has produced a large quan-
tity of silver as well as gold, and in that
respect is quite different from Mount Mor-
gan, which has no silver, although blister
copper generally contains some of the white
metal.
* * W *
Tin at £240 a ton has exceeded all the old
records, and producers able to place re-
fined metal on the market will obviously
benefit the most bv this phenomenal rise.
Old Mount Bischoff (Tas.)j with its up-to-
date crushing, tin-saving and smelting equip-
ment, should do well out of the rise. For the
half-year to December 31 the co. pro-
duced 151 tons of refined tin on its own
account, and 1008 tons of the metal from
purchased concentrates. The price averaged
£175 os. 5d., which is £65 below the recent
quotation. Reserves are 1| million tons,
but tin contents cannot be sworn to.
* # # #
It has frequently been alleged that
Canadian producers of molybdenite receive
a higher price than those in Australia. As
a matter of fact the Canadian Mines Depart-
ment controls the sale of molybdenite, and
the price for the highest-grade ore is Idol,
per lb., or, roughly, £448 per long ton.
This compares very closely with Queens-
land’s valuation -of £4677 for ICQ tons pro-
duced in the first quarter of this year. The
most important molybdenite show in
Canada is near the Ottawa River (Quebec).
So far as it has been proved, it is a shallow
deposit, worked in one instance from an
open cut 50ft. wide .and 115 ft. long. The
output last year was 5500 tons, averaging
about 2 p.c. of molybdenite. Four nits
have been sunk, and the workings expose a
large quantity of low-grade ore in gneissoid
granite. Fair recoveries have been made
with the aid of the flotation process from
1 p.c. ore.
