15 YEARS OLD
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 14, 1917.
My Dear Arini. —
As‘the Maorilanders swung down George-
street.the other afternoon the sun glinted
ou their multiplicity of brass buttons, so I
expect the scissors will be busy later.
They’re a thick set, solid lot, with heavier
limbs than usually go with our lean, tanned
Kangaroo. Also, more serious countenances.
The M.L. Minister, W. D. S. McDonald, was
the sole possessor, on the Town Hall portico,
of an old-fashioned black-silk tile. He looked
like a solemn elder of the kirk. But you
never can tell. The only elder present, so
far as I know, was Q.-G. Ferguson, and he
was in khaki. Besides, his Ex. gave up the
hon. collection-plate business when he left
the Land o’ Kilts. The portico also seated
Archbish. Wright, in the ordinary official
gaiters, etc., topped by a sporting grey felt
hat (to the undoing of a morning j>aper rep.
who refused to believe an ecclesiastic boss
could appear in such millinery). Municipal
lady dignitaries, past and present, viewed
each other under their eyelashes. Acting-
Premier George Fuller, beautifully groomed,
Owen Cox and Russell Blow (local Govern-
ment Agent for M.L. geysers) were also on
the deck.
The Fairguson and his lady sat under
(isn’t that the correct “Bunty” phrase-
ology?) Meenister Harper in St. Stephen's
Kirk on Sabbath morn. The learned Harper
is the principal of St. Andrew’s College,
who’s just back from a glimpse of Europe
and Ivultur on the high seas. He mounted
big John Ferguson’s pulpit as a special act
of grace to mark the election of the new
Scotohbyterian Moderator. Rev. Jock Pat-
terson wears the honiton-lace frills of the
billet since a week ago.
. When everybody’s friends, the Denmans,
lived at Government House, that com-
plete Who’s Who, Dick Nevill, kept
the invitation-list under his pillow. The
Munro Fergusons, with secretaries who
are profoundly innocent of the dam-
aged feelings of our Best People at the
present moment, unconsciously overlook
some of those former constant visitors and
there is some heart-burning. With Kerr-
Pearse military sec. and a stranger, private
sec. Harman, to send round the pastehoards,
local Society’s bosom heaves with apprehen-
sion. But, at any rate, there is no sum-
mons to the long-deceased citizen to give
Admiralty House the pleasure of his pre-
sence, as in the days when a brand-new
Admiral and his equally-raw sec. used to
reign there.
Until this Thursday a sale of work is in
full swing at Ashfield Town Hall. Funds
go to help the gentle Sisters of the Good
Shepherd Home in that hub of rose-gardens.
Lady Helen opened the affair last Saturday.
On Friday evening there’s the concert in
our Town Hall, to help the same cause, with
Kate Rooney as its chief Voice.
From far-off Egyptian sand comes
a line from a R.A.M.C. man. “Here,
it’s as co’d as charity in winter, and hotter
than Hades in summer. No fun of any
kind. Yet two fine Australian women,
Mrs. Chisholm and Mrs. McPhillamy, stay
and run a canteen and rest-camp for Bill-
jim and brother Atkins. They go on to
an uttermost desert, and a camp of soldiers
salutes their grit.”
That stalwart young Scot, J. C. Wright,
has paid the toll on the French Front.
Sculptor and artist, he won a sheaf of prizes
in British art schools before trying his luck
in Australia. For a time his luck was out.
Then recognition came slowly; but the war
fell heavily upon local art. J. C. Wright,
though burning to enlist, couldn’t afford to;
and the claims of a mother and sister in
Scotland drove him to the Department of
Education. To keep the home-scones burn-
ing, he took a job at the Teachers’ Training
College, Blackfriars, and immediately im-
proved its method. Then Adela'de’s School
of Art gave • him a good billet as its
principal, and 12 months’ leave of absence
to go to the war. The handsome Scot was
engaged to Sydney’s R. McAnderson’s eldest
daughter Jean, now (or was) on the other
side with dad and mother.
Over here on a visit, Mr. D’Abaza, Consul-
General for Russia. At a recent midday
snack he asked the Millions Club —and the
man in the street—to trust the Bear.
That useful little animal, the Boy Scout,
has mobilised himself into a perambulating
“See Sydney.” In twos and threes he has been
leading Jap sailors in port round the local
sights, and haranguing the visitors in first-
rate guide-book lingo. I saw two 10-year-old
boys in the Art Gallery the other afternoon,
one holding forth to about 30 Nippon blue-
jackets on the features of the Australian
court, while the other discreetly conducted
the strangers to seats till the exposition
should have finished.
Apropos of the Boy Scout attached to the
intelligence department of the Mosman War
Chest fete the other week, in the days when
Earl Dudley and his pale consort vice-
reigned over this continent, their small boy
wore the trappings of the Mosman Scouts.
Each parade he was escorted by Jeames in
livery to the scene of manoeuvres, and
was a highly-delighted youngster for the
afternoon. He was even more wildly excited
on the occasions his parent was host' to a
hundred or so of Baden Powell’s Own within
the Government House grounds. The same
youngster also distinguished himself by fall-
ing amongst the indignant gold-fish in the
Gov. House fountain, when Gruner was
painting it.
Some of our Y.A.D.’s are having a strenu-
ous time trying to make conversation with
the wounded Frenchmen who were dropped
into Sydney a week or two back. The other
day a soldier who was not supposed to be out
ot bed had disappeared when a couple of
V.A.D.’s arrived to undertake the usual
pillow-shaking. The astonished girls seized
excitedly upon the first Frenchman in sight,
but pulled up breathlessly to recover some
of their lost school French, so they could
ask him if he know where his mate had got
to. Half a dozen attempts having failed,
one of the charmers in desperation poked
out her tongue and performed a corroboree
before the astounded ally which might have
represented anything but a sick Guillaume-
Jacques. The soldier fled. The girls are
still working overtime composing a suitable
explanation for the offended man.
In the National Gallery (Sydney) there
is only one small sample of Edward Officer’s
brush-work. It’s a haystack, in the Baldwin
Spencer loan collection—now coming down
off its hooks. “The Plains of the Darling,”
the one permanent possession the local gal-
lery owns of the Victorian’s way with the
Outback, is at present on loan at Maitland.
A trustee of the Melbourne Art Gallery,
Officer went through the mill in Paris years
ago. First, there were the early springtime
days, when the youthful Australian wore a
velvet coat, pegtop trousers and a hat a la
Rudolf. He doesn’t admit th : s now, but
they were probably a joy in 1895-1990. Re-
turning to his native State in the latter year
he was met with the bleak news that there
was “no more money.” Drought on station
property. Then followed eight lean years,
when the painter battled through the
usual struggling artist experiences. In a
hungry interval he lectured on “Bohemian
Life in Paris.” Then the bad times lifted,-*
and Edward and a brother are able to collect
the proceeds of record wool-clips from •their
N. S. Wales station, Kallara, 650 miles
nearer to the sunset than Sydney.
Here is the man himself. Last year
Officer’s exhibition and sale of pictures in
Melbourne brought £BOO to thp Red Cross.
He hopes to repeat the dose for the N. S.
Wales branch, by the show which opens in
the Royal Art Society’s rooms on May 18.
The generous Victorian has not forgutten
that in one acute crisis of chasing a
crust he won the Wynne prize (N.S.W.),
which gave him £33 to jingle in his then
empty pockets. Not satisfied with his earlier
work Officer returned to Paris when oppor-
tunity offered, and took another three years’
study in the once-lively hub of artists. Now
he hands over £7OO worth of his best efforts
for sale. And the Red Cross hopes to bank
on the lot.
They also serve who only kneel and scrub
(apologies to Milton). Had the bard lived
to-day he might have written an epic on
the Lady of the Bucket. Rose Hall, on
the heights of Darlinghurst, is a convales-
cent home that runs on velvet tyres, with
the help of hon. nurses, hon. charladies, and
hon. laundresses, who tackle huge washing
days just for love of Billjim. More than
Billzac gets the benefit of their chores.
French soldiers, waiting for a boat to take
them on to New Caledonia, have been
squeezed into the airy sleeping-out veran-
dah. Forty-seven (there are usually only
32) soldiers answer the clang of the break-
fast bell, and the misty blue-grey of Francois-
uniform rubs shoulders with our khaki lads.
Here is Matron Fox, the honorary missus
responsible for the exquisite cleanliness and
perfect order of a home which is as fresh as
new china, from the trim wards with the
snowy quilts showing the Red Cross blaz-
oned in the centre to the uttermost corner of
the larder in the basement. Eighty-three
years ago, when Rose Hall was one of the
mansions of Sydney, they built pantries,
sculleries and kitchens as they have appar-
ently forgotten to build them in the modern
stucco villa. Opened as a convalescent home
in October, 1915, the old place was lent by
the Mutual Life and Citizens’ Assurance
Co. for the term of the war and for 12
months after. There is no rent. Matron Fox,
who gives all her energy, belongs to a squat-
ting family. Two brothers are station-owners
in Central Queensland.
Splashes Weekly breathed its last (till
the war is over, anyhow) quite recently,
but the Hollander Bros, wouldn’t part »on
that account) with their editress, Mrs.
Fotheringhame, until they handed her a
solid token of goodwill. There was a lunch
at Paris House to garnish the event.
It’s Grace Burrows who means to tackle
the anti-shouting campaign the next few
weeks. Gladys Owen sticks to the amateur
recruiting-sergeant’s job. The shout, as I
told you, is need.ng a thumb in the glass (do
you remember Marryat’s skipper’s wife who
hand-decorated the sailor’s tot of rum in
this way to keep the measure short?! to
protect barely-convalescent soldiers from their
too genial friends.
Do you remember the sporty Mrs. Pelloe,
one of two women who rode from Mildura
to the Sydney Show in 1916? Hubby is now
inspecting bank ledgers in W.A., and she
writes from Perth. “The W. A. Trotting
Association tried the Empty Saddle parade
with great success quite recently. There
was a dash to the lively stables to .get mounts
for those girls who hadn't any gee-gee to
lead. One damsel, nothing daunted by the
shortage, dragged a very willing recruit up
in front of her. Another got five recruits
and a proposal in a few minutes. I was in
the fun, my first soldier-to-be begging for a
bit of the ribbon round my horse’s neck for
luck. Girls promised to send their captives
socks, letters, etc., which is only fair. At
the finish the reinforcements were
drawn up to cheer as we landed our volun-
teers at the recruiting-office.”
Mrs. Mae’urcan, who is commanding offi-
cer of the Wentworth Hotel, is offering fat
prizes (£5 is the first bait) for the best six
pair of the woolly comforts. They will pass
the judges’ eyes (Mrs. Harry Finn and Miss
Knox have the last word) on May 30. After
that, tea and bridge. Proceeds go to the
War Chest.
The Phil. Society gave the “Damnation of
Faust” (Phil Newbury in the tenor rdle)
to a big audience in the Town Hall. Con-
ductor Joe Bradley waved his hands (one
thinks of Moses blessing his fiock) over a
dazzling white parterre of feminine chorus.
The males have dwindled since the war to a
meek background with its hair mostly thin
on top. Andrew Black was the Mephisto-
pheles, with W. T. Colyer as a genteel
edition of the roystering Brander. Berlioz’s
“Faust” is like a paddock in early spring—
some of it is dull sod, but most of it is
ablaze with gold and purple patches.
The unofficial Gallery Permanent First-
nighters take themselves more seriously than
their squeals of joy. when a new play
appears, would lead the long-suffering public
to believe possible. The other afternoon they
fed some of their favorites, Lizette Parkes,
Vera Pearce, Ethel Cadman, Minnie Love
and Nancye Stewart on buns and tea.
Melba, by the way, flung a stern glance
upwards, when the P.F.N.’s shouts ol'
glee came near breaking-up the sobs of
Miriam (Kathlene MacDonell) ou the first
night of the Outcast.
From “Rosna” (London) -
The Apple Island Agent-General, Jolyi McCall,
new president of the London A.N.A., is one of the
best-liked officials in Anglo-Australia, and doesn’t
share that common official delusion that if’ you give
your name to a committee you’ve given enough. His
time and services are always available for any matter
touching Australia, and if you want to see him
you don’t find him hedged in by 49 dragons. Having
a firm hand and a sense of humor he ought to be a
useful god-parent to the infant A.N.A.
George and Mary Hex have Col. Anzac, Major
Kiwi, Capt. Mapleleaf and Lieut. Springbok to tea
every Saturday at their little place on the Mall. A
few high commissioners are allowed to come along
too, but with the guests from hospital Royalty
hobnobs as though it' wouldn’t know' a crown if it
saw one.
Stella Miles Franklin, of Brilliant Career fame,
is in London helping to save the Empire. The witty
and bright-eyed Australian came from the U.S. in
the early days of the scrap and has been war-jobbing
ever since. She went to see the’camp where they
plant out delicate babies for the fresh-air cure.
“Well, what do you think of it?” they asked her.
“Worse than the Belgian atrocities,” said Stella
Miles, with a shiver. Nevertheless she became
absorbed in the work and -stayed on helping until
ill-health made her pack up and go. Her latest
ambition is to join the woman’s army in France,
but her health will probably be against her.
Esther Fairbairn had half Anglo-Australia to see
her married at Holy Trinity, Brompton, the other
day. She wore a. golden gown of brocade edged with
white fur, a most scrumptious garment. Her choice,
Lieut.-Col. Leonard Wheatley, who hunts with the
Argyle and Sutherland Highlanders, wore a D. 8.0.
ou khaki. The bride’s captain-brother gave her away.
Among Billjim’s faithful visitors in hospital,
Hampstead way, is Mrs. Mitchell. Formerly a Dutton,
one of the Anlaby (S.A.) clan. She used to stay
at Anlaby in the long-ago days when a father of
the present Justice Buchanan managed the run, but
left Australia as a youngster of 14 and has never
been back.
Dr. Ainslie Clowes, son of medico Clowes, of
Brisbane, and now a captain in the A.A.M.C., had
a matrimonial appointment at St. Andrew’s, West-
minster, on St. Pat’s Day. Bride was Miss Maime
Clowes, whose pater is something high up on the
pinnacles of the army. Another military knotting
was that of Major Hector Clayton, son of the J. H.
Claytons, of Sydney, to Mis 3 Phyllis Midwood, a
damsel who dwells in Oakley Hall. There is also
to be a job for a marriage-smith shortly to clamp
up Enid Burton, daughter of Canon Burton, one
time of Maoriland, and Capt. Torr, D. 5.0., M.C.,
who is also a son of a cleric.
One of the late (S.A.) Premier Tom Price’s boys
is among our wounded warriors at Boulogne. The
youngster is a lieutenant, and has a bad but not
dangerous leg wound to keep him out of the trenches
for a bit.
The three Cherniavsky brothers. Leo, Jan
and Mischel, will toss their amaranthine
curls in Sydney during June. Mike,
the youngest, has secured for life Mary
Rogers, a daughter of the millionaire sugar
king, of Vancouver. The heiress comes to
Australia with her musician.
Emerald-green ruches wind round some of
the. black velvet winter hats. They’re a
vivid color note, and reminiscent of the
trimming worn by the young ladies who play
croquet in Trollope’s old yarns.
What about Violet Day before the lovely
blooms pass_their meridian? The Soldiers’
Club wants to revive the 1915 flower-day,
which gave them a lucky start. Otherwise,
’ware wolf.
The Red Cross gets the proceeds of a new
song by Sybil Heydon—wife of our Arbi-
tration trudge. Music by May Fenner;
publishers, baling’s; title, “Give! Give!”
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

FALLING HAIR CAUSED BY
DANDRUFF.
HOW TO DESTROY THE DANDRUFF
GERM AND SAVE YOUR HAIR.
Thin, brittle, colorless and scraggy hair
is mute evidence of a neglected scalp; of
dandruff, that awful*, scourge. There is
nothing so destructive to the hair as dan-
druff. It robs the hair of its lustre, its
strength and its very life, eventually produc-
ing a feverishness and itching of the scalp,
which if not remedied causes the hair roots
to shrink, loosen and die —then the hair
falls out fast. For the purpose of destroying
the dandruff germ you should get your
chemist to prepare a lotion consisting of
3 ounces Bay Rum, 1 ounce Lavona de Com-
posee and i-dram Menthol Crystals. Rub
this thoroughly into the scalp with the
fingertips. It will kill the dandruff germ,
and soon your hair will take on that life,
lustre and luxuriance which is so beautiful.
It will become wavy and fluffy, and
have the appearance of abundance and
incomparable gloss and softness; but what
will please you most will be after just a few
weeks’ use, when you will actually see a lot
of fine downy hair —new hair—growing over
the scalp.— Advt.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, May 14, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie. —
The funeral ceremonies of the late Arch-
bishop Carr occupied the whole of Saturday,
and St. Patrick’s was packed all day, hun-
dreds standing for hours. The service began
with the tolling of the bell and the soft
playing of the Dead March. High digni-
taries in magnificent robes -went up the steps
of the altar; and hundreds of white-surpliced
priests took seats which surrounded the
coffin of the dead prelate. Altar boys car-
ried candles to the priests and the choir,
until hundreds of tapers were shining like
stars in the gloom. The choir of male voices
was magnificent. A snow-white mitre was
placed upon the altar —that was for the
new Archbishop. The jewelled mitre of the
dead Archbishop rested with his stole upon
his coffin.
The deacons then prepared to robe Dr.
Mannix in his new vestments. Surrounded
by chiefs in cardinal and purple robes and
white linen, the deacons and sub-deacons
enclothed themselves in magnificent stoles of
black velvet with white bands. The Master
of Ceremonies was enveloped in black
with a wonderful cape of lace. They and
the scarlet-draped incense-bearers knelt in
a circle before the altar and handed the
vestments to the new chief. First a cape
of snowy linen, crossed in front and tied at
the back; then a robe of white, without
sleeves, and, above that, an all-enveloping
garment of white, Avith sleeves to the wrist.
A ribbon of an Order was placed about his
neck, and a black-velvet gown was tied at
each shoulder. Over this was put still another
imposing black-velvet robe, fastened at
the shoulders by attendant deacons. Over
this again was placed the third magnificent
black-velvet robe, banded with Avliite. A
great cross was embroidered on the back, and
in the centre of the cross a glittering silver
sun. The white mitre was then placed
upon his head. With his hands clasped he
walked toward the congregation and then
to the steps of the altar, where he made an
offering of incense. The red-robed Apos-
tolic Delegate mounted the pulpit and spoke.
His vestments fairly blazed against the
black drapings of the pulpit, and a great
ruby glowed in the centre of his big gold
cross. Meanwhile Archbishop Mannix sat
quietly in his heavy vestments, with his
narrow Avhite face showing marks of his long
vigil and fast. His black eyes looked large
and sombre under his white head-dress.
The bishops were then invested with white
mitres and surrounded the coffin with the
priests. Impressive music Avas played and
chanted while candles and incense were
burnt.
In the afternoon' the body Avas carried
through the streets of Melbourne, and late
in the evening the final burial service Avas
read before the altar of the Sacred Heart
Chapel, in a tomb chiselled out of the con-
crete. A ribbon of black tiles is to mark
the place when the mosaic is replaced. This
takes the place of a suggested black cross
by the dying decree of Archbishop Carr.
He said it would be impious to place a cross
Avhere it would be trampled upon. The
weary, home-going crowds saw the great
windows softly lighted and heard the sound
of the organ playing solemnly as the dead
man was left to his rest.
Aubrey and Geoffrey Officer, reported as
‘‘missing,” are sons of the late squatter
Charles Officer by his second Avife. With
tAvo other brothers the lost lads were looking
after the family ostrich-farm at Kerang
when the war began. A third brother got
into khaki some months ago. About a dozen
of the Officer clan haA r e seen active service.
The W. L. Baillieu dame and-her husband
are naturally delighted about second son
Harry winning, the Military Cross. The
boy, a fine specimen of Australian youth,
eulisted in England at the beginning of the
war and got a commission in the 1 loyal
Artillery.
Harry Ross Soden has cabled home to his
mother that he is quite Avell and hearty. lie
was on the torpedoed Ballarat.
Iloy Bridges, a young soldier-journalist,
formerly on the Age, has built a war book
called “The Immortal DaAvn.” He writes of
the Landing at first hand, for he was one
of the first Melbourne recruits.
Louis Smith (elder son of the late L.L.),
who met a hero’s death in France, left a
sad little widoAV in Melbourne, and a three-
months-old son, to whom his soldier daddy
Avill only be an inspiring tradition.
The Sturt-street Kitchen, which was so
A'ery busy in early recruiting days, is going
to shut down, as the Y.M.C.A. and other
adjacent places completely fill the bill —or
Billjim—now that so many of the boys are
in a different locality. The kitchen has a
surplus of some £5OO or £6OO, and George
Fairbairn is asking permission to send the
money to the hard-up Anzac Buffet in Lon-
don. Alison Fairbairn Balmain has been
writing out about its hard-upness, likewise
Mrs. Alfred Deakin, aud appeals have come
from the Buffet itself. There was a meeting
in the Fairbairn house on Thursday, and
an extra £lOOO was promised by those in
the room. Mrs. Boss Soden also offered to
place her home at the disposal of a bridge
party.
The crews that battled for the “Head of
the River” turned up at “So Long Letty”
on Saturday evening. The Geelong Gram-
mar boys, who were the winners, sat in the
stalls in their pale-blue coats. Dorothy
Brunton, Marie Eaton, the men principals
and most of the chorus sported pale-blue
rosettes, armbands or garters; and flappers
with pale-blue hair-ribbons and proud
mothers with bits of ribbon pinned to their
dresses were scattered about the stalls and
circle. In the auditorium and on the stage
were also many who sported the dark blue
and green of the Geelong'College, which was
second in the race, and the purple and gold
of Wesley, which finished third. Through-
out the first act the performance was held
up at intervals by bursts of schoolboy cheer-
ing, signifying the arrival of a blush-
ful “cobber,” and generally a self-
possessed flapper. The boys also amused
themselves by throwing paper streamers on
to the stage and letting loose clusters of bal-
loons, representing their colors. Hugh Ward
beamed on everybody from the back of the
circle, Mrs. Tallis, with her two young sons,
was in the managerial pen, and Mrs; “Doc”
Yelland, with three girl chicks, was in the
opposite box. Next door was Archibald
Strong, with three khaki-clad friends, one of
whom was Reg Franklin, headmaster of
Melbourne Grammar, who is now in camp.
The ’Varsity is joyfully welcoming Pro-
fessor Osborne. The genial man has been
dodging submarines for the last eight months
in various seas while flitting round the
Allies’ slices of the world.
Mrs. Alfred Deakin, recently returned
from London, talked in the Town Hall last
week on baby clinics, and described the one
at Stepney and its school for mothers as one
of the sights of London. Mothers are there
given practical lessons in the preparation of
food for babies, in washing and dressing,
baby’s dressmaking, and taught how to
recognise symptoms of ill-health. Quite 2000
babies are brought to the Clinic every week
to be weighed and searched for scratches and
symptoms, a doctor being kept at hand to
tinker up any that need repairing. The lady
lecturer also described interestingly the care
of.child life in America.
Margaret Grosvenor, who was married to
Peyton Jones in England the other day, made
many friends when she sheltered for a year
under the wing of the vice-regal Talbots, her
uncle and aunt. Melbourne remembers her
as a handsome girl built on generous lines
with a happy nature which refused to be
depressed by a limited chiffon allowance.
Bridegroom Peyton Jones is an Englishman,
although his mother happened to be sister
to sporting Dick Casey and the Ken Brodribb
widow.
Mrs. Sep Miller’s young sister, Marjorie
Henderson, and a woman friend, are going to
try and run a farm, in the heart of the
mountains, near Woodend. A fortnight ago
at 6 a.m. they made a start.
Mrs. Ned Teague, once Violet Reid, re-
cently landed with a small son on an extended
visit to mother and sister Fairlie. Naval
brother Roger is busy at sea these days.
The smile of Hughes has been illuminating
the whole city since the votes were counted,
though during the anxious speech-making
period it really seemed as if he intended to
look as solemn as a wooden Indian for the
rest of his days. Damocles would have felt
as Hughes does now if someone had taken
down the hanging sword and substituted a
harmless penny balloon.
W. I>. S. MacDonald, who is here purchas-
ing wheat on behalf of the Imperial Supply
Commission, found time to eat a lunch with
Premier Peacock on Friday. Hagelthorn,
Minister of Agriculture, and his wife, the
Manson couple, and Mrs. MacDonald also
toyed with the cutlery.
There was a great dance at All Saints’
Hall last week. Many of the men were of
artillery bound for the Front.- There
were not quite enough of them to go round,
but the girls took turns at playing wall-
flower. Helen Buckland and Marjorie
Symes arranged the hop, and the Patriotic
Fund got the benefit.
Adelaide’s tablets :
Drenching rain on Saturday drove the Ministering
Children’s League fair in aid of orphanages from Mrs.
Legoe’s garden at Hyde Park into her house. How-
ever, the downpour had no appreciable effect either
on the attendance or its buying proclivities. Mr.
Pope, who has always kept a kindly eye on the
work of the Children’s League, made the official
declaration that the sale was well and truly open.
The Harry Gilberts have returned from honey-
mooning in the Eastern States and are settling down
in the T. G. Wilsons’ house in North Adelaide.
News has come through that the Military Medal
was awarded to the late Lieut. Denis Jones for •
conspicuous bravery in the field in September, 1916,
only a very short time before he was killed. Another
S. Australian to win distinction is Major Noel
Loutit, once of the 10th and now of the 50th Bat-
talion, who has got his D.S.O. Also, Major Greenway,
who was married in January last to Miss Constance
Phillipps, of our village, has five times been men-
tioned in dispatches.
Senator Pearce left the Indarra here and went on
by train to Melbourne next day, filling in part of
the interval by speaking at a soldiers’ relatives’
rally in the Town Hall. This rally was organised
in the interests of his namesake; Lieut. Pearce, an
aspiring politician. It was mostly a feminine gather-
ing, with- a woman J.P. (Mrs. Wallington) in the
chair and Mrs. Crawford Vaughan as one of the
speakers.
Mrs. Andrew Johnston, whose son was making good
progress from the wounds he received in France, has
now been notified that he died from pneumonia on
May 5. Flight-Lieut. H. Hayward, son of Lieut.-Col.
Hayward, has been severely wounded in. France, but
is progressing favorably; and Lieut. Niel Campbell
has been wounded in the right arm. Ilis brother,
Gordon, who was wounded about a month ago, is
convalescent.
Miss Kate Gardner returned to her adopted Mel-
bourne at tlie beginning of the week after a very
brief visit to Adelaide. She was the guest of the
Gavin Gardners at St. Peter’s for the few days that
she was here.
The R. L. Davidsons have left here for London,
via America. At least, he is for London and she for
America, till Fate and German submarines and the
powers-that-be will allow her to complete her journey.
The holiday brought us no excitement beyond the
first day of the S.A.J.C.’s Cup Meeting at Morpliett-
ville, where amongst the big crowd recruiting-ser-
geants might have done good business.
Lewis Cohen, who still holds the record for the
extent and lavishness of his entertainments when
Mayor, is back temporarily in office as loeum-tenens
for Mayor Isaacs, who has gone holiday-making to
Sydney with his wife.
Cheerful news comes from the Rosmans in London,
where the critics have been more ’than usually kind
to Mrs. Rosman’s verses, and where Miss Alice- Ros-
man is dividing her time between book-writing and
contributing to the British-Aurtrauasiax. Mrs.
Fawkner, once of Melbourne Ann us, is also on the
B.A.
The Presbyterian Conference has been -exercising
itself about our Sabbath-breaking propensities, week-
end cables and Sunday trains and trams being in-
cluded as part of our sins. A few moderates pro-
tested against the inclusion of cables and trams in
the general condemnation-, but the extremists won
the wrangle., It’s a wonder they didn’t include boil-
ing the kettle and washing-up the cups.
Mrs. T. Westbrook, of Hobart, had her
77th birthday lately, and celebrated it by
singing in a local church choir in company
with her. daughter, grand-daughter and great-
grandson. The old lady has been a chorister
tor over 60 years, and seldom misses a prac-
tice or a service.
The French Red Cross engineered a Gift
Afternoon of notable proportions at the
Grand Hotel on Tuesday afternoon, to
help stock the shop that the Patriotic
League intends to run for a week in
Collins-street in aid of the British Red
Cross. The League had a great day last
year for the French Red Cross, and this
was a graceful return. The management of
the Grand gave the use of the big supper
room and the. lounges, and threw in after-
noon tea. Visitors dropped gifts into huge
laundry baskets, which were wheeled away
by bellboys,, emptied, and trundled back for
more. The big crowd surged and jostled
in the corridors and filled the supper-room.
Red-white-and-bluc lights glowed dimly
among autumn leaves in the afternoon fog.
Boffard arranged a musical programme and
sang himself. Mrs. Evelyn Campbell, a
good-looking young matron', also sang, and
Charlotte Hemming recited. Agnes Copies
made herself useful at the piano.
The Hennessy held quite a reception, and
Mrs. Crevelli worked with her usual energy.
The new French Consul. Fliche, a tall,
handsome young man with coal-black eyes,
was there without his wife —a, delicate little
lady who can’t fuss at these functions. Her
Ex. came for a little while, and the Madden
dame a’so looked in.
The Playhouse had a big audience on
Friday night for the entertainment pro-
vided by . the French Croix Rouge in aid of
the British Red Cross. Governor Stanley
and his lady came along and brought the
whole family, with the exception of the latest
baby. The small tribe sat in a row flanked
by Genthelp Conant, the John Maddens and
the Hennessy pair. The genial Hennessy
made frequent descents upon the baskets
of vended sweets and presented huge boxes
of chocolates to the children. The Bowes
Kelly family were there, also Delprat, of
Broken Hill, and his daughter. French-Consul
Fliche was in the front trenches, like Lauers,
of Belgium. A very large, overdressed
matron kept standing up and gazing round
the house. She wore three rows of agate
beads, about the size of millstones, round
her neck. (You will know her next time
you see her.)
A little girl-pupil of Jennie Brenan danced,
and the Stanley children were wildly inter-
ested. They also leap under Brenan tuition.
The Mayor and Mayoress of Caulfield are
beating the big drum for a roll-up to a
Grand Novelty and Poster Night ,n the Caul-
held Town Hall on Saturday. There will
be dancing, card tournaments and music, and
you may wear evening dress like a sober
citizen, or fancy rags and chance winning
rt prize. The spoil goes to help the
Y.M.ChA. and Red Cross,
Gladys Frazer, the moon-faced beauty of
the three Frazer girls, has just altared her
name to Mrs. Charles Young, at Colombo.
The engagement was brief. They met in
Sydney just before Christmas, and after his,
departure to work amongst the tea, Gladys
gathered the- trousseau. Her husband isn’t
a planter himself, but is deep in the trade.
Melba got a slirieky welcome home from
the white-frocked girls of her Conservatorium
singing class. The dear young things nearly
swooned with rapture, and in return their
idol tearfully urged them to try and make
the world happy. Then she introduced
Charlie' Chaplin, and became eloquent on how
much he had done to avert melancholy!
Melba is now hard at work with Stella
Power. She wrote from America to her
friend George Allan, and directed: “Do not
exploit her until I come home; there is a
great future ahead of the girl and I expect
to see her make a hit in this country some
of these days.”
Governor Stanley and his little lady were
alone in a box at “Penelope” on Wednesday.
Her Ex. was heavily furred, and the house
was full of other pelts torn from the. animals
of the wild that are being exterminated.
The smart East Melbourne Collis girls
stalled with country sister Airs. Mackintyre,
and the Fenner girl sat wrapped to the
ears, with two well-rugged friends. There
were no sheepskin coats in the house ; but, of
course, we have to economise these days.
Cecil Bradley got two chances during the
recent fogs and rains. First, Maud Fane
went off with a cold, and Cecil ran on as
understudy. When Maud crept back into
her place, Dorothy Brunton retired to a
private hospital for a week, and Cecil
stepped into her silver tinsel slippers.
Dot Brunton is not going to seek her
fortune in America after all. The J.C.W.
chiefs have promised to cast her in a London
production of “High Jinks.” Connie Ediss
also is going back to the dear old city. What
a welcome they’d get if they filled their
portmanteaux with potatoes!
Grace Palotta has grown a little weary
of selling hats, and will shortly return to the
footlights on the Tivoli circuit.
Ada Reeve’s stage hair is a lovely auburn,
with the curls stiffened down to the skull,
according to the severe fashionable ideal.
Her dress is a powder-blue silk that can
stand up on its hem and is puffed up into
panniers over the hips with wreaths of pink
October or even November roses. The under-
petticoat is a real Nell Gwynne extravagance
of silver lace and ivory. The occupier has
a special tapestried drawing-room on stage,
with teak furniture. She still limps a little
in her silver-tissue slippers.
Toots and Lorna Pounds, the clever Aus-
tralian children who used to be hired and
petted by Melbourne instigators of At
Homes, are doing well in a London revue
at the Alhambra.
Felice Lyne, the soprano who took her
high G’s in Australia with the Quinlan
crowd, has been doing a singing turn at’one
of the big London vaudeville houses. When
here she put up' at Menzies, and the green
parrot belonging to the Youngs across the
street used to take free lessons .from his
perch among the blue lobelia window boxes
every morning. In his hilarious moments he
still screams bits of grand opera.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

Character, and Dewar’s for the ideal com-

Taste. The Taste of Dewar’s is the Golden
Key which unlocks all the pleasant avenues
of the memory. In Dewar’s Whisky there is
something creamy and yet not cloying;
something velvety and yet not heating;
something rich and yet not obtrusive ; some-
thing crisp and yet not crude Strong and yet
gentle, Dewar’s is the Paladin of Whiskies.
Age
is the Crowning Stage in a series of fer-
mentations. Fermentation means Life. Age
dissipates its roughness, rounds off its edges,
and refines its character, leaving nothing
behind but the Rarest Elixir of Life. Dewar’s
is the Veteran of Stimulants.. It is ripe for
Promotion to your Palate.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
"Werrimee” : Brumbies were once fashion-
able as hacks in country towns that were contiguous
to brumby haunts. There were good riders in town
as well as outside, and once in a while one of them
managed to bring home a prize. He might have
bought a reliable hack for less than he spent in
time, not to mention the risk he took of breaking
his neck in the capture of the wild horse; but there
was a distinction in riding about town on a genuine
brumby. Anybody could canter up and down the
street on an ordinary nag, but only one in a hundred
had the unique privilege of exercising on a wild
mustang. He attracted notice wherever he went.
The horse made him temporarily famous. • There
wasn’t a quadruped within 50 miles of Bill Brooker’s
brumby or Tom Faraway’s outlaw mare so well
known.
THE DEPLORABLE DISHONESTY
OF THE AVERAGE BLOKE.'
COUNSEL: “Do' you still say you were unaided in this crime?"
BILL THE BANK-BREAKER: “Yus. Yer see I didn't know anyone honest enough to trust.”
"Lantana'': Is Binglii losing his bush in-
stinct or craft? One of him, a grandfather of his
tribe, recently lost his way up at Maclean, Clarence
River (X.S.VV.), and when found he had been
describing’ circles for a fortnight, and was nearly
dead. He was a pretty ancient specimen, within
cooee of the century, old enough probably to have
forgotten whatever bush lore he had ever known or
inherited. That he was still alive speaks much for
his constitution.
‘‘Gullabong” : The Australian black has a
provident system for keeping the larder supplied
with cluck, which was described to me by an old
blackfellow on the Boorowa liiver, a tributary of
the Lachlan. In the breeding season, when the
young birds are coming on, the alios. go out in
parties, following the creeks until they come upon
some old birds covering their young. As the hunters
approach, the elders, thinking to detract attention
from the chicks, flutter away as if with a broken
wing; but Binghi is too wily to be fooled by such
a device. He captures the youngsters, bites through
the first joint of one of the wings, and puts them
back in the creek, where 1 1 1 ey feed and grow up
until they are large enough to make a decent dish.
M hen the time is ripe, there is no difficulty about
collecting the ingredients, as they are unable to fly.
This has probable given rise to the misconception
that exists that file blacks catch the birds by diving
into the water and coining up under them. Wild
duck are exceedingly timid, and alive to the least,
noise, and no blackfellow, however slick, could get
anywhere near them if they were able to fly.
* * * *
“E.S.S.”: The vocabulary of willy wag-
tail, or black-and-white fantail (jirri jirri and
pulcherry of the blacks), is about the best known
of any bird in the bush, for he loves to perch on
the woodheap, on stumps in cleared spaces and on
garden railings, twisting and turning all the time,
while chirping, “Sweet, pretty creature!” like a
conceited little dandy. At times he drops this
egotism and becomes poetical, calling cheerily,
“Kitty Fisher, get your pitcher.” A favorite perch
of his is on the back of a cow. Strawberry takes
no active notice of him as he hops along from her
tail to her head, sometimes pirouetting on her horns
and darting at the flies that settle on her. Occasion-
ally lie is seen on the back of a sheep, pulling out
little tufts of wool to line his nest, which is neatly
built of grass, well hound with spiders’ webs and
placed in a thick bush. He dances his little jigs
also on horses, even when they are being driven in
the shafts of a cart. The seissors-grinder, or rest-
less flycatcher, closely resembles willy wagtail in
appearance; the main difference being that willy
has a black throat, whereas the whole of the
grinder’s undersurface is.white.
“Te Wbare’': The diamond snake usually
breeds in hollow logs or under piles of brushwood,
etc., but 1 have found it nesting in the hollow of a
dead tree, about 12ft. up. The lady had carried
some dry grass up and lined her chamber with it.
Unfortunately for herself she left part of her coils
sticking out of the hole, which resulted in her
destruction. The body contained a string of soft-
shelled eggs about the size of those of a dove, but
more elongated. Another egg-laying serpent, the
brown snake, also breeds occasionally in tree hollows,
but more often deposits its eggs under dead leaves or
debris on the ground. Having done so, this amiable
reptile goes about its business; the diamond snake,
on the other hand, coils affectionately about its
treasures and assists in their incubation.

“H.V.E.”: Except the deadly red-back
there is no other Australian spider known to be
dangerous; at any rate that is the general opinion of
spiderologists. Red-back is often confused with a
larger plum-colored variety, having round spots of
bright vermillion. It is commonly found under
stones and pieces of bark. The red-backed devil,
on the other hand, is about the size of a pea and
has a band of red above the thorax. It. is usually
lurking in outhouses, beneath scraps of galvanised
iron and tin, and in jam tins, discarded billies,
kettles, etc., left lying about, but is not aggressive.
Writer unwittingly carried one about for some time
on a scrap of galvanised iron and it made no attempt
to bite. Symptoms of persons bitten somewhat
resemble those resulting from deadly snake-bite,
but are less intense. Medical journals record the
trial of strychnine and other snake-bite remedies
with varying results. But no spider should be
'trusted. I once imprisoned about 50 spiders' in a
large pickle jar. One bloated yellow-striped indi-
vidual out of ibis motley crew ambled solemnly
around biting all that came in its way. It would
stop for a moment, regard its victim fixedly, and
then literally fold it in a deadly embrace. The
assaulted party simply curled up and died.
“Luke” : Poisoned rabbit as an article ,
of diet has nearly wiped out the native cat
( dasyurus ), but his big brother, the tiger-cat, still
survives even in quarters where poison is laid. The
reason probably is that the tiger-cat, fiercest and
most courageous of our native animals, prefers to
kill its own meat, and is much less addicted to
carrion than the other smellful little beast. The
tigiers occasionally visit poultry yards on South Coast
(N.S.W.), cut the heads off several ebooks, and drink
their blood. One killed in a foray of this kind
measured over 4ft. from tip of nose to end of tail.
Rock piles bordering the coast are their favorite
haunts.

“Mrs. Lanc-e Rawson” : How's this? Was
once rocking my baby, aged 10 weeks, in a
chair before the fire, when I felt something bump
my head each time the rocker moved backward.
Without kicking up I several tirnep brushed* my
hand across my head, but went on soothing the
infant to sleep. Presently the Kanaka boy came
to the door with a message and exclaimed, “Quick,
you jump up, Missus; quick, quick!” .1 got up
and saw a huge carpet snake suspended from the
overhead rafter, head down. He had been trying
to get away and intended to use my- roof as the
first stage in the descent. He measured 6ft. 4in.
Another time I was sitting up with a very sick
friend, to whom the least shock might prove fatal.
It was in Queensland, and the mosquito nets were
drawn. Happening to look up I noticed a movement
on the top of the nets and towards the bed post.
It was only a big black snake who was endeavoring
to make his way down to inquire after the patient's
health.
“H.”: Items from explorer McKinlay’s
bill of fare at the tail-end of his journey along
the Burdekin River prospecting for Burke and Wills:
Camels liver stewed and steamed—“dry and juiceless,
but the heart as good as a bullock’s.” The party
grew quite fond of camel-meat. Wild figs—“insipid
and full of small flies,” but some baked roots (nar-
doo, probably) commandeered from aboriginal ovens
were voted “first-rate.” “Heart, liver and kidneys
of a horse excellent as hash.” Greenhide soup!—
“all enjoyed it very much.” “Shot a few crows,
a cormorant, and a white eagle with a blue back
(the white-bellied sea eagle). These, with a little
salted hide to give them a flavor, made an excellent
stew.” Gathered a “palatable fruit about the size
arid color of a plum” (the blue quandong, probably).

THE LADY: “If you chop the wood, bring in
the cows, feed the pigs and put er
coupler nails in the fence I’ll give yer
thrippence.”
LANGUID LUKE: “ Madame, I refuse to accept

“Our crow stew excellent.” In certain favored
localities the explorers fed luxuriously on wild
duck, pigeons, fish (bream, cod, and fresh-water cat-
fish chiefly) and kangaroo, wallaby and “euro” meat.
(What is that?) Later, MeKinlay and co. struck a
white man’s camp, and were soon pitching into beef
and damper, and “don’t let me forget it,” says
MeKinlay, “potatoes and mustard.” This civilised
tucker, especially the damper, gave most of the men
severe indigestion, and their legs and feet “swelled
very much.”

ON CALM REFLECTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Marie, it is so long ago,
And you come back again!
But why recall the distant woe,
Thy dead year’s mede of pain?
That I was kind is very true,
Yet, dear, this somewhat tardy view
But acts as a reminder ;
For kind as I was, sweet, to you,
To you you were still kinder.
os, yes, I do remember well
The furze edge bowed with gold,
The cooing birds, the distant bill,
The tender tale l told,
The atmospheric facts, and all
The piquant matters you recall.
Most eloquent inquirer ;
But though you did my eye enthral,
You were your true admirer.
You had the brownest of brown eyes.
The brightest hair I’ve seen,
Your lips were fashioned Cupid-wise,
Your teeth like pearl between
Shone when you sweetly smiled on me
(How oft you smiled to let me see!)
But now your scented letter
Proves though I knew these things, Marie,
You knew them sooner, better, j
I sought you out at every dance -
That’s recollected too.
1 tramped the hills because perchance
I’d catch a glimpse of you. .
These lines your lone condition tell.
That best it is it so befell
I’ve honestly concluded.
For though I feel I loved you well
Mono loved you, dear, as you did!
VIOTOIUA.
EDDYSON.

DRINKING WITH MEALS CAUSES
INDIGESTION.
“You should nev/er drink with meals if
you are troubled with indigestion,” I was
told at a time when I could hardly eat a
meal without suffering afterwards. I fol-
lowed the advice rigidly, and, although I
was somewhat relieved, still suffered a good
deal. I tried several medicines, but without
any beneficial effect until a medical friend
recommended taking about half a teaspoonful
of ordinary bisurated magnesia in half a
cupful of warm water after each meal and
at bedtime. The result was quite satisfac-
tory. The bisurated magnesia entirely
counteracted the acidity in the stomach,
which causes dyspepsia; relief came almost
immediately and I am now able to eat, drink
and enjoy my meals without bad effects.
Drinking with my meals does not worry me
now. In case any readers would like to try
this excellent remedy, which I understand
relieves other kinds of indigestion just as
effectually as it did mine, I may add that it
is obtainable from any chemist, but I strongly
advise anyone trying it to make perfectly
sure they get the bisurated magnesia, other
forms not being so suitable for this purpose.
—E.T.G.— Advx.'
“Silas Snell” : They were building a bridge
across the Piper, and Consadine, a very observant
man of an exceedingly curious character, developed
an abiding interest in Dave Eagles, who had a
small, parched, weatherboard house nestling in a
dust bath on the side of the rise about a quarter
of a mile up the road to Hoban. What drew
Consadine’s particular attention to Eagles was the
latter’s extraordinary habit of coming out every
evening at about sundown, standing in the middle
of the track, and peering up north to the line where
the horizon cut across the road. One evening
Consadine, feeling that he must know or bust, asked
Dow (an oldest inhabitant) what the devil Eagles
meant by it. Dow sniffed. He assumed a sentimental
expression. “Poor ole Davie.” he said, and sniffed
again. “A matter of 20 rear ago” (sniff, sniff)
“his wife run away with a baker front Hoban, and
every evenin’ since lie’s come out like that doleful
and alone” (sniff, sniff) “and looked up that there
road, hopin’ to see her a-comin’ back.” Consadine
is a man of sentiment himself; lie was drawn to
Eagles, and one evening he intercepted him when
taking his customary view. “Wlnit are you looking-
for, mate?” he asked. Eagles snorted. “Damn
vouse ’n’ yore bridge!” lie said. “The brewer’s
lorries what comes from Hoban at odd times always
gives me a gallon of beer for harnessin’ up my
’orse, ’n’ givin’ them a pull over the ford. I’m
gener’ly on the look out for them.”

“Montalcx”: It is tolerably certain that
mouse-multitudes originate always in grain-accumu-
lations that have been loft long undisturbed. About
every eighth week sees anew mouse-family of six
to 10, and these, in about another eight weeks,
start reproducing in turn; so that, with constant
food, shelter and safety, the figure soon runs into
millions. The strangest fact about these mouse-visi-
tations is that the plague sometimes totally dis-
appears in one night. Many years ago, in north-
western Victoria, a district that had for weeks been
swarming .with the vermin found itself one morning
without sign of a mouse, except the hosts of slain.
Fantastic explanations of this phenomenon are
legion, but no reasonable theory has yet been
advanced.
“Deck-hand”: The German boiling-down
corpse factory isn’t new. Nearly 30 years ago the
Merrie England discovered on the coast of New
Guinea that the lamented cannibal was not wasted
after he had thrown his alley. First, choice cuts
were devoured; then other sections were boiled to
extract juices which were infallible poisons for
arrow-tips; finally the scraps were rendered down
to grease. It was a rarity to find even small bones
disregarded, and shins and larger bones were used to
clinch arguments in the young men’s Bible clubs.
* * ■* *
“Bib”: The ring-tailed ’possum isn’t
always satisfied to have its domicile in trees. I
know a pair which have dwelt in a nook or gable
of the same house-roof for over 10 years. They
descend at night to nip tender shoots off rose bushes
and will usually say “thank you” for apples left
out for them. While the roof was undergoing re-
pairs its lodgers left and returned when the fuss
was over.
* * * *
“Montalex” : The Egyptian black rat, said
to be gaining ground in S.A., is not identical with
the black rat observed amongst those destroyed in
Adelaide during the plague-scare. This blue-black
rat —once common in Europe, but now almost exter-
minated by the grey Norway rat —is smaller, slen-
derer and swifter than its supplanter, with sharper
muzzle, larger ears, and a much longer tail. It is
occasionally seen about the wharves in Melbourne
and Port Adelaide, but does not seem to have made
good in Sydney or Brisbane.

“Musketoon”: “Narroe” (B. 12/4/’l7)
casts doubt on my knowledge of the Lake Cowal
country. In my par. (B. 15/3/’l7) I did not men-
tion the area under wheat or oats in the lake bed.
All I do know was that the cocky mentioned filled
the cracks in the ground with sawdust, slung a bit
of manure about, and reaped a good crop. Cracky
soil is not unsuitable for crops. It is met with in
all gilgai country, of which Forbes (N.S.AV.) is an
example; and Forbes is the chief wheat-producing
district in the State—last season 480,000 bags or
so of grain were stacked at the one railway station.
At Weelong, a few miles put, 60 bushels of wheat
per acre has been harvested, and there is a gilgai or
two close handy. Sometimes in this cracky soil
the farmers have to build bridges across the wrinkles
in the surface so that the harvester and other
machinery can gather up the crop. Another word
with “Narroe.” The “pelicans” he says he saw
on Lake Cowal were cranes, of which there are hun-
dreds about at any time. One other lake-bed which-
has grown crops is Lake George, near Gundaroo
(N.S.W.).
“Melos” : We are slowly awakening to the
fact that many pests have their uses. Lantana, one
of the sorrows of the man on the land, can be
turned into paper, as also can' Chinese burr, blady
grass, Scotch thistle and Paddy’s lucerne (Sida
retusa). The last-named has long since formed the
basis of a popular remedy for scours in stock.
Prickly pear has proved a god-send to many cattle-
men in droughts, and a decoction of the leaves is
the finest thing on earth for whooping cough. Other
virtues have been overlooked no doubt in the inten-
sity of the war waged against the vegetable. The
fruit bat (flying fox) lias been regarded as a curse
generally, yet he possesses about six shillings’ worth
of glove-making- skin and an edible carcase.* Leich-
hardt testified to the life-sustaining properties of
the flesh. Living exclusively on fruits and honey,
the animal should be better eating than offal-devour-
ing beasts which we favor.' The smellfulness is only
skin deep. Even the white ant has its uses. As an
article of food in the chicken run, I know nothing
to equal this insect, and the bush dweller can tell
you the value of the ant-heap for building ovens,
barn floors, etc.

That hard-case of ours, Billjim, nearly
brought the wedding of his pal to an untimely end
one day last week. The ring had apparently shrunk
en route to the church, and the groom had some
difficulty in slipping it on his sweetheart’s finger.
The awkward silence was broken by the supporting
force: “Spit on it, Bill,” he advised in a bull’s
whisper!
“Musketoon”: When the excavation work
was going on in the floor of the Murrumbidgee at
Burrinjuck weir, a toad was dynamited out of a
shelf of rock which was 50 feet under the bed of
the river. He gave a few kicks and then died,
probably disgusted with the discovery that even down
in the bowels of the earth a bloke wasn’t free from
interruption.

“James E. D.”: Although I’m a pommy
out only two years and consequently supposed to
know nothing, I propose to correct the statement of
“Wandi” (B. 19/4/’l7) that the apostle, magpie,
lark and the swallow are the only Australian birds
which build mud nests. On the North Coast
(N.S.W), where I was recently employed there’s a
little bird called the pee-wit. lie builds a mud
house.
“Me.”: Aboriginal hand-prints similar to
those mentioned by “Salex” (B. 29/3/’l7) exist in
several small caves at Cowan, an inlet of Broken
Bay (N.S.W.). The markings are scattered here and
there about the walls and roofs of the caves, but it
is evident that many more have been obliterated by
the erosion of the sandstone and the action of
generations of camp-fire smoke. The staining material
was applied on the outstretched fingers, and the
hands were then daubed on the rock face. Some
of the stains are red, others are black. Most of
those remaining are well preserved, which fact gives
rise to a question as to the nature of the stain that
has lasted down the centuries so well. Whilst on
the subject of Binghi. art, can anybody tell me the
meaning of the numerous giant footprints carved in
the rock at Ivuringai Chase, 20 miles or so from
Sydney ? Right and left feet are represented in their
proper order, and each impression measures about
14in. long by 6in. wide. The length of the stride
varies from 6ft. to Bft. Are they intended to record
the existence of some palaeolithic giant who strode
over the face of things with seven-leagued feet?
* * * *
“Sarto”: “Wandi” (B. 19/4/’l7) mis-
leads when he says that the apostle bird, the white-
winged chough, the magpie lark and swallows are
the only Australian birds which build mud nests.
What about the kingfisher and' the diamond-sparrow ?
These two make tunnels, longer than a person’s arm,
in banks of creeks and faces of cuttings. What beats
me is why they frequent the banks of creeks subject
to floods ? Do they provide for this emergency in
the structure of the home or is it for the same
reason that we build castles in the air?
“Te Whare”: Wallaga Lake (Mission
Station) aboriginals (South Coast, N.S.W.) at one
time boasted a gum-leaf band of over a dozen able
performers. This sable orchestra visited neighbor-
ing towns at intervals, and gave performances to
which crowds were attracted, not only by curiosity,
but also by the intrinsic merit of the music itself,
svhich resembled that produced by many Violins
played in flat keys. The leaf is bent double, and
held to the lips jew’s-harp fashion, but few leaves
apparently will serve the purpose. Having a fine
oar for sounds the Australian aboriginal easily learns
to play such instruments as the concertina, accordeon,
mouth-organ and violin.
**.*.'*
“McLirr” : “Nick Bottom” (B. 29/3/’l7)
could have barracked for iron-bark juice in yet
another direction. The blacks in Central Queens-
land use it for sprains and strains, and from personal
experience I can say it is effective. Take a piece of
the bark off a young tree, and, stripping the outer
skin, place the inner portion in a billy with plenty
of water, boil for a feiv hours and apply’the resulting
liquid as hot as can be borne to the effected part,
and immediate relief will be felt. After a few
applications, the sufferer will be right again. If
the part afflicted can be immersed in the liquid the
good effects will be sooner apparent. This holds
good also for boils or for the soreness resulting from
jarring the muscles. The liquid must be warmed at
each fresh application.
“Pick”: When a sudden flood sweeps the
flats under which burrowing animals have made
warrens, not such numbers are destroyed as some
landowners think. During the wet times of 191(1
on low levels among the Jingeras (N.S.W.) the
numerous wombat holes v'ere converted into brimful
wells. The tunnelers came on deck ; but when dis-
turbed before they could remove the furniture and
babies to drier apartments, they dived through six
or eight feet of water and stayed below. From the
bottom of each shaft an upward lateral drive was
tunnelled, at the end of which compressed air walled
back the moisture. In this space the family
assembled ,at prayers for drought.
“H.V.E.”: According to E. R. Waite, no
mean authority on Australian serpents, there are
about 60 venomous and about 60 non-venomous
species amongst them. Fortunately, many of the
venomous ones are exceedingly rare. In addition
to ordinary varieties the Upper Murray swamps
shelter two savage and dangerous six-footers, both
uncommon. Recently, on Monaro (N.S.W.), some
quail shooters struck a rare snake, also in swampy
country. It was of a dull red color, about 3ft. 6in.
in length. Another Monaro snake which I have seen
once or twice is of a silvery grey color, and is,
f think, a harmless variety. A third devil, found
occasionally about rivers on the tablelands, is light
green in coloring-—the green tree-snake, probably.
It is also a non-venomous species; but of black,
.brown and tiger snakes Monaro has a liberal supply.
One black specimen measured over Bft. lin.

“May D.”: Add the magpie-robin to
“Wandi’s” list (B. 19/4/’l7) of mud-nest builders.
This little black-and-white bird makes its home in
the gums of nearly any part of Australia.
“Pick”: When your horse limps from
corns; under the hoof curse the shoer. Shoeing short
tends to throw the prad’s weight upon his heels, and
a crop of corns, with subsequent lameness will
sooner or later appear. To euchre them pare away
the growth with a sharp farrier’s knife until blopd
begins to show through the quick. Then fill the
cavity with an equal mixture of brown sugar and
resin and sear with a red-hot iron. The’ operation
gives the animal little pain, if you haven’t bored too
deeply, and in a few days the remainder of the corn
may be scraped cleanly away. A leather pad under
the next shoes will permit the horse to clout the road
in comfort.

“0.K.” : The unconscions humor of show-
men's puff pars, touches its highest point in
dealing with picture-theatres. Take this,
for instance : “A film feature of more than
ordinary interest will be the first appear-
ance in Melbourne of Mary Pickford under
her own management." A number of black-
and-white ghosts, including the photographic
shade of Mary P., perform a drama on the
screen, and the audience is told to be speci-
ally interested in the fact that Mary is run-
ning the show. That is to say she is “head
of the strong financial organisation” that
paid the salaries.
Moving pictures at Nliill (Vic.) drew one
of the audience right up on to the platform.
A recruiting-meeting lilm was depicting
atrocities committed by the Germans (how
the photographer happened to be on the
scene is not stated), and the earnest citizen
was so carried away that he started to climb
up with a loudly-expressed intention of kill-
ing every unspeakable Hun on the screen.
Whether’ his excitement was temperamental
or spirituous, or stage-managed, is not clear,
but it caused great enthusiasm and induced
a good crop of enlistments when the ques-
tion was put a few minutes later.
“Ami”: Wilson Forbes first came under
the Melbourne eye as a humble member of
the Bijou company, with Mr. and Mrs.
G. B. W. Lewis (Itose Edouin) over 40
years ago. He studied and worked hard,
and became a country manager, known from
Brisbane downwards; but his ideas were too
lofty for the public. He always wanted to
be at the classical. His Sir Peter Teazle
was a fine, ripe performance. With Bellew
and Potter, in Melbourne, he acted Sir
Oliver Surface—the finest I ever saw. I
doubt whether any Australian thespian ever
went through greater hardships. Once when
he was stranded I think he had to work in
a mine for his bread. He tried management
at Melbourne Alexandria before it became
Her Majesty’s, but didn’t do as well as
Dan Barry.
“H. 0.”: In their “Bunyip” panto, the
Fullers try the experiment, of ' a female
Dame. The results are dismal. But what
a bid for success Connie Ediss would make
in tlie Dame part of the next J.C.W. panto.!
“C. Oekney”: The English Divorce Court
seems likely to be working overtime shortly
unravelling the matrimonial knots of mature
theatrical stars. A few days after Lillah
McCarthy had applied for the restitution of
her conjugal rights the cable, announced that
Violet Vanbrugh had called round on the
Judge on a similar errand. Violet, who
owns up to 50, has long been the spouse of
Arthur Bourchier, one of London’s best-
known actor-managers. Her younger sister
Irene (Mrs. “Dot” Boueicault) was in Aus-
tralia with John L. Toole, who gave the
Vanbrugh sisters their first parts on the
professional stage.
A man on Poverty Point in the U.S. sig-
nals some old friends :
After a period of comparative quiet . Margaret
Anglin opened at Buffalo, N.Y., on February 5, in
“The Lioness,” a dramatisation of Gertrude Ather-
ton’s “The Perch of the Devil,” by Rupert Hughes,
author of “Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford.” The play
is hardly suitable for the lady, but it may become
a box-office masterpiece. O. P. Heggie keeps his
place among the headliners. He was in the cast
of Chesterton's “Magic” in New York, and also
played the part of the diffident baby-carrier in
Galsworthy’s trifle, “The Little Man.” There is
some talk of the Australian going into management
on his own account. Young Dion Titheradge has,
become a minor playwright, and tested the Yanks
with a small thing called “A Girl of To-day.”
Lionel Atwill, hero of “The Whip” in Australia, was
lately producing at the New York Bandbox, the
midget theatre where the celebrated Washington
Square Players organisation was born. Rupert
Darrell, formerly J.C.W.’s “generally useful” in
pantomime, has found a place with the Ziegfeld
Follies. Annette Kellerman is mainly submerged
in another nioist act, “The Queen of the Mermaids,”
at New York Hippodrome. Hardee Kirkland, who
was Charles Waldron’s mortal enemy in the Aus-
tralian production of “The Squaw Man,” has climbed
out of an Eastern movie studio to impersonate a
Bad Thirst in a prohibition play, “A Dry Town,”
at St. Louis. Irene Franklin has blossomed into a
song-composer. Her voice and her frocks are as
good as ever. • Gerald Griffin, who successfully
featured Gerald Griffin in Irish plays in Australia,
was last heard of in a farce, “Fifty Miles from
Boston,” at Seattle. Appearing in a new piece,
“Rolling Stones,” at Oakland, California, is Virginia
Brissae, the twang-some actress who ragtimed the
leading part in “The Chorus Lady” at Melbourne
Majestic. Stousher Les Darcy, engaged in vaudeville
for a while, slunk away from Chicago with £6OO
for a week’s shirk. Showman Buffalo Bill left his
widow £13,000 embedded in a pub and a couple
of small ranches. Lily Langtry has come over to
sell her “memories” to the Hearst newspaper syndi-
cate, and is receiving, it is said, £2OO for each
of them.
Business, Bobbery, etc
“8.J.”: About 90 per cent, of the em-
ployees in Melbourne tobacconist shops hav-
ing become members of the Shop Assistants’
Union, they are now asking for hours similar
to those enjoyed by men who work in shops
where mere necessities are sold. At present
Melbourne’s tobacco shops close at hours
varying from fi.3o to 11.80, few in the city
closing earlier than 9 o’clock. Sweating is
rampant in the trade, and it is time that
something was done to bring the fag mer-
chant into line with the tripe vendor, the
drug seller and others. There seems no
reason why the weed should not be declared
a thistle after fi o’clock.

“Hamer” : I had agreed to share my hotel
bedroom with a stranger, and had just com-
menced to dream comfortably when a tragic
voice remarked, “Socks, sir? About what
price?” Then the voice went on, babbling
desperately about socks of all descriptions,
-until in tones of despair it said, “Sorry we
can’t suit, sir. Are you sure these, won’t
do? Good morning, sir!” Then there was
a whirlwind of sighs, and sounds like a
dying whale as the unsuccessful salesman
heaved in his sleep. J.ust as I was dropping
off the dreamer started again: “This is a
very nice line of shirts, sir!” “All right!”
I yelled, “I’ll take them —the whole d —d
lot of them!” He moaned and turned over
and I hoped it was closing time; but he
had only transferred himself to the haber-
dashery department, where lie started to
offer his fastidious customer ties, collars,
braces and other articles. None of them
would suit, and I yelled at him fruitlessly.
The poor wretch could only see the one
implacable customer, and things I offered
to buy out of compassion were wrapped up
and put away while the salesman com-
plained : “Sorry you don’t like them, sir.
Good morning!”
“Tekelo”: Interested to hear from
“McNorth” (B. 28/T2/TG) that cocoanuts in
North Bananaland “bear better, and often
sooner, than in any other part of the world."
Have heard a similar yarn in every cocoa-
nut country I have been in. But once you
have shoved the seed into the ground you
generally find a good seven years elapse
before you begin to handle real money from
them. Cocoanuts should be appraised by a
pessimist. They are very patchy in their
results, even in ideal country. Have known
trees to bear before their fourth year, and it
is fairly common to see occasional trees
I -aring, and dropping nuts, between their
fourth and fifth years. Taken by and
large, the cocoanut palm does not pro-
duce copra till it is five and a half, or
six, years old. Even then the crop is a com--
paratively light one. But should the first
crop, as sometimes happens, be heavy, things
are evened up by the palms producing very
little for the next year or two. Then, about
the seventh or eighth year, they seem to drop
into their stride and bear regularly about
12cwt. of copra per acre per annum. There
is a lot of hot-air being blown about the
copra industry just now, and if any get-rich-
quick newcliums rush in in the expectation
of making easy money in five years they will
be disappointed.
“Lahma": Got one of the pleasures
of my life when, leaving Gallipoli, a
Jack Tar gave me a fill of navy
tobacco. He was supplied with lib.
of leaf in Home waters, and 21b. when
out of Home waters. The leaf, with stalks
removed, is slightly damped with cold tea
and placed one upon another ; then they are
enclosed in a cloth, rolled compactly into a
cigar-shaped bundle and bound tightly with
yarn. In a few days the twist is ready for
choking strangers. Of course, the borrower
is told that plenty of rum has been put on
the leaf; but the sailor only knows one way
of usin' l, rum, and tobacco doesn’t enter into
it.

“Lou" : Beware of the “master gardener”
—the cove who sends his man a day a week
to trifle with your lawn-mower, edge up the
grass-plot and brush all the gravel off your
pathways. The “master” dodges in the side-
way during the afternoon, when no one is
home, and lifts the prize bulbs he sold you
last year and sells them to someone
else. He stocks his miniature nursery
with cuttings from your shrubs, sells
’em back to you, and charges for the
pots that somehow can never be found. A
barrow of manure is called a “load,” and
with a truck of gravel he does your paths
and someone else’s. (That’s a truck each.)
If he does up your garden by contract his
liurry-up methods make his men indifferent,
your shooting jonquils are turned heads
down, and you wonder what makes them so
late. He swops you spades, rakes and even
mowers, and is the neatest thief and cutest
middleman in suburbia. Just now he
is notifying his clients “that owing to the
increase in wages (in Vic. only) his prices
from the end of March will be a bob a day
more. And lie is getting an extra
sixpence under the lap this way: The
man lie formerly classed as a “gardener,”
and paid eight shillings under the old award,
is now regarded as a “gardener’s laborer”
and paid Bs. fid., the new rate for that class,
instead of 95., the new wage for “gardeners.”
* * *
"J. Gee”: I have seen thousands of tons
of black wattle stripped, but never sighted
the stripping tool (a cross between a spoon
and a chisel) mentioned by “Wattle Bark.”
I know only the keen-edged axe and a toma-
hawk. Not many barkers climb the trees.
They get the bark off sooner and get more
of it by cutting the trees down first. They
only use the axe on one side of the tree,
and when the vegetable falls it does not fail
flat, but is propped up at one end, on account
of the butt not being cut through from both
sides. Then the barker lops off the side
branches and runs his tomahawk right along
the full length of the trunk, cutting right
through to the wood. The bark is levered
and pulled off in long strips, which later are
cut into about four-foot lengths and
bundled in hundredweights. If the bark is
taken off’ by climbing the trees it is torn off
in strips of all lengths and sizes, and takes
longer to bundle. “Wattle Bark’s” tree that
gave only lewt. of bark must have been
a sapling. I have seen 4ewt. taken off a single
black wattle.
* * * *
"Melos” : Mention of the late Alban Gee
and Cordingley (B. 2G/4/T7) recalls some
interesting facts in connection with the meat-
canning industry in Australia. Itamornie
Meat Works, which were the first as well
as the largest and most successful of their
kind in the “colonies,” began operations in
1856, and have continued to the present with
but one idle season (1910). Cattle in those
days could be bought for £5 or £(i a head.
In 1849 herds could be had for ridiculously
small figures—Ss. fid. to 12s. fid. each was
considered a good price for the beefy sort.
Itamornie confined itself exclusively to tal-
low-making at that time. Apart from can-
ning meats and mutton, these works have
treated vegetables, kangaroos’ tails, poultry
and horseflesh —the latter for the French
market. In 1874 the weekly killing averaged
250 head of cattle: and 20 years later this
had increased to 800.
* * * *
“B.": Some of the Fiji commercial
houses are going to be bitten hard.
In the fierce competition for copra and trade
Morris, Iledstrom, Ltd., introduced Chinese
traders into the eastern area, and also sup-
ported small Indian traders in the sugar
country. Now on the beach at Levuka goods
can be bought from these Orientals at a
price substantially lower than similar goods
can be obtained from firms whence they 'draw
their supplies. The second stage has been
reached in the sugar districts of western
Vitilevu, where a firm of Indian merchants
now imports goods for a business built on
credit from their white competitors. The
growth .of these Indian storekeepers, who
naturally draw, the great coolie trade, is
amazing, and lias made the white firms sit
up and take uneasy notice. It is confidently
predicted that the Chinese will be the next
to combine and strike out for, themselves as
direct importers and copra buyers. Decidedly
the white merchant has interesting times
awaiting him.
* * * *
“Te Babarantiko” ; The cost of living in
-Australia is a good deal less than in Fiji.
At Suva, outside of hotels, which charge
from 10s. per day upwards, there is no board
and lodging cheaper than 80s. per week,
with additional 12s. to 15s. per month for
laundry. No matter how indifferent the
meal or the bed the lowest charge is 2s. for
each. This is following the lead of the
coastal shipping cos., who, in vessels beside
which some N. S. Wales coastal timber
droghers are sumptuous, levy a rate of £1
per day. These co.’s in their turn are evi-
dently following the track of the Union S.S.
Co. of Maoriland and the A.U.S.N. of Aus-
tralia, which for the eight hours run from
Lautoka to Suva, when never more than two
meals, and sometimes only one, has to be
served on board, demand £1 12s. fid.,- or at
the rate of 4s. per hour.

“K.Q.”: “Globe” (B. 26/4/M7) is right.
Some inquiry should be made re hand-saving
automatic-sending devices for telegraphists.
N. S. Wales will supply them only to
broken-down operators whose nerves are so
played out that they can’t use the old-
fashioned key. In a recent Arbitration
Court case Dr. Chisholm Ross, of Sydney,
said that a man needed to be superhuman to
perform a telegraph-operator’s work under
present conditions, and that the facial con-
tortions of one nervy break-up he saw at
work were sickening. Yet it is necessary for
a man to have reached this pathetic state
before he is supplied with an instrument the
early use of which would have saved his
health and the stupid Department’s time.
“Murkali”: If, as “T.0.D.” (8.19/4/T7)
suggests, a recognised system of word-count-
ing was introduced, much bother and bad
language would be saved in the Telegraph
Branch. The receiving telegraphist has to
count the words of each telegram to see he
has the number specified in the “check.”
Scores of words, such as “postoffice,” “good-
bye,” “tomorrow” and “alright,” are con-
tinual sources of trouble on account of being
written and counted by the customer as two
words, though admitted by Departmental
rule as one. The result is that the receiver
has to stop the sender and query the “check.”
On a duplex line this means stopping four
men, and it is being done on scores of lines
all day long. The dunderheaded Department
contributes as much as possible to the tangle.
For example, “anti air craft gun” is ad-
mitted as one word, “an revoir ” is rigidly
two, while “good-bye" is one.
A MAGNIFICENT A[?].
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
Abo Oofbloom was tlie man wlio made
The Oofbloom Antibilibus Rill,
Then vigorously pushed his trade
In every land on earth, until
The pill enjoyed in either zone
The largest circulation known.
Ilis posters flared on every wall,
lie hung his streamers in the sky.
Whites, yellows, Indians, Zulus, all
Were urged the Oofbloom pill to try.
With blest use of advertisement
Was Abe implicitly content.
His pill won him a million clear,
And into Parliament he swam ;
And ere he reached his sixtieth year
He was the great Sir Abraham.
And then his ads he falsified—
The foolish man fell ill and died !
Years passed, and Gabriel’s bugle skirled.
Came Oofbloom blinking from the clay:
“My vord dot’s heard all rount der vorld,”
He said. “See, Gabriel, I pay
One tousand sovereigns if you vill
Make mention of der Oofbloom pill!”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
THE END OF MACGREGOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Thespian sighed into his beer.
“Poor Tom Macgregor's done, ’ he said.
“I fear he has no chances here;
His acting days are surely sped.
He is too old by far to roam
And start again beyond the foam,
Or take to other duties, hence
There but remains the Actors’ Home
And deadly total abstinence.
“Theatric bosses here about
Had given him just one more try,
Vntil he wore their patience out,
Then whispered him a fond good-bye.
His weakness when in haste is gin,
With beer he trifles otherwise.
For months he lived upon his kin,
Then came a fair engagement in
A local picture enterprise.
“He did the villain pretty well
In Bride, The Cnoba Queen,’
But one hot evening, strange to tell,
He stepped right down from out the screen.
Of course there was the deuce to pay.
It can't be done'? That’s what you think.
In fact, he simply couldn’t stay.
Some chap stood up and shouted: ‘Hey,
Macgregpr, come and have a drink!’ ”

Here we meet again ! How are you ?
Quite well, thank you ? Yes, so am I.
Awfully well and awfully pleased, but I bet
you don’t know why lam so pleased. Well,
I’ll tell you —yes, bai Jove, I will. I’ve just
installed a G.E. ELECTRIC RADIATOR.
Convenient! Economical! Comfortable ! Its
radiance and warmth adds cheer to any
room —its operation is simple—its appear-
ance attractive and ornamental.
How many times do you want a fire
without the trouble of making it, or the
nuisance of clearing the dust and ashes
away ? A G.E. Radiator eliminates all this
unnecessary labour and energy. It may be
used in any room where a suitable electric
receptacle is available —gets “ into action ”
immediately, and continues to produce its
glowing warmth until “switched off” at any
moment when its discontinuance is desired.
Oh, this is the life, to be sure —if
you’ve got a G.E. Radiator. How about
getting one —what ?
