POLITICAL
POINTS
Melb. Argus actually quotes ex-Treasurer
Higgs as saying:—•
The Defence expenditure is extravagant, and I
believe there is not the supervision that there ought
to be over the officers responsible for that expendi-
ture.
After which the Phewsion organ remai'ks
that “the Ministry will be expected to act.”
When the Argus quotes respectfully the
utterances of a Labor ex-Minister there must
be something in the wind, and the difficult
job of shifting Pearce seems to be the some-
thing in this case.
GOT THEM AGAIN.
THE HAPPY WARRIOR ( noticing numerous
reflections of himself in the barber's
mirrors): “’Struth! The blanky
platoon has followed me in, and they're
all potty!"
“Mr. Hughes is far-seeing,” burbles a
Tory sheet. Well, he ought to be. He never
has any trouble with his I’s!
The party system as it is run in Aus-
tralia boils down every time to whether the
people want Waste or Waist.
According to Hughes and the Argus all
German votes went to the Labor party.
Well, it was not through want of the
Liberals cadging for them. In Die Kirchen
und Missions-Zeitung, a German sheet
published in South Aus, two advertisements
in German appeared, one asking for
votes for “Your old, well-tried and big-
hearted Representative, Patrick McMahon
Glynn,” and the other for support
for the Phewsion Senate candidates. What
would Hughes • have had to say if
Labor had issued such a supplication?
[B. J. 11. Owen, 75 Glenhuntly Road, Elwood, Vic.]
OUT OF IT ALL.
YOUNG LADY: “Oh, I’m sure you must have
had some awfully exciting experiences
over there."
BILL: “No, never got much chance, Miss.
I was up at the Front fighting all the
time."
One method employed by the Argus to
help the Phewsion to victory was the pub-
lishing of sensational accounts of attempted
assaults upon Phewsion supporters, and
other alleged crimes by Labor followers.
One yarn, published five days after the
alleged occurrence, was to the effect that
vitriol was thrown at a motor-car in which
Mrs. Maxwell, wife of the Fuse candidate
for Fawkner, was waiting outside the South
Melbourne Town Hall after a meeting, and
burned a hole in the driver’s coat. The
police at South Melbourne knew nothing
whatever about it, nor was it reported to
the C. I. Branch at Russell-street. At the
meeting in question a police sub-inspector,
four detectives and 15 John Hops were in
attendance, and none of them knew anything
of the sensational affair until it was “starred”
in the Argus five days later. Hughes in
his speeches made a point of waxing indig-
nant anent the vitriol-throwing habits of his
opponents, and the Argus states that the
“incident” lost many votes for Labor.
Another Argus yarn was that- Jowett.
Phewsion candidate for Maribyrnong and
his chairman had been stoned, the chairman
sustaining a gash four inches long in his
cheek. Two days after the election the
paper which is demanded of its conscience to
speak the truth, printed a letter from the
chairman, which began : “I am glad., in the
interests of fair play, - to contradict the
rumor that I was injured while acting as
chairman at Mr. Jowett’s meeting.” The
regrettable feature is that Phewsion. sup-
porters and the press should have mislaid
their love of fair play until after the polling.
[B. J. H. Owen/75 Glenhuntly Road, Elvvood, Vic.]
“Hamer” : The horrible old sport of hang-
ing has had a revival in N. S. Wales since
the formation of the National Government.
The Executive decided last week that James
Wilson, a hopeless degenerate by his appear-
ance, must hang for the murder of a cafe-
keeper in Sydney. No doubt it is on its
ability to march backward in this fashion
that the National Government bases its
claim to be “progressive.”
Speaking without bile Hughes informed
a reporter after the election that “Australia
had proved'worthy of the men of Pozieres
and Gallipoli.” This from the rancorous
little man looks fairly cheeky. Hughes should
fet into touch somehow with the men of
’ozieres and Gallipoli and find out just
how many of them voted against him.
There are a few hundred back in Australia,
and some well-chosen remarks about their
unworthiness of the country that proved
itself worthy of Phewsion might keep things
moving while the repatriation scheme
being dished up or fished up or whatever is
happening'to it.
“Wagga”: This is how Pat McGarry,
M.L.A., lately turned - Nationalist but still
anti-conscriptlonist, said a bit both ways for
the Senate Labor candidates during the
recent fight: “I know ’em well, they’re foine
min—the devil better. Watson is one of
them wowthers; Bolan’, he’s an anarchist,
and Rae, he’s an athiest. But I didn’t come
up to tell yez that.” A broth of a bhoy is
Pat!
The expected end has come to Neil Neil-
sen’s soft job In U.S., by the termination of
his appointment as representative of the N.
S. Wales Government. The office has been
costing the State £OOOO per anuum, so Neil-
sen (without his £4 a day) evidently had
some frills. There are a few other jobs that
the obliging Holman found for friends, etc.,
calling for the shears.
Politicians named “Bill” and “Joe” seem
to have an unholy partiality for each other
in these parts of the world. Massey and
Ward fused in Maoriland, and' now the
Commonwealth’s Joe and Bill have embraced
each other in the “tart shop.” The
Bulletin expects the proletariat to arise
and demand that at all future christenings
these names be bogus.
Robert Purnell, a wealthy coal-handler of
Newtown, Geelong.'s silvertail suburb, won
Bill Plain’s vacated seat in the Vie.
Assembly, after an easy canter around the
course in front of Laborite Thomas
McCormick. Some 8000 electors voted, Bob
getting 500 more than Tom. At the last
election (1014), Plain was returned unop-
posed as a Laborite, and in 1911 he polled
1500 ahead of the selected Liberal. In this
connection it is interesting to note that the
said selected Liberal was lawyer Alban
Morley. Shortly before the enlistment of
Ozanne, M.H.R. for Corio, which contains
the Geelong State electorate, Morley was
chosen as the Liberal candidate to oppose
Ozanne at the first opportunity; but as
soon as Ozanne joined up, Morley sportingly
withdrew from the contest. Since then
Morley’s name has never been mentioned in
connection with a Liberal selection. It was
evidently an offence to stand down for a
Laborite even if he was away winning the
war.
R. G. Nesbit, S.M., has caused a flutter
among South Australian hotelkeepers and
owners by a discovery he has made in the
Licensing Act. Coupling up one section with
another, he finds that, after two fines for
unlawfully supplying liquor, the license has
to be absolutely forfeited on a third offence,
and this no matter how many years have
elapsed between the three. The S.M. remarks
that this is “very drastic, and appears to
have. been overlooked by magistrates,
licensees and the general public.”
AT UNCLE'S FARM.
London, Wednesday.—The N. S. Wales Government
is issuing £3,000,000 of 5½ per cent, debentures at
the fixed price of 98. The amount has been under-
written.—Cable.
CHANTICLEER: “Behold, in answer to our
clarion call, the glorious suns arise
again."
Poor old “independent"’ candidate! This
time there were eleven of him —seven for the
Senate and four for the Reps. Several
boasted better-defined policies and a truer
Australian outlook than any of the party
men ; but every one of them was crushed so
flat that he lost his deposit. We have got.
down to “tank” politics all right.
WHERE CREDIT IS DUE.
VISITOR: “Oh, Mr. Stoney, I have enjoyed
your pictures so much. I think artists
deserve great credit."
MR. STONEY: “Yes, Miss, I'm sure we do;
hut we don't get it."
Only nine of the first Australian Senate
will remain at the old address after July 1.
Five of these are from Westralia —De
Largie, Henderson, Lynch, Needham and
Pearce. The others- are Milieu (N.S.W.),
Givens (Q.), Keating (Tas.) and Guthrie
(S.A.). Victoria has lost all of her original
six, and all of her original Reps, but two.
The anti-shouting crusade now being
pressed as a side-line in the off-season by the
Cold Tea crowd seems to be about the last
thing in plain futility. Booze may be all the
bad things it is accused of being; but the fact
that you can’t stop its use by introducing
a paltry scheme which even the dullest sort
of person can evade
as he pleases is clear
enough to suggest
that there must be
some deep-laid stra-
tegy behind the
clamor for “Jimmy
Woodserism.” Pos-
sibly the idea is that
by multiplying the
possibilities of law-
breaking for Bung
that unhappy person
may be more discred-
ited in the public
eye; and as a law
against shouting
would of necessity
be broken in every
pub some thousands
of times daily, the
reformers could go
out and cry at the
street corners: “See,
we have tried regula-
tion and you all
know how it has
failed!” This seems
the most reasonable
explanation of, a n
attempt by the ex-
perimental party to
push an alleged re-
form that they ought
to know is absurd
and unworkable. As
for the principle of
anti-shouting, it hits
hard at ordinary
social good feeling
and gets pretty close
to an attack at the
spirit of the men
who have fought and
are fight i n g our
battles. Mateship
exists in some form
in most lands; but
the tendency to run
in couples is more marked in Australia than
anywhere else,, and has played a definite part
in making the Australian character.
THE NEW RELIGION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A congregation which was bored simply could
not worship. How was congregational boredom to be
prevented? We heard much about brief, bright ser-
vices, but too many bright services meant a harlequin
in the pulpit and ragtime from the choir. By all
means let the services be as bright as it was possible
for human beings to make them. —New Moderator of
the Presbyterian Assembly.
Shun the sixty-minute sermon and the message drear
of doom;
Cease the everlasting harping on the nearness of the
tomb;
Open here and there a window, and let in the good
fresh air,
And don’t groan at “fellow-sinners’-’ when about to
offer prayer.
Oh, the modern type of parson he must never rant or
rail
If he wants to bash the Devil and tie hymn-books to
his tail;
For the modern congregation has got ragtime in its
bones,
And it hates the old salvation that is made of
threats and groans.
Let us change the mode of worship! Let us put the
thing to rights,
Stipulating that the singers must avoid the use of
tights;
And, of course, the preacher shouldn’t paint his nose
a brilliant red,
Or have whited limelight playing on his sleek and
reverend head.
While avoiding all such measures, we might somehow
yet contrive
To import some playhouse brightness and keep saint-
liness alivp;
But the wild, old-fashioned Devil he must plunge and
snort no more,
And we will not let him rattle heated hoofs upon
the floor!
Give the souls their soothing-syrup in a calm and
cultured way,
With some music that’s not ragtime, but is still
intensely gay;
Then impress upon old Satan that he may not howl
or scorch,
Or get firing penny rockets in the belfry or the
porch.
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The quality of horseflesh competing over
Sydney suburban middle-distance handicaps was testi-
monialised on Saturday, when the Rajah of Pudu-
kota s Burradoo led all the way in Canterbury Park
Handicap and ended up with three lengths of day-
light between his hindquarters and the next nag.
This happened to be Foil, now nothing like as good
as when he was winning decent events at Randwick.
Burradoo was kicked to the front by his apprentice
pilot, Warne, as soon as the tapes allowed. At one
stage the Rajah’s colors were 10 lengths ahead of the
others, and when the top of the straight was reached
there was still a break of 20 yards in their favor.
Warne therefore pulled his whip and punched his
not-too-reliable mount all the way down the straight.
He was taking no risks, Burradoo having never pre-
viously shown a liking for a distance or an argu-
mentative finish. Burradoo won this event in just the
same manner as little Coat o’ Mail has appropriated
similar events. Both animals are trained at Rand-
wick by Dick O’Connor, who must have a secret for
stealing marches. ~
* * v * *
Over 12 months ago Quinn’s Post (imp.)
secured the A.J.C. Challenge Stakes at Randwick.
Between that time and last Saturday he had not
worried the judge. Quinn’s Post is trained at Can-
terbury Park, and it was there he annexed Flying
Handicaps. The occasional pommy was favorite, and
after the first furlong led all the time, to scramble
home by a little less than half a length from Asia
Minor, a gelding which threatens to be unlucky in
so far as place-getting is concerned. His last two
starts have been to just get beaten. Quinn’s Post is
a fine-looking animal, and now that he has managed
to secure some money for oats may decide to further
help towards the cost of living. He was indented by
his trainer, P. Keith, and is by Martagon from
Isabel May.
* * * #
Three years back the writer had an idea
that a colt named Algous, by Tartan —Alga, then in
Joe Burton’s stable, would be pretty good. The colt
had his first run in a maiden event at Warwick Farm,
and although left lengths at the start wound up close
to the winner. But he never did anything on the
flat. On Saturday he won the second hurdle race of
his career. This was at Canterbury Park. The first
win was at Wagga a few weeks ago. Algous wasn’t
favorite on Saturday, but the fact didn’t prevent him
from winning with the bit between his teeth by eight
lengths from Compromise, which two weeks earlier
secured Moorefield Hurdle Race on a protest against
Kinlarlc. The latter was favorite on Saturday, but
made a holy show of himself.
* * * #
Norbury was expected by the hard-headed.
fraternity to take Newcastle Cup. No horse worked
better at Randwick during the week preceding the
race, and on the course he was solidly backed from
14’s down to B’s. All Norbury’s performances have
been away from Sydney, and among his good deeds
was second to Bazetta in last Armidale Cup, seven
furlongs. On the day following that defeat he
downed Bazetta. Norbury was prepared for New-
castle by Jimmy Barden, and was steered by the
trainer’s son. Probably the best performance in the
race was that of Secret Service, who lumped 9st. 21b.
into third place.
* * # *
“X”: Meeting with an accident in his pad-
dock at Dandenong (Vic.) the other day, the ancient
racing-pony Tommy the Nut was put out of his
misery with the aid of a friendly revolver. Thus
ended the career of a well-known performer with a
goodly list of wins to his credit. For many years
Tommy held the record pony prize, a member’s
handicap of £125. The old fellow (he had just
turned 32) started earning his corn as a two-year-old,
his first owner giving the then stiff price of £42 for
him.
* * * *
The tote has weighed out a rather remark-
able series of fat dividends in connection with the
S.A.J.C. Fisher Stakes, won by Scots Jean (a Pistol
mare) at the recent meeting. Martagon in 1900 paid
£67 Bs. In 1902 backers of Lome received £6l
18s.; Barr paid £2l 12s. in 1904 ; Masher £l5 16s.
the year following; Spinaway, £lO 13s. in 1906;
and Fiction, in 1909, £2l 12s. Five years ago
Phormio, who ran second to Braw Scot, paid £l3 19s.
* * * *
Scobie has been visiting Adelaide for Cup
meetings for 30 years, and Bobby Lewis for 20, but
they have seldom cleaned-up things more effectually
than last week. A. E. Hamilton’s Bourlang won the
Goodwood Handicap for S.A., but Lewis had a run
of three in the events not handicaps. Scobie’s own
Seabound won the S.A. Stakes (two-year-old) from
Hawker’s Mareca (much better fancied) ; Falkiner’s
Stageland took the Leger in a small field; finally
Mrs. Hawker’s handsome Cyklon, being in the right
humor, ran away from everything at weight for age,
Gold Cuffs being a bad second, but equally superior
to the rest. The Adelaide Cup was for once a really
good thing. Handicapped largely on his Sydney Cup
form, Timms’s Green Cap had for several weeks
stood out from the field on paper,- and when the
time came he did it on turf. He carried top weight,
9st. 61b., and did the mile-and-five-furlongs in record
time, 2min. 48tsee. Gold Cuffs, well backed, was
again in the unsatisfactory position of being an easy
winner if the actual winner had been away. It
was Lewis’s third Adelaide Cup, Eyeglass having
brought him home in 1911 and again the next year,
and Scobie’s fourth. J. Timms had starters in three
races, put up a different jockey each time, and vron
the treble.
* * » *
“Pungawerewere”: It took my memory
back a good many years when I read the other day
that John Marshall, of Auckland (M.L.). was giving
up racing—at least temporarily—through ill-health.
1-Ie has owned track horses for about 30 years in the
northern city. and is one of the whitest men at the
game. Amongst the best of nags which ever ran
in his name was that brilliant mare Necklace. She
and three other good horses, Thunderbolt, Derringer
and Turquoise, were raced by a syndicate of four,
Marshall, Tom Morrin (who, I believe, died not long
since in America),'James Dickey and Pierce Lanigan
(the contractor who built the Calliope Dock). That
was about 30 years ago. Morrin and Lanigan were
amongst the original shareholders of the Sylvia Park
stud, the proprietors of which secured the famous
stallion Musket, then obscured in the wilds of the
Waikato, no one dreaming at that time of the fame
he was to bring to the horse-breeding industry of
Maoriland. Sylvia was one of the first mares bought
by the new co., and she, mated with Musket, produced
the celebrated Martini-Henry, winner of the V.R.C.
Derby and Melbourne Cup. Necklace, a daughter of
M'-sket ard Locket, won any number of races in
M.L., including the Dunedin Jockey Club and Forbury
Handicaps, and ran second to Nelson for the Dunedin
Cup. She was trained by the veteran John Chaafe,
who rode in races about Svdney in the early days.
Amongst the horses John Marshall raced on his own
account was Necklet, winner of the Maribyrnong
Plate in 1888. It is characteristic of his liberality
that, being about to retire, he should give his horses
away. In this wav F. Stenning, his present trainer,
will secure Pearl Necklet and General Joffre.
* • * *
Reason is beginning to prevail at last
about race meetings in Maoriland, and the list of
meetings is to be carved down. The Government
should operate with a firm hand in the interests of
military service, the protection of the public and
ordinary decency. The idea that the restriction of
racing within reasonable compass means a concession
to wowserdom has prevailed far too long. If Maori-
lan4 is unable to get enough racing on, say, a
dozen racecourses, someone ought to inquire into
the reason, with a view to putting the remaining
score or two of tracks to better use. There is talk
about one result of fewer meetings being the transfer
of numerous prads to Australia. This may be so.
If it is Maoriland will lose nothing that cannot
be easily spared, and Australia will gain nothing the
Commonwealth would not be just as well without.
The volume of racing and betting in Maoriland has
for many years been out of all proportion to what
public policy should tolerate.
* * * *
The gelding Hopfield, a brilliant miler who
won at Randwick and Sydney suburban courses last
year and was subsequently sold to Maoriland, is
being put to hurdles over yonder. If he retains any
of his old pace between the places where he might
fall down Hopfield ought to escape catching a chill
anyway.
* # * *
“Merrigal”: Here are some more samples
of old-time N.S.W. endurance horses. Jack
Jones, of Merrigal, on a Little - John get,
rode the 90 miles from there to Cobbora
in eight and a half hours. Alec Black, of
Buttabone, on Darkie, covered the 100 miles separat-
ing him from Dubbo between sparrow-bark and nest-
time. Bay Adelaide transferred Sam Meirs from
Dick’s Camp to Dubbo, 60 miles (Irish at that), in
five hours. Hobart Pasha, with George Foley aboard,
travelled from Collie to Dubbo, 120 miles, in 11
hours. Tom Egan, with Daniel Canieron, of Buddah
Lake, Trnngie, to keep him from going to sleep
(Egan will go to sleep while you’re talking to him),
drove a pair of prads from Dubbo to Mount Harris,
107 miles, between dawn and sundown ; and Jim E.
Tonkin, once M.L.A. for Bathurst, piloted his five-
shilling purchase, Toby, with buggy attached, from
that pitch to Wellington and back, 200 miles, in
48 consecutive hours.
* * * *
“Nemo”: The Derby and Oaks coursing
fixture at Rooty Hill (N.S.W.) recently was as
full of surprises as a schoolgirl is of giggles.
- Favorite dogs went down with great regularity, and
there was much nasal rejoicing in Canaan over the
finest exposition of coursing seen for years. At the
first calling of the card Wild King was favorite for
the Derby, with Brown Hope so strong a tip for the
Oaks that outside of her a punter could almost name
his own price. Brown Hope promptly went down in
the first round before My Jewel, who lasted till the
third ties. The winner turned up in Symo, about
whom 50 to 1 • was mentioned at the calling of the
card. The Derby favorite, Wild King, lasted till the
third round was* reached, when the turn saw the flag
raised against him by Comedy Chief. Had Wild
King not experienced three very severe falls in his
course against Grand Crystal on the second day he
probably would have won the stake. He was so
sore from the knocking about that when he met
Comedy Chief he could scarcely raise a gallop.
Comedy Chief,’ who eventually won the stake, is a
fawn-and-white puppy of more than average size,
whelped in October, 1915. The runner-up, Mick
Barden, a Roman-nosed black dog, was the outsider
in every course he ran. Someone must have cleaned
up a tidy bit with him.
The meeting was also remarkable for the terrific
slaughter of the hares, 26 being killed. They were
too fat to run away to safety. The loss of these well-
nourished animals knocked the idea of running the
Australian Waterloo Cup meeting there next month
on the head. The Waterloo will have to be decided at
Werribee (Vic.).
* * * *
Frank Hewitt, an old-time professional
champion ped., finished the 73rd lap last
week. He, with Bird and Topley, arrived
in Melbourne on December 16, ’69, under
George Coppin’s management, and made his
first appearanc e on Melbourne Cricket Ground, March
5 and 7, ’7O, his opponent being an amateur (a
saddler by trade), one J. G. Harris. The 100, 150,
200, 300, 440 honors were evenly divided, Harris
winning two, Hewitt two, the fifth (300) was a
dead-heat.
* * * *
“W.A.M.”: Bill was hard at it in the
snow to-day struggling with a bit of timber (pinched
from an engineer’s dump) a few yards from his
dugout, when he heard one of the Huns’ H.E. shells
coming over. He sprinted for the cellar, but -the
going wasn’t good. The shell beat him badly and
blew the happy home skvhigh, while the runner was
at a safe distance. Bill tcok a long breath. “Thank
Gawd,” he said, “I ain’t Postle!”
* * * *
“Victo”: Heel-and-toe? Well, my uncle,
in 1888, walked from Back-creek station, outside
Trunkey, leaving there at 10 Monday morning, to
Rigsdals homestead on Sydney road near Goulburn,
arriving there at six Tuesday evening. Total, 108'
miles in 32 hours. And mother recalls that when
she was eight years old the familv in Queensland
walked 90 miles in 12 days, and arrived in civilisa-
tion three days before her brother was born, and
granny was none the worse for the exercise either.
“The Immature Male”: A few weeks ago
the battered old town of El Arish (Abdul s late
stronghold on the jSinai Peninsula) seemed as far
and inaccessible as Berlin. But things move swiftly
these days, and under Routine Orders Billjim was
warned that “owing to dangerous currents, surf-
bathing in the vicinity of El Arish would be for-
bidden if troops ventured more than 25 yards from
the shore.” The brown surfers borrowed a lot of leg-
ropes from the camelry, made a rough life-line and
presently the breakers were full of men wearing only
identification discs, as the quarter-master s stores
didn’t include any neck-to-knees. It is considered
quite the .thing for the ladies and gentlemen of
surrounding villages to promenade along the shores
of Tina Bay and watch Billjim having a dip between
battles.
* * * *
Bessie Grandmange, one of the Botany
family of sprinters, fled over 220yds. on Saturday
before the watch had got beyond 29 4-ssec., thus chip-
ping a tick off the best time previously registered by a
woman. But Bessie didn’t do this in a pair of gaudy
high-heeled boots and with a lot of wild animal’s
skin hung round her neck, nor was she tied up in
corsets or wearing a collection of vegetables and
poultry skewered on top of her head. She wore her
brother’s spiked shoes and a sprinters garment, all
of which, of course, detracts from the merit of an
otherwise remarkable performance. What this page
yearns to hear about is the sample of the feminine
gender who in ordinary marching kit can run 220
yards in less than 30 minutes without fainting in the
first lap, and demanding to know if her blouse fits
at the neck.
* • # *
“Yarra Ben”: One of the sports who
rolled up to enlist in the Sportsmen’s Thousand in
Melbourne a day or two ago described himself as an
“urger and guesser.” The definition puzzled the
enlisting-officer, who requested further particulars.
The would-be soldier thereupon explained that he
was a racecourse “professional,” who guessed pro-
bable winners and urged other people to back them.
The recruit got through all right, but not on his
turf credentials. He happened to be an expert sculler
as well as a guesser and urger.
* * # *
Thus a Seotchbyterian at Sydney the other
day, orating to the brethren about imposing upon
all amusements the condition that they must be
“without sin” and “without excess”: “Then there
was prize-fighting, erroneously called boxing. The
police told him that the thing was disgusting. The
language of the patrons was blasphemous, and the
air reeked with impurity. It allowed free play to
the lowest passions in the human nature. It was
creating a wrong impression of heroism, and was
producing shirkers without number.” This would
be fairly hot stuff coming from anyone who knew
anything about his subject. Coming from a. man
whose stock of information depends upon what he
was told by an anonymous John Hop, it misses the
mark by a mile. Boxing is as legitimate a form
of pastime as a bun-devouring competition. There
may be many harsh things to say at times about
some of its aspects, but it seems to this writer that
saying them with a fulness of knowledge is better
business than yelling a lot of uncorroborative trash.
To say that “the language of the patrons is blas-
phemous and the air reeks with impurity” is as
wildly absurd as the further remark that “boxing
produces shirkers without number.” Where is the
evidence of this? The Rev. Mr. McTavish has got
a bee in his bonnet! There is more blasphemy to
be heard in an hour in the vicinity of a political
meeting during periods of public excitement than
can be detected at the Sydney Stadium in a month —
the air of many places where men labor at tasks
which minister to the comfort and convenience of
Mr. McTavish reeks with an impurity that all the
disinfectants on earth would not remove. Mr.
McTavish is listening with the wrong ear, or a John
Hop has been pulling his leg.
* * * *
When Greek met Greek in the Spartan
days of old at the trial of skill and strength there
was something to excite the populace of Athens to
take a holiday, but when Mexico met Ould Oireland
at Sydney Stadium on Saturday, represented by Mr.
Benny Palmer and Mr. Matt Murphy respectively,
there was nothing to excite anything except a hope-
fulness that they would not transpire any more. Mr.
Murphy’s reserves of wallop were too far behind the
firing-line, and as the Mexican dropped a barrage
they got no further. When in the 17th round the
neighbor of Uncle Sam violently pushed a sleeping-
draught under Matt’s chin and the weary youth asked
for no more, somebody noticed that the war was over.
It was Benjamin’s first win in Australia.
* * * *
A further instalment of the Sid Godfrey-
Wave Geike serial was presented at Brisbane on
Saturday. It was a one-sided affair. Godfrey
sprawled tli# stolid Geike for nine in the thirteenth
melee and infected him with the staggers during the
,next two. • Then the referee seasoned justice with
mercy, and passed Sid the verdict. As Waverlev con-
tracted a wife only the week before, a couple of
friends went home with him to prove his identity.
* * IT *
Another of the Old Brigade of East
Melbourne cricketers has gone home. Dan Wilkie
was one of four brothers all of whom were prominent
players. Originally a slow round-arm bowler, he was
provoked by the doings of Chris. Tinley, the Eng-
lishman, to bowl underhand, and soon became an
exoert in the delivery of lobs. In the 10 years, from
’65 to ’75, he secured 515 wickets at an average of
7.95. In one match against Northern Tasmania he
took 18 wickets for 28 runs. He also played against
N. S. Wales. Wilkie was a good hitter, and scored
the first century (124) ever made for East Mel-
bourne. When not playing or talking cricket he
pursued Costs.
* # * *
“0.K.”: The present generation of Mel-
bourne knew not Dan Wilkie as the famous lob
bowler and batsman who won matches for East Melb.
50 yeai’s ago, but he was widely known as,a rather
severe-looking and well-gmomed citizen who walked
about town with a swelling of fat —a huge swelling
at the last —on the right side of his iaw\ The lump
had been growing more and more unsightlv for about
a quarter of a century, and it was naturally supposed
to be an unavo ; dable' disfigurement—something that
couldn’t be safely removed. At length developments
made it advisable to risk the knife. The operation
proved to be quite a simple matter, and -two days
after being relieved of his ancient burden the patient
was sitting up. But he was already a man of 70-odd
—much older than he looked —and the shock of the
belated operation and the incidental dieting were too
much. He gradually weakened and died within two
or three months.

J. M. Jamieson, of Junee (N.S.W.), killed
in action with the A.I.F. in trance, was one of the
Ma State’s crack rifleshots, and frequently com-
peted on the mounds at championship meetings in
other States. He won the first stage of the King’s at
Bisley (Eng.) in 1914.
“Tam o’ Shanter” : There’s no use talking
about the distance to aim in front of a hurrying
Sunday dinner if you don t know your gun. Com-
paratively the speed of guns vary from Donaldson's
pace to that of George Reid’s. The best rule to
follow is to find what your cannon can do, allow
accordingly, and don’t depend on mathematics or the
Religious Editor.
* # » *
“Eryander”: I cannot controvert “Cylin-
der” (B. 10/5/’ 17) about the shot-speed claimed by
cartridge-makers, but the figure I gave is set forth
in Gardiner’s “Ballistics,” Tranter's “Ratios of Pro-
pulsion,” and elsewhere, as the mean result of a
long series of experiments. As regards comparison
with rifle-bullet speed, it is theoretically quite possi-
ble for the lighter projectile to have a higher initial
velocity than the heavier. A cork thrown hard from
the hand would travel the first few yards much
faster than would a stone of the same size, though
the stone could be thrown 10 times as far as the cork.
The well-known authority, J. J. Dougall, in Shoot-
ing, writes: “A carrier-pigeon can fly four miles five
furlongs in four minutes; about 102 ft. a second.
With a distance of 40 yards, the aim taken at a
bird crossing the shooter at that speed should be
more than five feet in advance, the flight of the
shot to that distance requiring l-19th of a second.”
As for the speed of the black-cheek, he differs only-
in color from the peregrine, and the latter —the
ascertained fastest of all birds —has been timed,
when making his dash, at up to 130 ft. a second; a
rate of over 88 miles an hour.
* * * *
“Alfonso” : Now then, stop fishing for
those sardines and bring your lines over here to the
Western rivers of N. S. Wales. A few ordinary
samples of what may happen: In ’93 a farmer named
Lake, then living at Dripstone, near Wellington,
hauled a giant from the Macquarie. It weighed
841 b. About eight years ago writer saw a 511 b.
fish caught near Wellington by a man from Orange.
The fish at that stage occupied a cornsack, its tail
protruding from one end, and its head from the
other. Thirty years ago writer saw two fish (481 b.
and 521 b.) caught in the Abercrombie. Although
fish of 201 b. and thereabouts are fairly common
catches in these rivers (I caught one of
221 b. last week), there are very few fish of excep-
tional size captured nowadays. The reason, I think,
is the increased use of nets by greedy and impatient
fishermen. If people persist in this practice, which,
by the way, is prohibited in these rivers, there will
soon hardly be a decent-sized fish left in the fresh-
water streams. Eight or ten summers ago fish up
to 1001 b. were not uncommon. The writer has some
fairlv-correct, though otherwise inadequate, reports
of numerous fish of this weight caught in the
Murrumbidgew ’ These he knows not to be phantom
fish. Several times such monsters have been sus-
pended for show in front of fish-shops in Wagga.
There is no great variety of fish in our streams,
but it is doubtful whether a better fish than our
fresh-water cod can be found anywhere in the world.
The catfish is a long way ahead of the salt-water
cat, provided his overcoat is removed before cook-
ing. But the useless, bony, bait-robbing carp is a
thing accursed.
* # # *
“F. J.”: The reminiscences of these sprat-
catchers at the ’Awkesbury make me tired. What
do they do to fill in time waiting for the next bite?
If you want real fishing tie yourself to a heating
apparatus and some whusky and get over to the Bluff,
in Maoriland, and see about blue cod. This inhabi-
tant of the wet can only be caught by handline.
One time when it happened to be fine over yonder
for a week, we steamed 25 miles to the cod grounds
and back o’ nights. For six consecutive days five
of us totalled 7 tons of fish. Why do these bream
and whiting trappers talk so much instead of catch-
ing something ?
* * * • ♦
“Boro-Belle”: For the most eiciting of
moonlight sports let me nominate turtle-stalking in
North Queensland. The track can be anywhere along
the milky-white beaches of the coastal towns within
the Great Barrier Reef. The first event generally
starts just about an hour before high water, when
the turtles generally emerge from the sea and waddle
gingerly up the beach to lay eggs. A real heavy-
weight would turn the scales at anything from a
quarter to half a ton, and would require at least three
pairs of hands to turn it over. Even then the effort
is great. A Malieoola islander of my acquaintance
slipped, while trying the job alone, and the forward
whack from the turtle’s flapper broke his arm, and
the return movement shifted his nose to the back of
his head. Of the two species of turtle to be found
in the north the shell-turtle has the highest commer-
cial value, and rarely goes over 4cwt., while the
big greenback, from which the soup is made, goes to
nearlv three-quarters of a ton.

The Passport to Good Health.

tissue.

oxygen, deep breathing should be the foundation of eveiy
system of exercise.

from oxygen starvation —and yet they wonder why they are
fatigued, depressed, under weight—why their vital organs
fail in their duty.

Why Look Old ?
You need not use hair dye to conceal grey hair.
Grey hair adds years to anyone’s appear-
ance. The objections to dyeing the hair are
too numerous and too obvious to mention
hew people are aware that a simple, old-
iashioned recipe, that can be made up at
home, will quickly turn grey hair back to a
natural color without injuring the hair 'in
any way. One has only to get two ounces
of concentrate of tammalite from the chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum
With a small sponge apply the lotion' to the
hair for a few nights and the desired change
vvill have been neatly accomplished. This
simple formula has given great satisfac-
tion for generations to those in possession of
it.—AdVT. rq

About three-fifths of the capital was written
off; but, as it turned out, the writing-off was
a bit too severe, so that when the assets
were subsequently sold fair profits were
available for reserves. That makes the 10
p.c. dividend possible, notwithstanding that
there is only £33.620 of deposits and other
money belonging to the public in the busi-
ness. Practically everything is now out in
advances, as the following comparison
shows: —
. , July, ’Ol. Jan.,’l6. July,’l6. Jan.,’l7.
Liabilities ..£37,015 £37,768 £35,225 £33,620
Advances .. 19,998 117,226 119,251 118,251
Foreclosures . . 72,305 7,937 3,780 3,765
Cash, etc. . . 2,778 3,192 3,540 3,487
Because the deposits are so small by com-
parison .with the assets, the depositors’
security is excellent; and for the same reason
the shareholders’ risks are comparatively
small. But it seems very doubtful whether
under normal conditions it will be possible
to pay the present dividend and put by a
decent pmount for contingencies unless the
co. can get' hold of more business—which,
of course, means taking more risks with
other people’s money. And the chances of
active building-society business for some
years to come don’t look brilliant.
* * * *
The late Mount Morgan D’Arcy appears
to have been the first Britisher to invest
money in the Persian oil industry. He
acquired concessions from the Shah’s Govern-
ment, and was said to have spent £20,000 in
what threatened to be fruitless endeavors to
develop the property. When lie was moon-
ing around London with his Persian white
elephant on his hands he met Greenaway,
a man who, knew the game and had been
instrumental in ousting the Standard Oil
Trust from India. His negotiations with
DArey resulted in the Burmah Oil Co.
(whereof Greenaway was a director) acquir-
ing a two-thirds interest in the Persian spec.
The Anglo Persian Oil Co. was formed, and
the late Lord Strathcona induced the
British Government to take rather more
than a half share in the concern, on the
understanding that the Navy was to have
first call on the oil supplies as soon as they
should be available. As things w r ere going
before the war it seemed practically certain
that Germany would eventually have been
in a position to take over those oil wells.
The Deutscher Bank had some splendid land
concessions in Asia Minor, and the scheme
was and is, to have German colonies along
the Bagdad Railway route, with the ultimate
capture of Mesopotamia in view. The
acquiescence of the Young Turk party in
Bank policy can be seen in
the fight which the Turks are putting up in
opposition to the British advance, now that
it has reached as far as Bagdad. Germany
and Turkey will stand or fall together in
Asia Minor, and it is Turkey that will stand
practically all the- loss of life.
* * * *
According to V. S. Shallcross, who read a
paper before the recent Kalgoorlie confer-
ence, the abuse of booze was the cause of
much of the wastage and inefficiency in
the Westralinn mining industry. He
gave a couple of illuminating instances.
Out of 000 men employed underground in the
Great Boulder during the first quarter of
this year, an average of 34 absented them-
selves for three days after each fortnightly
pay. At the now idle Kyarra mine. Meeka-
tharra, where an average of 65 men were
formerly employed, no less than 74 had to be
dismissed in 12 months for drunkenness.
Shallcross considered that local mine-owners
should take a leaf out of the Yankee steel
corporations’ book and make total abstin-
ence, both on and off duty, a condition of
employment; but, as someone pointed out, if
this was done there would be practically no
labor for the mines at all.
* * * *
As Mt. Bischoff (Tas.) is a smelting as
well as a mining co., it is in a better posi-
tion than most other cos. to do well out
of tin at 2s. per lb. Last half-year sales of
tin averaged only £175 per ton, and the
present price is £5O ahead of that. Biscboff’s
smelter turned out in six months 151 tons
of refined tin from the eo.’s ore and 1008
tons of metal from purchased ore. The
quantity of crudes treated in six months was
about 50,000 tons, which compares with
double that quantity years ago. The new
crushing plant, equipped with the latest tin-
saving appliances, is practically ready for
use.
* * * *
“McLirr”: A case interesting to miners
was recently heard in the Warden’s Court,
Mount Morgan (Q.), when an application
was made by Conrad Hotz for a prospecting
area of 160 acres, near Camboon. The
Warden decided that there was no doubt
from the evidence that Hotz had been in
possession; _ but as to title, though he had
complied with the regulations in respect of
three pegs, the application "ould not be
granted, as the north-eastern peg was repre-
sented by. the stump of a growing tree. The
same decision was given in a similar case
in Victoria some years ago.
* * * #
Last year was a bad year for most N.S.W.
colliery cos., but Seaham (Newcastle) was
able to increase its dividend from 5 p.c. to
6 p.c. per annum. The net profit for 12
months was £11,470, and the dividend
absorbed all but £7O of that.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (.except of a hostile character ) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Iota: What on earth has that long ramble
got to do with the plain quotation from an English
magazine which tells its own tale? The man who
wrote the brief comment came from Ulster, and
knows the position as well as y0u.... Chock-a-log:
Rambling description of nothing... .D.E.: “Out in
the Rain” doesn’t score... .B.B.: The men who
fought at Gallipoli hardly deserve that....
J.M.S.; It would take outstanding genius to avoid
disaster in that cramped metre... .T.J.M.: Letting
“down the sliprails of the grave” by a person on
the other side of the hole is distinctly unusual....
L.E.8.: Crude, but some promise. .. .R.8.K.: Very
lame verse... .J.H.: “Evening Shades” lacks force
and novelty, but otherwise decently done. .. .M.S.:
No. The person who rhymes “josser” with “Bar-
barossa” goes out on his cold, pink ear... .Fernleaf:
It-’s some distance ahead of the last you sent, but
you’ve a long road to travel 5ti11.... D.E.: Your
bloodthirsty desires must learn to squirm in better
verse... .W.G.0.: Alleged poem rejected. It starts as
blank verse, but fuddles, over into bad prose at the
' fourth line.. . .G.S.: “Bad News” doesn’t quite con-
vince. .. .C. 11.: Long way round to a very small
yarn R.V.C.: Not quite... .C.A.M.: Verse setting
too poor. .. .R.8.T.: Don’t choose such a difficult
measure. It wants a lot of skill to keep it on the
track.... M.TI.H.: You “feel the strength of mad-
ness” in your veins. That’s our own sensation
when you mix ancient and modern verbs in that
chaotic fashion., . .V.F.K.: The words are better
than the thought.. . .G. 8.: Deadly du11... .S.S.: The
office murderer laced his whiskers and started on your
track-.... Tom R.: Come right in:—
Out all night and every night 3 immovable sisters,
Admired every day, by Misses, Mrs. and Misters.
Silence in woman? Unknown except when sound
asleep;
But, these 3 sisters were never known to speak.
Silence is golden, but rocks and walls have ears,
And golden things are scarce in this vale of tears.
The moral in these lines is just as clear as day,
Believe all you see, half you read, be dubious of
hear’say,
....Maltese: It’s too wild. The thing wouldn’t
stay on the table while the vacuum-cleaner whizzed
round... .R.N.T.: Bomb-throwing is a fine art. Your
best endeavor only amounts to hurtling an exhausted
cat....Le Noy: Dreary effort.... C.S.: “Contem-
porary Portraits” looks like a lot of rhymes that got
hydrophobia and ran amok.... Ekoorb : Oh, go away.
You can’t make a noise like that here... .G.S.: Not
interested in a mechanical manufacture of heather
out of ill-fitting classic properties. .. .No Name: The
fact that you can’t understand, Bowden’s verse is
merely your misfortune. .. .M.C.D.: Heaven forgive
y0u.... J.S.M.: An idea; but verse not up....
Alfonso: Small joke; miserable handling... .K,F.:
But who is to say the invention of soap wasn’t as
great as the discovery of the telephone, or that the
man who first lighted a fire was inferior to the man
who discovered radium ?.... Cham: Good idea, if
lines didn’t fall back on old tags to get the final
rhyme.. . .R.L.J.: We believe you when you say that
you wrote them in a minute or two. If you had
spent 10 minutes to achieve that result the offence
would have been multiplied 10 times. .. .8.5. A.: The
person who persists in rhyming “dawn” with “morn”
ought to be compelled to listen to Joe Cook speak-
ing.... S.L.: There’s some cheer in it: —
The froth was high and white;
The beer was gleaming bright;
He turned it once around,
And held it to the light.
He woke and slowly rubbed his bleary eye,
And to the copper said: “Lor’ blime! I’m dry!”
. ...5.8.: Any bookseller anywhere in any country
will order for a customer any book published, if you
specify the title, name of author and publisher.
The procuring of books happens to be the book-
seller’s job. Curious that you don’t know that....
J.C.W. Spakesheare: The copyright of a song pub-
lished in Australia belongs to the author, and there-
fore, presumably, includes the title, which could only
be used as the title of a play by the consent of the
owner of the copyright. However, there are titles of
songs, consisting of words or phrases in common use,
to which the author of a song could hardly claim
copyright. Anyhow, why not be original enough to
title your play without borrowing?. .. .Miss E.H.:
No; too delicate.... Marjorie: “Clasped to her
bosom,” etc., would go perhaps if the Religious
Editor wasn’t away converting a cannibal....
Jas. Paterson: Now, honestly, do you think that sort
of thing is worth a man’s thought '/.... Wolseley:
Received clipping, from S. M. Herald, that a man
lias died aged 106. If you know anything interest-
ing about him, write it.... Anne: No; it is not
suitable for publication in The Bulletin. .. .E.T.11.
(Maldon) : “Official Information” no use to us.
TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
E.S.: Too crude to be seriously considered....
F.C.H.: Terrible exhibit.. . .H.W.8.: Too many
. gravestones... .M.P.: Bumps and lumps... .E.A.S.:
The grim truth is that you can’t draw... .Bill: Very
gloomy stuff... .C. 8.: You’re in the nursery stage
yet. ... W.P.: Everything and everybody out of per-
spective. .. .H.S.P.: Where’s the point?
“Green Tape” reports a collapse:—
The slide for life over the Norton Griffiths pact
has given the N. S. Wales Public Works Department
the staggers. All railway construction now goes
over to the Railway Commissioners. “Joe” Davis,
the Director-General of Public Works, is respon-
sible for this. Thinking himself a cert, for
the job of Railway Commissioner, he last
year transferred a large slice of his Department to
the Railway Department. Now with the N.-G.
scheme gone bung another big slice from Works
moves, and Davis has to follow as Director-General
of Railway Construction, with the Commissioners
between him and the Minister. Now Minister for
Works Ball finds himself with an emasculated
department, and though he is Minister for Railways,
the Chief Commissioner is supreme except in matters
of high policy. To give Ball an excuse for drawing
his big salary it is proposed to make him Lord of
the Water Board (an idea which strikes the Board
as foolish) and High Priest of Sydney . Harbor Trust
(a proceeding which the Commissioners, regard as an
outrage). As for the rank and file, they dunno what
is about to happen to them. Some have been jerked
over to the Railways, some had been handed over to
Norton Griffiths and have to be taken back, and
others will have to bring up in strange places. All
have been guaranteed their jobs, but no one knows
where.

IN BANANA LAND
There is no accounting for votes. Some of
the strongest Labor constituencies in Queens-
land voted in favor of the retention of the
Legislative Council, despite the fact that
their local Labor M.L.A. was busy advocat-
ing its abolition. Bulimba, a red-hot Labor
stronghold, gave a vote of 3242 for extermi-
nating the Fossil Chamber, while 6112 voted
to give the Councillors for the first time in
their existence the evidence that they are in
power by the voice of the people.
The new’ meat rates as fixed between the
Queensland Cabinet and the Imperial Gov-
ernment are: Ox-beef, 4 7-Bd. per lb.; cow-
beef, 4 5-Sd. The State butchers’-shops, how-
ever, will be supplied at 3d. per lb. for chilled
or fresh beef; and frozen beef for 3jd. per lb.
The differentiation between these two prices
is even greater than it was last year; but, of
course, the claim of the man at the Front to
cheap meat is as nothing compared with the
c-laim of the stay-at-homes.
Gone to the Great Laboratory, William J.
Trouton, Brisbane’s most conspicuous
chemist and a good sport. Away back in
’Bl he arrived from County Armagh, Ireland,
in Bananaland, and a couple of years later
his pills started making the corner of Queen
and George Streets famous. A few years
ago he retired for a while, but the calomel
called again, and up till the death knock
he was distributing mixtures. Trouton was
a keen judge and a great lover of nags, and
in his time owned some of the best horse-
flesh in the State.
“The Bell-ringer’s” news :
Brisbane rubbed its eves the other day when it
read that Father Kelly, S.S.M., was to preach in
the local Anglican cathedral. It was all right. Kelly
is an Anglican, and is known as “Father,” according
to Anglican usage, because he is not a “secular”
but a “religious”—a member of one of the semi-
monastic orders which have sprung up in the Angli-
can Church since the “Catholic” revival started at
Oxford by Newman and others 80 years ago. Kelly’s
order —the “Society of the Sacred Mission’’ —was
founded by himself, and includes a well-
known training college at Kelham (Eng.),
where sons of more or less poor wage-
earners who wish to wear the clerical collar are given
a general education equal, and a professional training
much superior, to that which the wealthy classes buy
at the big public schools and universities. Just now
A FAVORITE.
THE MANAGER: “The public will miss you now you have left the stage"
ACTOR: “ That's why I left — I dislike being hit."
the college is closed, and all the students are wearing
khaki, so Father Kelly has been lending a hand to
the Anglican missions in Japan. Archgaiters Donald-
son recently beckoned him across to help a special
winter rally, and he will find a few of his old
students passing the plate in Bishop Feetham’s
diocese at the back of Townsville.
“Boro Belle”: The numerous islands off
the North Queensland coast, from Broad-
sound up, offer splendid opportunities for
fruit and vegetable farming. There is one
island, 10 miles from Mackay, that produces
excellent, tomatoes, pines, citrus fruits and
Swede turnips; while Green Island, a further
10 miles north, has yielded splendid samples
of English potatoes. Lindeman Island, 30
miles further north, is famed for its large-
sized oranges and bananas, besides its mutton
and wool. Fresh-water springs abound in
most of the islands, but, anyhow, there’s a
plentiful supply at hand 15 feet down. The
Government steamers from Mackay, Towns-
ville and Cairns already carry supplies to
the neighboring lighthouses and island
squatters, so communication with the main-
land on a bi-weekly service could easily be
established. For building there are millions
of feet of pine available. At least a couple
of thousand settlers could be comfortably
accommodated in these fertile dots.
Saddled and Bridled.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It’s a wondrous world we live in, and our casual
fellow man
Is a curious kind of study, be he black or white or
tan;
And the task of all reformers is to drive the world
along
In a way that’s straight and narrow from a road
that’s wide and wrong;
But the problem tliat our pundits cannot solve and
cannot burk,
And the problem that is setting every honest brain
to work
la to run (he country rightly by a just and golden
rule
When the Hogue is in the Saddle and the Saddle’s
on the Fool.
For the worldly-wise are mighty and they boast a
ready crew
Who, with sycophantic eagerness, their slightest bid-
ding do;
And the Rogue who rides the jackass is as sharp as
any pin,
And he rides for pelf and glory through a fatted
land of sin,
While the Fool who bears the saddle, though he’s
sound in back and wind,
13 but feeble hi tile region where he ought to keep
his mind ;
How to mend it, how to end it—-this would puzzle
any school
When the Hogue is in the Saddle and the Saddle’s
on the Fool.
For the saddle’s very weighty and the brains of him
below
Are pondering on the journey and the road he’s
got to go.
He’s been used to bear the saddle thus for many a
weary year,
And to lose it would upeet him and disorganise his
gear.
ne thinks the Lord intended him to spring beneath
the spur
And if a rider mount on him then why should he
demur ?
So he buckles to the burden like a workman to the
tool—
Oh, a Rogue will fit the Saddle when the Saddle’s
on the Fool.
And it makes a mortal ponder on the curious scheme
of things—
Is it going to last for ever or till mortals borrow
wings ?
Is the half of poor Creation for the bit and stirrup
born ?
Is its table but a manger with a meed of chaff and
com?
Can we fight it, can we right it? If we pull the
villain down
Can we keep a second villain from remounting on
the clown?
You will always find a master for the man who’s
half a mule
And a Rogue to fit the Saddle when it’s placed upon
the Fool.
And the creeds of all the Ages—-some of Love and
some of Fear—
Though we know them great and potent, yet they
seem to shatter here;
’Tis the same in ancient China, ’tis the same in old
Japan,
’Tis the same in frigid Britain and in torrid
Turkestan;
If half the earth is callous to the feeling of its shame
And the other half is careless to the guilt upon its
name,
Is it possible to free us from our misery and dool
When the Rogue is in the Saddle and the Saddle on
the Fool?
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, May 12, 1917.
My Deab Thabgomindah,—
His Ex. and Lady Goold Adams have been
getting a touch of the anxiety that parentage
is heir to. A few days ago their son
developed pneumonia; and her Ex. tied
hastily. back to Dalveen to be with him.
Latest reports say that his strong constitu-
tion is getting the trouble down and that
he will soon be well.
The Mayor and Mayoress of Adelaide
(Aid. and Mrs. Isaacs) have been spending a
few days in Brisbane. Yesterday our Best
people were mustered by Mayoress iletlier-
ingtou at the Council Chambers, to be intro-
duced to the visitors over a c-up of afternoon
tea. On the previous day the two Mayor-
esses had bustled round the various Red
Cross depots and had a look in at the
Women’s Mutual Service Club.
John Ashburn, squatter, has heard the
call of the war-horn and gone into camp.
Before doing so he sold his run. Rocklea sta-
tion ; and Mrs. Ashburn and family will live
at Toowoomba until John comes marching
home.
Sister B. L. Gibbon, a Brisbane girl, is
one of the nurses on whom the Royal Red
Cross was conferred in February. She left
here with the No. 1 A.G.H., and was for a
while at Heliopolis. After that she w T as
on transport duty and then went to Netley.
Now she is back in France with her original
unit. Another of the Gibbon girls is matron
of the military hospital at Rabaul.
The knitting-needles in this State have
been going at top speed during recent
months. The year’s output shows 20,742
pairs of socks to the credit of the Soldiers’
Sock Fund, which held its annual meeting
this week. Miss Philp, who is hon. treas.,
mentioned also a bank balance of £163 18s.
Over £l6OO has been spent on raw material
during the 12 months.
Mrs. R. L. Gordon managed a coin-and-
gift afternoon at her home at East Bris-
bane on Thursday. As a result, our men
overseas will benefit to the extent of £l2 ss.
in cash and 10 guineas’ worth of tinned
stuff.
Fairholme, the Cameron place on the
Range at Toowoomba, has been sold to the
Queensland Scotchbyterian Church' for a
girls’ college. There are 10 acres of ground
and the price was £5000; but, as Mrs.
Cameron handed half the proceeds back to
the college, Elspeth and .Tean seem to have
scored mightily in the transaction.
Phyllis Peberdy took her pretty person
and good contralto voice to the altar last
Saturday. The man who got these gifts was
Seigfried Benjamin.
Mrs. Lohde has her little son Rolf back
again now, after nearly two years of anxiety
and suspense. Pastor Trenz, acting on the
father’s instructions, had placed the child
with a German family at Toowoomba; and
before the mother could recover her offspring
Pastor Trenz and Dr. Lohde had to be
dragged back from a camp in N. S. Wales
for the purposes of the case.
There is no sign of patriotic effort slack-
ing off. As a result of a “tin” tea in aid
of the Soldiers’ Comforts Fund, huge stacks
of canned eatables are awaiting the trails '
port which will place them where they are
awaited. For 2nd L.II. Comforts Fund,
Sirs. ,7. S. P. Bourne, whose son, Lt.-Col.
Bourne, has lately added a D.S.O. to his war
souvenirs, gave a fete at her home at Clay-
field to-day, with satisfactory results.
R. W. T. Kendrick (who holds on to the
public moneybags of Papua) and his wife
spent a few days in Brisbane this week, and
then passed southward to visit friends.
Miss Macfarlane. a charming American
visitor, gave a farewell tea yesterday in Miss
Vera Ramee’s studio, before leaving for the
South.
On Friday night the Rev. J. B. Cosh,
while delivering an address on social reform,
"caused his hearers to sit up and take notice.
He referred to a mysterious “crime” whiei
a State politician had recently committei
and which the press was carefully smother-
ing. A reporter stayed behind after the
meeting and begged for details; but beyond
saying darkly that the “crime” took place
on' a Friday, Cosh, 8.A., was as silent as
the Sphinx. Everybody is now asking who,
what, and why.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“E.F.H.”: —
A pur. about the vanishing Thursday Island fleet
(B. 5/4/’I7) makes my thoughts fly north. A man
who might have written a sheaf of stories was Cap-
tain Pym, the skipper of the last Three Cheers schooner
mentioned in Tjie Bulletin. When the seven fleets
were working in the Aru Islands (as the Celebes
Trading Co.) the old Sydney Belle replaced her.
The Tea Chest, as she was locally known, and the
Alice were the last of the fore-and-afters. The
Sydney Belle had had a pretty lurid career in the
South Seas and was a new departure at the pearling
game, being rigged as a barquentine. She was
rechristened the Torres when she was brought up to
the Dutch Indies Worn Sydney by Pym. He went
down in the Australian trawler Endeavor, so I
suppose his fine collection of yarns has gone with
him. They were as good and as typical of pearling
as Connolly’s are of the Newfoundland fishing and
its schooners. Mention of the Alice brings to mind
those other fine craft in the Arus. Most graceful
and fastest of the lot was the Ariel, which once-did
a trip from the Arus' to Cape Don, 410 knots, in 40
hours. She was designed by one of her owners, and
w'as a double topsail schooner, and could leave them
all on a slog to windward. The Aladdin, a topsail
schooner, was once a yacht that always carried a
trysail at anchor to steady her. If the old Bothwell
Castle could have seen her roll she would have sunk
with envy. The Aladdin once rolled right round.
Her whale boat was full next morning to prove it.
Her crew didn’t mind that; what worried them was
that she might not have enough water under her if
she did it again, and get her topmast either foul or
bioken off. Among the many fine hands at a tiller
up there, that grand old man, Steve Clark, skipper
of the Alice, stood alone. A game of his was to
win a match with his boat, then double the Stake
and resail after changing boats. He went to Sydney
to see the first Sayonora-Bona race, and always
maintained that he could have won with either boat
as they were sailed. Some of the sails on the Alice
would make a crew of a Port Jackson 14-footer
wonder. Her main boom was 70 feet and the sail
set like a mainsail on an America Cup boat, as did
all her other sails. Steve had a big balloon jib
made out of duck ; it was 182 feet on the four-stay,
and when it was set. it used to fairly lift the boat
along. As it sheeted home on the same bits as the
main sheet it was a beauty to handle when it carried
away, as it sometimes did. To see about 70 'or 80
luggers atid half a dozen schooners beating out a
narrow passage in the early morning was a sight
for a yachtsman that I suppose will never be seen
again in any part of 'the world. The cruel and hard
part is that the fine men. who were men in every
sense of the word, are being replaced by the yellow,
brown and brindle mixture now at the game.

The Directors submit herewith for the information of Shareholders the Balance-sheet of the Bank as
on 26th March, 1917, also Statements of Profit and Loss Accounts and Reserve Fund on the same date.

last .. ....

and to carry forward..
Since the last Report the Karoonda Agency has been converted into a Branch, and an Agency has
been opened at Wanbi. The Agencies at biigh, Muljarra, Mypolonga, Port Rickaby and Wynarka have
been closed. .
The General Annual Meeting will be held in the Board-room at the Head Office of the Bank, Ring
William-street, Adelaide, on Wednesday, 2nd May, 1917, at 12 noon, at which the Shareholders will
be called upon to elect two Directors in place of Arthur Waterhouse, Esq., and George Brookman, Esq.,
J.P., who retire by rotation, and, being eligible, offer themselves for re-election.
Shareholders will have to elect two Auditors for the ensuing year, and to fix the remuneration
of the retiring Auditors. s

“Xela”: A strange craft of unusual in-
terest is the Messenger, a little cutter which
anchored in the lagoon at Papeete recently.
Pitcairn Island, of Bounty mutiny fame, is
without any regular means of communica-
tion with civilisation, which in this instance
is represented by Papeete, 1100 miles distant
from Pitcairn. A sailing-boat in need of
water occasionally visits the island, but the
inhabitants appear not to be satisfied with
that. Wherefore about 18 months ago the
entire population set to work on the building
of the Messenger. It took 15 months to com-
plete the weird-loooking ark, and no such
absorbing interest has ever been taken in
the maiden voyage of any Clyde-built liner
as when the pioneer set out for Papeete in
February. It took her three weeks to reach
the Tahitian town, and, sad to say, her sea-
going qualities are not looked upon with
enthusiasm by the simple Pitcairners by
whom she was manned. She is anchored in
the lagoon at Papeete, and when the last
trans-Pacific liner left that port the old crew
didn’t seem particularly anxious to again
do that 1100 miles to Pitcairn.
“Lahma”: After a year in the wilderness
the three troopers were swinging the axe at
Shepherd’s when they suddenly came face to
face with a beautiful vision. His costume
included high-heeled silver-buckled' shoes
with large bowß, silk stockings, white
breeches and an elegant-flowered waistcoat
covered by a flowing martial tunic smothered
with braid. The exhilarated musketeers re-
covering their balance, bowed and shook
hands. The vision was delighted and posed
smilingly for a snapshot, while a British
staff-officer up stage went black in the face.
Some hours later the joyous soldiey-men saw
a huge crowd being addressed by their decor-
ative friend. He was Venizelos’s No. 3
general!
Jack Sommers: Billjim certainly lacks
reverence. Saw a group of three of him
arguing excitedly on a wharf. A few paces
away a general and some of his staff chatted.
Presently one of the disputants detached
himself, hitched on to his toes, took a
glance at the big chief’s shoulder-straps, and
yelled cheerfully to his cobbers, with a jerk
of thumb over his shoulder: “There y’are, I
told yer ’e was a —- General!” Bets duly
paid.
The neutrality of Germany’s nearest
neighbor is pathetic in its one-sidedness. A
Dutch newspaper may say pretty well what
it likes about the British-, but the editor
who wrote—“ln Central Europe there is a
group of rascals without conscience who
courted this war,” was sent to gaol for
three months. Holland lives in daily fear
of the Hun Government and takes its instruc-
tions from Berlin as to what to do with
editors; but let Germany be beaten, let the
nose of the bully nation be rubbed in the
dust, and then will all the little neutrals
join hands and dance around their natural
enemy. The world is not half conscious of
its hatred for Hunland. Ninety per cent-
of the truth about German character re-
mains to be told after the war. A million
or two prisoners have to bring their con-
tributions to the sum of information re the
effects of a century of uninterrupted mili-
tarism on the Prussian soul.
Ramchaud Kashinath Dattarya’s solemn
warning:—*
My purposes for writing on you this is to

Two or three things happen at Victory Garden to-

give their sits to womans. This happen two or
three times to-morrow yesterday and I question you
why ? I again tell you whv ? Mans and womans
are similar in this world and then why mans honors
womans? If they honors old, old womans, one
thing, but they honors young lady. My purpose
to write this is to enform the English Sahib that
when they do this they spoil their feminine lady
and they this lady get proud and walk like peacock
and then ask vote and then spoil Ken Garden and
throw bomb on Loid Gorg put bursting powder in
envelope and post and create other mischief. There-
fore I say to my English brothers, please dont spoil
English womans because by honoring them you
people put in them the sids of Sufragetism and then
they will get wild like Misses Pancurs.
The son of a well-known singer who died
lately at a ripe old age has lately been work-
ing in one of the Government Departments.
He had seen active service himself, and
when given some soldiers’ pay-books to col-
lect certain details from he found that the
leaves of many were glued together with the
blood of their owners. Then he looked round
the room and saw so many able-bodied
clerks fit for service that he felt he couldn’t
work there any longer, and resigned. He is
now trying to return to the Front or get
work amongst men who have done their bit.
HIS GREAT PREOCCUPATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She sat there at the ingle side.
And stitched a patient gait;
He sank within the glare, and eyed
The embers in the grate.
And as he looked it seemed a blind
Preoccupation grew.
That she was absent from his mind
Instinctively she knew.
She ceased to sew, and watched him there
With jealous eyes, and he
Sank deeper in his absent air.
What might his musings be?
There were those other ladies fair
He’d loved in summers sped.
Was’t she with heaped Autumnal hair
To whom his dreaming led ?
There was another, dark and small,
Whose tantalising smile
Once mocked her from the Study wall.
Whose eyes of cunning guile
Had fleered upon the lawful wife,
’Twas she who led him out
Beyond the meek and measured life
To some old, shameless rout.
She clenched her little hands in hate.
She felt that she must scream.
His eyes were still upon the grate.
He shook away the dream.
Said he: “If I don’t shift it, Ruth.
'Twill have me dippy soon.
I’ve had a bristle in this tooth
The whole dashed afternoon!"
VICTORIA.

FRANK GRAHAM.
JNFORMATION is required as to the
whereabouts of FRANK GRAHAM, for-
merly of Ambleside, Westmorland, who
emigrated to Australia about 28 years ago.
The last letter received from him by his
mother was written from Brisbane, and he
then stated he was about to go up coun-
try in charge of cattle. He has not since
been heard of. If he wishes to benefit under
his Mother’s Will he must send in a claim to
us before the 6th March, 1918. Anyone who
can give any information is requested to
communicate with GEORGE GATEY &
SON, Solicitors, Ambleside, Westmorland,
England.

Weakness
Every movement of the
body uses up a definite
amount of vitality. That
is why you feel tired at
the end of the day. When

BEAUTY SECRETS
REVEALEP.
By “MIMOSA ”
HAIR CULTURE.
DISTRESSING lack of hair will mar
the most beautiful face, and after all
* v there is really no need to have poor or
impoverished hair if a few commonsense
rules are observed. First of all, hair tonics
must be freshly-made in order to obtain the
best results, and thousands of women, and
men, too, are now making up their own
lotions with most satisfactory results. For
this purpose they obtain from the chemist’s
one ounce of boranium and mix this with
pint of bay rum, quite a simple formula,
and according to all accounts remarkably
effective. This simple home-made lotion
quickly removes all dandruff, and creates a
growth of new, healthy hair which will
gladden the heart of any woman. One word
in conclusion. Do not experiment with
your hair, long suffering though it may bo.
Nature will surely revolt if you continue
to subject the hair and scalp to all kinds
of fanciful treatments. Employ a tonic by-
all means if such is required, but having
found a satisfactory one use no other.
GOOD SHAMPOO IMPORTANT,
THE hair should be allowed to breathe,
and the greasy film around each strand
must be removed with a mild non-
alkaline shampoo. Soaps should be tabooed.
The very best solution for the purpose can
be made by dissolving a teaspoonful of stal-
lax granules in a cup of hot water. It
stimulates the scalp to healthy action, and
at the same time leaves the hair in that
soft, fluffy condition so much admired. Any
chemist can supply you with an original
packet of stallax, sufficient to make twenty-
five or thirty shampoos.
MAGIC FOR SUPERFLUOUS HAIR.
IT is astounding the number of women
who suffer from unsightly growths, of
hair on the face, and it will come as a
piece of good news to know that there is a
simple substance, known as powdered phe-
minol, which will remove it immediately
and permanently. Mix a small quantity
into a thin paste with a little water, and
apply to the objectional growths. In two
minutes all trace of the hair will have
entirely vanished, and your skin will be as
soft and smooth, as a child’s.
RENEWING COMPLEXIONS.
THE average woman of to-day is usually
satisfied if her complexion is passably
pretty, little knowing that an exquisite
skin is now within her reach, providing she
has the good sense to stop using cheap made-
up preparations and adopt what has now
become known as the mercolised-wax habit.
It is a pity that every woman in the land
cannot be apprised of this simple method.
The few to whom its virtues are already
known have complexions that are the envy
of all their friends, and, as is usually the)
case, the majority of them refuse to divulge
the secret. However, the formula has now
been verified, and is known as mercolised
wax. Inquiry at several chemists’ shops
have resulted in ascertaining that this sub-
stance has been known to the select few for
some years as a complexion boautifier, but it
is only until quite recently that it has been
used to any extent for this purpose. It was
also explained how mercolised wax renews
the complexion, but as this is rather tech-
nical it is not proposed to repeat it here. It
will be Sufficient to say that through some
peculiar action the wax absorbs the dead
and discolored outer cuticle, revealing the
fresh young complexion which every woman
has underneath. The process occupies about
ten days, and the change is so gradual and
imperceptible that no discomfort whatever
is caused. The treatment is perfectly harm-
less and the wax may be used as often as
desired.
BLACKHEADS FLY AWAY.
A PRACTICALLY instantaneous remedy
for blackheads, greasy skins and
enlarged pores, recently discovered, is
now coming into general use in the boudoir.
It is very simple, harmless and pleasant.
Drop a stymol tablet, obtained at the
chemist’s, in a tumbler full of hot water.
After the effervescence has subsided bathe
the face in the liquid, using a small sponge
or soft cloth. In a few minutes dry the
face and the offensive blackheads will come
right off on the towel. Also the large oily
pores immediately close up and efface them-
selves naturally. The greasiness disappears
and the skin is left smooth, soft and cool.
This simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days to
ensure the permanence of the result. [i

The Inky Way.
“Ghoul” : In the old days, when we were
young and morbid, some of us wanted to
write morgue verse, but J F. Archibald, then
in The Bulletin’s chair, gave his fiat, that
that work was the sacred preserve of Victor
Daley. 1 had every qualification for it my-
self except the poetic. My daily gruesome
duty was to get the key of .he Melbourne
Morgue from the Princes’ Bridge constable,
and see if there were any cold customers.. On
a summer dog-day 1 might find the place open.
There would be the Coroner’s fat, jolly police-
man Hoey, in shirt sleeves, with cutty pipe
in jaw, hoseing with copious sluices an array
of ladies and gents on the slabs. Hoey
would relieve my melancholy with a hearty
laugh, and the remark “Business brisk!” I
remember once seeing the body of an infant.
Said Hoey: “Look at this one! Finest
child you ever saw, and yet the poor girl
that brought it into the world had to throw
it into the river to keep ‘respectable.’ ” Have
often wondered since how many companies
of Anzacs have gone into the Yari’a.
* * * *
Second-Lieut. Charles D. Scott, a Banana-
land inkywa.vfarer, has received the Military
Cross as evidence of his bravery in action.
When the war started he was one of the first
of the craft in Brisbane to scrap the pen
for the sword. At that time he w r as on
the Courier ; previously he was a member of
the Queensland Times staff. From Gal-
lipoli he was invalided to England, thence,
on recovery, to France.
* * * *
“S.T.S.”: The modern habit of long life
comes hardest on humorists. If Frank
Burnand had only died at 55 or 60, like so
many of his Bohemian predecessors, his fame
would have been greater. He was a fine
example of the punning humor of the ’7o’s
and ’SO’s, but with later developments he
was quite out of touch. His “Happy
Thoughts”-—the earlier volumes, before the
vein got worked out —are worth digging up
even now, as an almost uncanny study of
the psychology of a shy man. Then came
along the clever and original crowd of young
writers in the ’9o’s, and his style, now stereo-
typed, was not in demand. He still seemed
a fixture as editor of PuncVi ; but at last
he had to be pushed out, as.kindly as might
be, but quite firmly, in favor of Owen
.Seaman.
“Cyanide”: When the much-boomed Ash-
mead Bartlett was being entertained by Mel-
bourne journalists, artists and .X. N. (etc.)
Tait, a complaint was made by war-corres-
pondent Chas. P. Smith that the daily jour-
nalist got no credit for his most brilliant
police paragraphs and his most dashing foot-
ball reports. But when election time comes,
he might have added, we get our chance.
For example, witness the Argus of May 2:

The Sydney estate of John Norton paid
in probate duty £7113 16s. lid. The N. S.
Wales estate was valued at £69,512.
* * * *
“Muskctoon”: Jack Mclntyre, editor and
proprietor of the Peak Hill Express, near
Forbes (N.S.W.), struck bad health lately,
and a committee of townspeople ordered him
to take a holiday by the sea, and promised
to keep the wolf from the office door while
he was away. In addition they gave the
sick man a wad of about £lOO to help make
his spell interesting. Mclntyre* who is not
a young man by any means, had tried sev-
eral times to go to the war, but the nearest
he got to the firing line was Dubbo camp,
where the doctor passed him out.
“S.T.S.” : In “The Boad to Hogan’s Gap,”
in “Banjo” Paterson’s new book of poems,
an out-back Australian tells a stranger that
if once you get off that track “You haven’t
got the whitest hope of ever getting back.”
Clearly this is intended to mean “haven’t
got the slightest hope.” It’s an unusual
misprint.
* * * *
“Ferro”: I don’t regard the literal sins
of proof-scanners as fair game for comment
but would respectfully beg the Melb. Argus
deskman to explain this statement regarding
the claims of the Australian fighters—one
killed, the other wounded on April 19:
“Both officers were the youngest sons of the
late Mr. George Throssell, C.M.G., at one
time Premier of Western Australia.”
A SONG OF THE PEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The poet's wife has need of gloves,
Or Cossack boots she’d wear;
Forthwith a jaded pen he shoves
To write for her a pair.
The fancies of his teeming brain
Are changed to food and dress; .
A ballad means a peek of peas,
A roundelay a pound of c-heese,
A sonnet buys some cress.
The poet’s children must be fed.
It is a sad mistake
For czars of lofty rhyme to wed:
How their poor brains must ache
As gentle songs of love they write,
Which have to rhyme and scan,
Well knowing they do but indite
Addresses to their goddess bright,
To pay the baker -man.
The poet must his thirst appease
(The hardest task he’s got!)
The wheels of prosody to grease
And stop them running hot.
From rhymes and metres never free,
lie labors in his den;
And, while he makes his songs of glee,
The meaning learns of pen-u-ry-
The wages of the pen!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
