In the Ashes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
I FOUND the cold grey ashes of a fire
Which these two lit, whom Vengeance
followed fast,
Although the dragging lawyer-vines were
cast
To stay the following foosteps—Hate’s de-
sire,
Breaking all barriers that the scrub set up,
Thirsty to fill with blood its brazen cup.
I found the ashes that such memories keep.
Tall ironbarks were round them, scored of
trunk;
And here and there a wan bush flower,
drunk
With sun and dew, and falling into sleep,
Yet murmuring nothing of the vows it heard,
Though its pale heart was redder by a word.
And overhead a bronze-wing in the boughs
1 tippled swift pinions, and a pink galah
Strutted in seeding grass, yet kept afar
From that grey ring that wed to Life’s
carouse
Pursuing Death. The blue smoke o’er the
trees
Betrays no more the rendezvous of these.
With stirless leaves the ironbarks look down,
Yet they must know that never human
tongue
Can tell of how those lovers kissed and clung,
And how grey eyes struck flame from eyes
of brown.
At least they did not live to see Love pass
Into a sear of ashes on the grass.
Beneath her hair they told me, when she lay
Ready for burial, in the small bush inn,
There was one bullet mark to pay her sin.
Her small white hands were folded. Did she
pray?
After her death? (In life not much, I vow !)
Pray to the God who would not hear her
now!
But he died harder. When I saw her there
I understood how„ he would fight for life,
Although he had no weapon but a knife
With which to parry bullets. She was fair,
And Death was not an easy thing to choose
When there was life —and life with her—to
lose!
But they were very quiet when they slept
On those rough trestles. So wo laid them
down
Under the weeping myalls. Then to town
One for the sergeant went. But I —l kept
Pact with the promptings of a strange desire
And rode to find their little burned-out fire.
There was a wattle blooming at the edge
Of that thick timber, and it spilled its gold
Before my horse’s hoofs as though it told
Of golden reeds that rustle through. Life’s
sedge,
Making papyrus over which to write
Record of hours that were all too bright.
For mortals living. Death had given them
these
Ere for himself the price he claimed. I know
There was some special glory in the glow
Of that small camp-fire shining through the
trees
And that, ere each crisp twig on it they set.
Often across its warmth their hands had
met.
T left my horse, and idly, in the cold
Of that dull pyre, with gum-switch stirred.
It was no sob of shattered hopes I heard
(Dead leaf and chip that once were fairy
gold!),
No hieroglyph of graves in cinders spelled—
One quick, sweet laugh was all the ashes
hold !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Harbor Lights.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SMITII discovered that he was in love
with Jones’s wife, but he couldn’t find
an opportunity to tell her so.
Not that Jones suspected and deliberately
frustrated his intentions, but being an
obtuse, egotistical individual, he took it for
granted that Smith’s frequent visits were
due to that gentleman’s fond desire to bask
in the light of superior knowledge and in-
telligence. Therefore, when Smith called of
an evening, hoping to find the baldheaded
Jones taking a night off, and his saintly and
wife all alone he was always dis-
appointed. There sat Jones, in slippers and
smoking-cap (both decorated by the fair
hand of his wife), holding forth upon ques-
tions of the day and laying down the law
entirely to his own satisfaction. It says
much for the depth and sincerity of Smith’s
budding love that he could sit through these
tirades without doing personal injury to the
tirader. But Love has ever bestowed
strength and endurance upon his victims;
and Smith was able to .keep a calm exterior
and appear to listen attentively, the while
he made expressive eyes at Mrs. Jones. This
method of telling a hidden and devouring
affection is always unsatisfactory; and is
liable to be mistaken for the outward and
visible sign of inward physical discomfort.
Smith’s training and environment had
made him cunning and dulled that code of
honor we are all supposed to carry about
with us. Ife was in trade —the hardware
trade : —and from tender years had learnt the
gentle art of deception in all its branches. It
is not to be wondered then that commercial
morality and social honor should, in times
of stress, become somewhat jumbled in his
mind, and in seeking a means to smooth the
way for his declaration to Mrs. Jones his
methods savored more of the business than
the social code.
If you are in the hardware line it is an
accomplishment to be able to produce, from
a cracked or otherwise damaged article, the
virgin ring that proclaims it to the buyer as
a worthy purchase. It is, I say, an accom-
plishment : and in trade is not only coun-
tenanced but commended. But should you
treat your next-door neighbor or dearest
friend in a like manner —well, you would
soon know in what light your conduct was
regarded. When I say Smith’s method of
procedure fell short of the social code, I
have very plainly told you he didn’t play
fair. He failed tu discern that what you
do from eight o’clock till six is a very differ-
ent matter from what you do from six till
eight.
Smith didn't go to Jones and in
a straightforward manner tell him the
state (2 his affections and desire to
be allowed to speak with Mrs. Jones. He
hadn’t even the decency to send a fictitious
message that would get Jones out of the way
for an hour or so comfortably and without
causing him any inconvenience. His train-
ing wouldn’t let him. He must do the smart
thing, in business and out of it.
This is what Smith did. A friend of his
was part-owner of a motor-boat; and he
begged the loan of it for a few hours one
evening. Then he bragged to Jones about
his boat, pretending that he also was a part-
owner, and offered to take Jones for a run
round the harbor when he had a night to
spare. Of course, Jones had every night to
spare, so they arranged for the one follow-
ing. Smith had often been out with the
owners of the boat and knew how to manage
it. He took Jones all over the place. They
started at seven, and at about eleven were in
the vicinity of the Heads. Jones began to
feel the lateness of the hour and suggested
that it was time to go home. But Smith
seemed to be in an excursive frame of mind,
and insisted that before turning home they
must examine, at close quarters, a red light
that showed intermittently just inside the
Heads. Although Jones demurred, Smith put
her head for the light, and was soon along-
side. Then he begged Jones to hang on to
iron uprights that supported the light and
steady the boat while he looked to her
engine, which, he declared, was going wrong.
Jones clutched the floating buoy with a
strong grip, but the boat seemed to persist-
ently refuse to come alongside and stay
there. Smith bellowed to him to hold hard ;
and as he did so he felt the boat gradually
receding until only liis feet remained over
her side. Then the engine sparked up, and
he fell with a plop into the water, only his
mighty grip on the buoy saving him from a
'watery grave. He yelled frantically as he
slowly drew himself on to the buoy, and
shuddered at the thought of the death that
had so nearly been his. But Smith and the
boat was nowhere to be seen; and faintly
over the water came the chug, chug of the
motor as she went swiftly on her way. He
was deserted! Marooned on a bobbing buoy
in a waste of water, miles from home, and
out of the way of any hope of rescue till
daylight.
What did Smith mean? Was this some
beastly joke? Why did he want him out of
the way? Had he anything Smith wanted?
Smith knew he hadn’t any money. This was
Thursday, and he wouldn’t be paid till to-
morrow. What did it mean? For long he
sat huddled on his slippery perch and pon-
dered wretchedly, till the ceaseless rolling
of the buoy caused him to feel intensely un-
comfortable. With an effort he parted with
his carefully-prepared and thoroughly-enjoyed
dinner, and sank exhausted, still clutching,
in frenzied embrace, the ironwork of the
buoy.
All through that long night Jones crouched
on his insecure refuge, shivering and ex-
hausted. He wondered how long it would
take him to perish of starvation, and just
how long a man could live between drinks.
To die of thirst in a waste of water and
within sight of land —could fate play a man
a more scurvy trick? Even and anon he
dozed fitfully, to wake with a start as he
felt his grasp on the buoy slackening and his
body steadily slipping into the dark abyss
below. In his efforts to keep awake he cast
his eyes upon the blinking .light and endea-
vored to keep count of the intervals that
succeeded each show of the illumination ; but
as the light bobbed with irritating regularity
he gave it up in disgust.
Even a long night on a bobbing buoy must
come to an end ; and the first streak of grey .
dawn discovered the worn-out Jones keeping
his unstable vigil. While daylight was still
in the throes of birth and earth and sky
seemed one misty pall, Jones could make out
an indistinct mass coming in his direction
and hope beat high in his heart. As the
mass approached by the inner channel he
could discern that it was a collier, appar-
ently bound for the north, and his heart sank
like lead. Of what use to be rescued if
hours, perhaps an entire day, must elapse
before he could return home and investigate
the depths of Smith’s perfidy! No, he must
let the ship pass by: and he painfully
erawled to the other side of the buoy in case
some inquisitive member of the crew should
espy him and effect a rescue perforce.
The hours crawled slowly by, but no sun
came to instil warmth into his chilled
bones. Instead, a persistent drizzle added
to his sickly misery, and Jones came near
to blasphemy as his cramped position began
to tell a prickly tale.
It must have been five o’clock before his
solitude was again broken—this time by a
tug drawing a punt loaded with slime. The
tug passed without noticing his frantic
efforts to attract attention; but the man on
the punt sighted him and manoeuvred so as
to bring his clumsy barque within hailing
distance of the buoy. An almost unintelligi-
ble conversation then ensued, the gist of
which was that Jones couldn’t swim and
that Smith was up to some underhand work,
for which he’d make him pay dearly when he
got ashore. The man on the punt could
make little sense of this; but as Jones
couldn’t swim he must hang on till the tug
was returning, when he would be picked up.
He did ; and about an hour afterwards his
rescue was effected. He could give no
satisfactory account of himself. He hadn’t
been wrecked, his boat hadn’t broken down :
in fact, he had no reason at all to give for
his presence on the buoy in direct contraven-
tion of the notice placed thereon, that all
trespassers would be prosecuted. The cap-
tain of the tug was sorry—he said so—but it
was his duty to give Jones in charge as soon
as they reached land. Which he did.
As it was Jones’s first offence, he hoped
for a fine, and prayed for a speedy appear-

ante before the magistrate. This was granted
.iccause the court happened to be sitting and
business slack; and at one o’clock Jones left
the court under oath to return within
twenty-four hours with two pounds.
Aow for his vengeance; but first to dis-
cover why Smith had wished him out of the
way. As his appearance was far from neat
and lus clothes were still wet, he decided his
first step must be home. Besides, his wife
might possibly be anxious, unless Smith had
called On liis return last night with some
lying message.
He arrived at his small villa and was
surprised to find the blinds drawn, the morn-
ing paper lying on the verandah, and the
milk-jug standing on the gas-box. The
general appearance of dust and disorder verv
clearly indicated, that no housewifely hand
lmd that morning performed the usual
duties. Jones was frankly puzzled. Was
lus wife was so overcome by his absence as
to be incapable of rising? lie must investi-
gate.
His key admitted him ; and an exploration
of the lower portions of the house further
disclosed the stoppage of the domestic
machinery. No breakfast had been prepared,
and the rooms were practically the same as
when he left the night before. His half-
smoked pipe lay upon the dining-room table,
his slippers were where he kicked them when
preparing to go out with Smith. Everything
seemed to breathe mystery. With a heavy
and apprehensive heart he ascended the
stairs, going straight for the conjugal bed-
room, where he felt sure he must find the
key, to the mystery.
The room was in utter confusion. Clo.tlies
were strewn everywhere, empty boxes lay on
the bed, under it, and in every available
space. The place looked as though it had
been ransacked by thieves. With this sus-
picion in liis mind, Jones stepped cautiously
round the bed, lialf-expectiug to find his
wife’s murdered body stretched beside it.
Instead he found, placed prominently on the
dressing-table, a letter addresed to himself.
With trembling hand Jones seized the
letter and staggered to the bed for support.
What horror might not be contained in that
innocent white envelope ! His brain refused
to conjecture, so he made what haste his
perturbation would allow in opening it. This
is what lie read :
It will be useless for you to follow me or attempt
to bring me back. I have fled with Henry, who will
keep me in better style than I have been accustomed
to and surround me with a purer love than you are
capable of. You are too middle aged and prosaic
to satisfy the longings of a romantic heart like
mine. Henry fulfils all I ask of a lover. He is
the prototype of the knights of old. Did he not
dare the perils of the deep for my sake? I hope
you will not be ill after the exposure of last night
and that you will change your clothes immediately
upon getting home. Do not shoot yourself; it would
bo useless.
Jones sat for some time with the letter in
his hand. He appeared to be stunned ; and a
casual observer might have suspected him of
planning a terrible revenge in which 'a revol-
ver played the star part. His sudden return
to animation and the gratified—almost
greedy—look that lit up his countenance,
quickly dispelled any such idea.
“Two hundred pounds!” he muttered
slowly; then he repeated the words over and
over again, each repetition gaining in volume
until it sounded like a paean of delight.
Hastening to the bureau which contained
his personal belongings, he unlocked the top-
most drawer and brought forth some papers
which he triumphantly waved aloft. Then,
without even waiting to obey his wife’s last
behest, he hurried to town, presented him-
self breathlessly at the office of the “Wife-
Desertion Insurance and Guarantee Com-
pany,” and Avithout the least difficulty
secured the amount of his policy—two
hundred pounds.
PEETA.
System.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
THE military train which feeds this part
of the firing-line sneaks up during the
night, and Driver Billjim and Guard
Jimbill have an exciting and sulphurous time
shunting the trucks into positions convenient
for rapid delivery to the khaki-clad con-
> signees in the morning. Through the dark
hours are heard, mingled with the wailing,
of shells, the distressed pantings and puffings
' of the engine as the wheels whirl round in
■ frantic efforts to grip the mudslimed rails.
When the alleged daylight comes, platoons
of infantry descend out of flip mists upon
the loaded trucks. Goods are pitched out
P>H -mell and with no regard for the wishes
of the other consignees. Each party grabs
its own and as much more as it thinks a
claim might be bluffed to. The infantry,
being able to mobilise in greater numbers
than other units, are, of course, easy win-
ners. Being Artillery, I’m always at a
disadvantage, for few men can be spared
from the guns’ just now; and at that hour
of the morning I can rarely muster more
than an army of fifteen. However, Ave sail
in valiantly where Might is Right, and the
odds are great, and make a system as we go
along.
For instance, our battery had been getting
rather severely strafed of late; and there
was a sudden demand for sandbags, rails,
“bursters,” etc., to protect us from small
shells and fragments at least. An order was
sent down to “the shop,” and, although I
was advised that the whole lot had been
sent up, when it came to claiming them I
found that the bursters had been “lost.”
Next day I wandered aimlessly amongst a
stack of stuff belonging to the —th Aust.
Inf. Div. There I saw a small pile of
bursters. Forty seconds later my party had
advanced unobtrusively and mustered for the
attack. I spoke a few words to them and
the first man swung a burster up on his
shoulder and retired. But he didn’t go far.
From behind a stack of sandbags appeared
a clay-covered Australian youngster, who in
a casual but business-like way placed his
rifle and bayonet across the front of my
leading brigand’s body and effectively barred
the progress.
“Whoa! Teh can’t take them away, lad !”
Affecting my most ingratiating manner I
approached the pair of them.
“It’s all right, sentry. I’m heavy
artillery, and this is some of our stuff that
was unloaded here by mistake, and I’m just
getting it away.”
“I dunno anything about that,” said
young Jimbill, with a cheerful grin. "But
I was posted guard over this stuff, and no
one can take anything away without a signed
order from Sergeant Fisher.”
“Oh—Sergeant Fisher!” I smiled loftily.
“This is my stuff and”
“Can’t ’elp it,” he grinned. “If Birdie
’imself was t’ come along he wouldn’t take
it while I'm here.”
Now, it isn’t the usual thing to hear such
heroic sentiments from the Australian, and
this youngster was evidently merely enjoying
himself.
“Come oh, come on, don’t act the goat,” I
said persuasively.
“Can't ’elp it,” he answered. “That’s what
I’m here for.”
The humorous possibility of internal
conflict in the British forces flashed through
my mind ; but as my artillerymen were not
armed I dismissed the idea and called up
the railway sergeant who was passing.
(Also he was a friend of mine.)
“Now, these bursters are artillery stores,
as anyone can see.” I began, “and I’m sure
you haven’t got the Division signature
for their receipt, have you?”
The railway sergeant produced a rain-
sodden, mud-smeared mass of paper from his
overcoat pocket and made a pretence of
looking through it.
“No, they haven’t signed for it yet,” he
answered (truthfully: he was rarely able
to get anyone’s signature for anything).
“But they’re artillery stuff all right, an’ you
can take ’em.’.’
Whereupon I called up my forces; and the
sentry promptly waggled his bayonet sug-
gestively and shooed them off again.
The railway sergeant looked at him with
a weary smile.
“Gawblimy. lad, you’ve got nothing t’ do
with this stuff,” he drawled. “It hasn’t been
signed over to your crowd yet. It belongs
to th’ railway still. It’s mine.”
But still the youngster wasn’t taking any ;
and seeing the bluff had failed, the railway
sergeant said I’d better fix it up with Ser-
geant Fisher ; and washing his hands of the
affair he slouched off in the slush.
Now, possibly the stuff in dispute did
belong to the —th Division. But I’d lost
much at their hands in the past. Our own
people required those bursters urgently; so
I decided to fall back on System.
I found Sergeant Fisher, a lieutenant and
a lance-Jack seated in a dug-out before a
(stolen) coal fire. I wished them the usual
ironical compliments of the season, and was
invited to sit on a cartridge-box near the
fire.
“By-the-way, do you know if that pile of
bursters I saw down, amongst your stuff whs
meant for us or for you?” I began with
engaging frankness.
All were instantly on their guard.
“Oh, they’re ours all right,” said the
lance-Jack promptly. “They came up on
that mixed truck two days ago.”
A tense pause.
“Well, I was only wondering,” I con-
tinued casually, “because I know we had
something on ' that truck—and to-day I
received direct advice /rom the ‘shop’ that
they’d been sent up to me. Our people want
them badly just now.” (This was all quite
true —except the alleged direct advice from
the “shop.”)
The sergeant drew a cigarette from behind
lu’s ear and tapped it reflectively on the back
of his hand ; the lance-Jack produced a damp,
dog-eared notebook from inside his tunic
and commenced to look through it just as
though he expected to find something. Then,
with a preliminary caustic comment on the
weather (spoken in the vernacular), I took
advantage of the delay to get to real busi-
ness.
“By-the-way, I’ve got some lengths of
trench-pump hose that' I might be able to
let you have.”
The sergeant looked up keenly.
“We could do with ’em, sarge,” he said.
“You see all our pumps just outside there—
without a yard of hose amongst the lot of
’em!” Then lie silently lit his cigarette.
“Well,” he began, in a quick, business-like
way, “how much hose can you spare?”
“Hummm!” I reflected impressively, “let
me see. Well, we finished issuing pumps a
fortnight ago, and there’s fully fifteen lengths
more than our share been wrongfully con-
signed to us since. If they’ll be of use to
you you’d better have ’em.”
The Infantry accepted the offer promptly.
“Righto, sarge; I’ll send a party over with
the bursters, an’ the men can bring the hose
back when they come—that right?”
It was; and I turned and departed in a
chorus of genial farewells.
A minute later I met a Tommy member
of my mixed party (Heavy Artillery in this
part is Aus.-cam-Imperial) hurrying towards
me from a stack of our goods. In the
distance an Australian was - ploughing off
through the mud with a nice, burnable piece
of timber over his shoulder. I greeted my
Tommy rather angrily.
“Good God! Didn’t I just post you on
guard over that stuff?” I began.
“Oi did, Sawgint, but”
“Then why the didn’t you stop that
chap from pinching our timber?”
_,“Oi —Oi did, sawgint!” replied Gunner
Bloggins almost tearfully. “Oi did, but ’e
towld Oi to be [bad word]., Sawgint.”
The Australian members of my composite
party broke into broad grins. So did an
A.M.C. Major who had become interested in
the dialogue. I was tickled myself, but
silently and for the second time that day
cursed Sergeant Fisher. I also vowed to
speak to him seriously on the predatory
habits of his energetic men.
But Sergeant Fisher is a popular acting-
quartermaster, and great is the motive power
of a liberal rum-issue to men living under
such conditions as ours. This I found out
for. myself that night as I sat bursting with
sociability in the warmth of my rival’s dug-
out. sipping at the fragrant steaming potion
he handed me. while the rain tsipp-tsipped
outside, and the shells from some absurdly
energetic battery wailed protestingly over-
head.
